THE RED PAGE.
To Her.
Here between thy loving arms
Lies the kingdom of my heart, love 1
Wrapped about in fairy charms,
Here between thy loving arms,
Shielded safe from earthly harms,
Never, never shall we part, love 1
Here between thy loving arms
Lies the kingdom of my heart, love !
Q.
MACHETE.
The Local Muse .
Judged by her latest appearances, the local
Muse wants a tonic. She is anaemic, sallow, lack-
lustre : her pulse is feeble and her gait drags. The
sooner she is packed off to the mountains, with
instructions to some decent sanatorium-keeper to
give her three good meals a day, sunshiny walks,
and a pint of claret with her dinner, the better it
will be for her product. She has been “ letting
herself run down.”
*■'•••
Somebody met W. Shakespeare in Spiritland
the other night, and tendered the usual felici-
tations. The bard, who is as jocund, and
plethoric, and ruddy as of yore, and
has slightly accentuated the curve of his
long waistcoat, took tribute not unamiably, but
deprecated praise of his personal exploits. Said
the visitor: “ I have always been particularly
struck by the intellectual force necessary to create
Antony’s speech.” “Pardon me,” said Shake-
speare, “ but I did not write that speech.” “ Did
not write ‘ Friends, Romans, countrymen !’ ”
“No,” replied the dramatist with a smile, “that
was written by a magnificent haunch of venison
of my acquaintance, with the assistance of twin
quarts of unusually fragrant Alicant.” “ Indeed !
but at least, sir, the airy extravagance of
1 The Tempest ’ sprang from no such gross diet
—that dainty fragment of Ariel’s ” “ Was
born from the fumes of a pottle of sack I
had at Shrewsbury,” interrupted the poet, medi-
tatively caressing his paunch. “What! your
songs, your sonnets- —?” “As to the songs, I
cannot be certain ; but the sonnets are distinctly
not mine. They were the work of some very fine
old ale on which I got drunk every night at an
Eastcheap tavern during my second London
season, after gorging myself from a splendid sir-
loin. I invariably woke up warm and poetical
—rbeshrew me ! the knave has run for it! ”
* * * •
This interview is but one of a thousand testi-
monies that literature rests upon a beefsteak
basis, and no poetry ever came out of a pump.
For energetic intellectual achievement you must
have a brain swollen with rich blood, and you
can’t make rich blood out of pap and cabbages.
The upholders of the Cabbage theory of existence
invariably argue to Duration, and point out how
nice it is to be a noodle and live to ninety. But
the need of literature is Intensity—we want
thrilling lines, not dull volumes. Or they argue
to Size, and point out what fine large animals are
the elephant, and the horse, and the cow—who
live on grain and grass and clothes-line. They
ave large—and so tame ! Unluckily for the Cab-
bage-men, we want wildness, not tameness in
literature—the lion’s roar, the tiger’s spring, not
the bleat and waddle of the sheep. Literature
cries for concentration, and to get concentrated
mental force you must have concentrated physical
pabulum.
• • * •
It looks as if the local Muse had been browsing
off turnip-tops lately, with occasional reference to
a stick of asparagus. She is so flat, and stale,
and unprofitable. Per medium of E. L. Scharkie
she issues “Fragments, Poems ” whioh show that
ohe poor lady has grown too weak to think—she
is light - headed, and babbles fine - language
incoherently. The words look as if they might
mean something; but, O Muse, what is it? E.g.,
page 28 :
SONNET,
“If I could fly ” ; but says the sage you’ve got
No wings so there’s an end of it. In sooth,
The Socialist, chiselling with wilful thought,
Would build a better pyramid of Truth,
Composite without atom, and in his dreams
Would fly like I would, but he has no wings
So there’s an end of it. Doctrine, false-lipped*
Vesting all life in instant good, empowers
No hideous wrong desoend, no good evolve, —
Heights, whioh nor form nor method can dissolve
But by blood on the soul, as drop, gaunt cliffs,
Piecemeal, to ever-sapping waves, and towers
Rear up to aggregate completing themes,
The making, and the breaking laws of things.
This form of the sonnet, by the way, does not ap-
pear to have been known to the Italian masters,
but the bold uncertainty of its rhymes needs no
defence. Only—the purport ? “By blood on
the soul ” is obviously a new and handy expletive;
but what is “ empowers no hideous wrong des-
cend ” ? why cannot “ form nor method dissolve ”?
and why do “towers rear up to aggregate com-
pleting themes’’? A sheet of foolscap will be
presented to the solver.
* * * *
E. L. Seharkie seems to have made a long list of
“poetical” words and phrases like “twilight,”
“amber,” “empanoplied,” “dappling,” and so
forth, and deliberately set sitnself to alliterate
them in lines not too particular about their feet.
This is a fairer sample of sis labor :
Sunset pillowing longing in its anus,
Twilight tingling clown the slopes of time^
Peaceful waters waving out their charms,
Anther ripples of eternal rhyme,
Clear image of Eternity, sweet rest,
Down in the golden harbour of the west.
If the mind moves half-way to welcome them,
these lines might be called pretty : that is to say,
the disconnected images have a flavor which titil-
lates the fancy. They represent the extremity of
E. L. Scharkie’s art. Se threads ser words as a
child threads colored beads : sometimes the com-
bination harmonises, but ’tis only a string of
colored beads when all is done.
* * • •
The author of “Perdita, a Sonnet Record,”
modestly suppresses his name, but he is plainly
Tomlinson.
“This I have read in a book,” he said, “and that was
told to me,
And this I have thought that another man thought of a
Prince in Muscovy.”
There is not one impressive look-like-original
thought in his whole sheaf of thirty sonnets. Oc-
casionally there is fairly dexterous expression of
an attractive fancy, as in
CUPID AND PSYCHE.
Thus did the ancient artist-soul arrest
A perfect thought and model it for me,
A truth in stone, to face eternity.
Lips turned to lips they stand, and breast to breast;
The fingers of his right hand lightly rest
On her dear cheek, his left hand lovingly
Holding her head—so stand they, he and she—
Her arms about his naked body pressed.
These are the gods I worship, even these,
The body and soul of Nature ; girl and boy
For ever young ; I fall upon my knees
And worship them. Pure life without alloy 1
Spirit and flesh made one ; and so each frees
The other. Perfect Love and perfect Joy 1
This has some grace of conception, but Tomlinson
has not vigor enough to fill even 14 lines. He has
to eke his thought out with pad-phrases like “ he
and she,” “even these,” which do not advance
the idea one whit, and with forceless repetitions
like “ I fall upon my knees and worship them.”
The result is flat: there is no vitality, no dynamic
force in it. You muse at the climax: “Hm !
where did I see that before ? ” and pass on unin-
fluenced.
* * * *
And this is Tomlinson’s best. “Worship the
body and soul of Nature!” Nob ho; he never
comes within a long cooey of Nature. He wor-
ships the literary images of somebody else who
may possibly have worshipped Nature, and stews
them into his book at second or third or fourth or
fifth-hand. Here is his very first sonnet: —
LOVE, THE HUNTER.
The hart that wanders, where thick shade relieves
The summer heat, beneath the interlaced
Delight of flickering frondage, and hath paced
Anieh the hunter’s camp, too late perceives
His risk ; and, spent at last, his flanks he heaves
By springs whose waves are bitter to the taste.
But all his mournful passage may be traced
By ruddy splashes on the grass and leaves.
So marking well each sonnet may ye know
Which way my soul in travail flees, and where
It finds brief rest, and how it hears the flow
Of soft or thunderous waters, but must bear
The clinging pain for ever, though it creep
Out to the edges of the endless deep.
Isn’t it an abomination of bloodlessness? Where
did Tomlinson get that hart? Chaucer ? Spenser ?
Lord knows—but it isn’t his own. Why should
Australian writers ransack ancient pastorals for
weary tropes when they have their own new con-
tinent —original, bewildering, charming—right
under their foolish noses ? And Tomlinson wor-
ships the body and soul of Nature, quotha !
* * * *
So throughout his flat batch of rhymes—mere
echoes laboriously caught from the writings of
bards who looked out of their own eyes, and
thought their own thoughts, and invented their
own images for sedulous Tomlinsons to copy.
When an angel comes to visit Tomlinson
In her hand she bare
Two cups, one filled with Rhenish bright and rare—
Why not Chateau Wr.nghnu? And beneath
Perdita’s breast “ was burnt the muzzled bear”—
who danced in the villages of the Middle Ages.
Why not a possum? Certainly, Australian litera-
ture should not be fettered by the limits of
Australia : it should be universal, and it has a
right to draw its material from any source it
pleases. But a writer who observes and thinks
for himself in Australia cannot avoid giving his
work an Australian tinge, and when the Austra-
lian Tomlinsons come along with ideas and figures
culled, not from life, but from books, we know
they have not been observing and thinking for
themselves, and justly curse them for worthless
wordspinners.—Now take the local Muse away.
The American Short Story.
Somebody sends four pages of an American periodioa
America, containing an article by Maurice Thompson o
the American short story. In reinforces the opinion her
frequently expressed that the short story of the America
magazine is, in the cant literary phrase, “thin.” Th
fiction of Harper’s, and Scribner’s, and The Century
either a colourless photograph of commonplace scene
and people, or an intricate play of tedious trivialities—
Henry James in extremis—glimmering vaguely lilt
summer lightning, never startling with a zigzag flash
Mr. Thompson tells why.
The magazines control the trend of literary tast
and the scope of dramatic invention in fiction, anc
the magazines are controlled, in a large degree, b;
men who measure everything by the urban stan
dard of taste. Now the fact is that the extra
urban influence is the preservative of the true
dramatic vision, the conservator of invention, o
lyric purity, of forthright expression.
. -And for this reason, one imagines: that the urban stat
is at present an unnatural state ; it enfevers the mind
warps the nerves, blunts the sensations, demoralises the
judgement. In the country the atmosphere is clear fo
mind and body: the mental reaction to the stimuli o
literature is quicker, juster, and stronger.
The result is that we Americans who write
short stories are the worst handicapped authors
earth. There are two fields open to us : dialeo
U* ew England or negro) and croquet-party sketch
Everything.else is forbidden. If we dare venture
on dramatic vigor or imaginary freedom, as
Shakespeare did, our MSS. are countersigned
“ sensational ” and returned to us. Eliminate the
free handling of unholy passion from the French
short story and there yet remains a residuum oi
powerful fiction, picturesque, startingly dramatic,
novel, original, told with close, fine economy oi
phrasing and with a sumptuous expenditure oi
imagination. Such a story-literature cannot exist
in America, because the magazines of America are
not in sympathy with it.
It does not exist in Australia, because our population is
small ar.d our writers are still learning how to handle
their tools. A few of The Bulletin short stories are
things to be proud of, but there are not enough of them
to constitute “a story-literature.” Wait a while.
While all of our best critics admit that the
French short story is the best that is written, our
magazines will not publish short stories written on
the French model by Americans. For example,
no American magazine would print such a story
as “The Ghost,” by Guy de Maupassant, or one
of the “Letters from My Will,” by Alphonse
Daudet, to say nothing of the far more dramatic
stories, by other French authors, continually
appearing in the Parisian journals. Of course, I
do not refer to stories whose moral tone is ob-
jectionable. I make bold to say that if Poe’s
“ William Wilson,” or his “Fall of the House of
Usher,” or his “Gold Bug” were now first
written and offered to an American magazine,
there are ten chances to one that it would be de-
clined out of hand as being sensational. And yet,
when do we see a story comparable in value to one
of these in any of our monthly journals?
True. And again, looking at home, it is admitted with
some contrition that if Poo’s “ Fall of the House of
Usher” were now first written and offered to The Bulle-
tin it would probably be returned with a suggestion to
cut it down one-half and omit the introduction. But
what would you have ? The Bulletin is The Bulletin—
that is to say, it is not a magazine. It cannot do every-
thing : it does what it can. (Wanted : an Australian
magazine.)
A mere character sketch is not to be properly
called a short story, at least, not in the sense
contemplated in my discussion. The best pro-
ductions of the French short-story writers are
condensed dramas ; now a tragedy, then a comedy,
anon a farce ; but in every case the substance and
essentials of outright drama are present. A local
study, no matter how picturesque, is not a short-
story. It may be good as a bucolic bit of color,
or as a glimpse of out-of-the-way life; but if the
invention, the dramatic organism, the story, in
short, is not there, there is no vital fiction. Style
cannot do everything. It is substance that sus-
tains ; the story must have a body; a living,
active, symmetrical body. This is not all; it
must have the independence, the individuality,
the novelty, the surprise-power of original drama-
tic art, which is the soul of creation, especially
when the fiction must be generated with the
strictest economy of materials. The American
short story wants this inner force.
Points that The Bulletin has insisted on over and over
again.—And, finally, could it be guessed that this article
of Thompson’s was penned as long ago as 1890? Except
that the dialect-story has rather less vogue, its comments
are just as true to-day.
Australian books post-free from The
Bulletin office for stamps or postal notes of any
province:—“Banjo’s” poems, ss. 4d.; Lawson’s
poems, ss. 5d.; Lawson’s prose stories and
sketches, ss. 6d.; Beeke’s “By Beef and Palm,”
Is. 9d. and 2s. 3d.; Becke’s “ Ebbing of the Tide,”
3s. and 4s. ; Dyson’s poems, 6s. 6d. (to be pub-

is Round,” 9d. and Is. 3d.
Reprints.
Flowers from, Omar’s Garland,
[Omar Khayydm (Omar the Tent-maker) was a Persian
poet and astronomer, who died in 1123 at a good age.
There are several translations of the 400 or 500 Arabio
stanzas attributed to him—that of Edward Fitzgerald
(1809-83) dwarfing all others for English-speaking people.
Fitzgerald’s final edition collects 101 stanzas distilling the
essence of Omar's philosophy—that of Solomon, Horace,
Rabelais—and impregnating or completing it with Fitz-
gerald’s own thought and imagery. “The melancholy
riohness of the rolling quatrain with its unicorn rhymes,
the quaint mixture of faroe and solemnity, passion and
playfulness, the abundance of the imagery, the power of
the thought, the seduction of the rhetoric, make the
poem actually, though not original or English, one of the
greatest of English poems.”—Saintsbury.]
I sometimes think that never blows so red
The Rose as where some buried Caesar bled ;
That every Hyacinth the Garden wears
Dropt in her lap from some once lovely Head*
And this reviving Herb whose tender Green
Fledges the River-Lip on which we lean—
Ah, lean upon it lightly ! for who knows
From what once lovely Lip it springs unseen !
Ah, fill the Cup what boots it to repeat
How Time is slipping underneath our Feet:
Unborn To-morrow, and dead Yesterday,
Why fret about them if To-day be sweet!
A Book of Verses underneath the Bough,
A Jug of Wine, a Loaf of Bread—and Thon
Beside me singing in the Wilderness—
Oh, Wilderness were Paradise enow 1
Why, be this Juice the growth of God, who dare
Blaspheme the twisted tendril as a Snare?
A Blessing, we should use it, should we not?
And if a Curse—why, then, Who set it there?
And much as Wine has play’d the Infidel,
And robb’d me of my Robe of Honor—Well,
I wonder often what the Vintners buy
One half so precious as the stuff they sell.'
Oh, threats of Hell and Hopes of Paradise 1
One thing at least is certain— This Life flies;
One thing is certain and the rest is Lies :
The Flower that once has blown for ever dies.
Strange, is it not? that of the myriads who
Before us pass’d the door of Darkness through.
Not one returns to tell us of the Road,
Which to discover we must travel too.
I sent my soul through the Invisible,
Some letter of that After-life to spell;
And by and by my Soul return’d to me,
And answer’d “ I Myself am Heav’n and Hell” r
Heav’n but the Vision of fulfill’d Desire
And Hell the Shadow from a Soul on fire,
Cast on the Darkness into which Ourselves,
So late emerged from, shall so soon ex-pirn
Yesterday This Day’s Madness did prepare ;
Tomorrow's Silence, Triumph, or Despair :
Drink ! for you know not whence you came, nor
why :
Drink ! for you know not why you go, nor where.
Myself when young did eagerly frequent'
Doctor and Saint, and heard great argument
About it and about :—but evermore
Came out by the same door where in I went.
With them the seed of Wisdom did I sow,
And with mine own hands wrought to make it
grow ;
And this was all the Harvest that I reap’d—
“l came like Water, and like Wind I go.”
Into this Universe, and Why not knowing
Nor Whence, like Water willy-nilly flowing;
And out of it, as Wind along the Waste,
I know not Whither, willy-nilly blowing.
Then to the Lip of this poor earthen Urn
I lean’d, the Seoret of my Life to learn :
And Lip to Lip it murmur’d—“ While you live,
Drink I —for once dead, you never shall return.”
And fear not lest Existence closing your
Account, and mine, should know the like no more;
The Eternal Sifkl from that Bowl has pour’d
Millions of Bubbles like us, and will pour.
Oh, Thou, who Man of baser Eartb didst make,
And ev’n with Paradise devise the Snake :
For all the Sin wherewith the Face of Man
Is blacken’d —Man’s forgiveness give—and take!
Some for the Glories of this World ; and some
Sigh for the Prophet’s Paradise to come ;
Ah, take the Cash, and let the Credit go,
Nor heed the rumble of a distant Drum !

BEFORE YOU
X_, E A FM
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round —
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
AWAY BEYOND THE BELT OF BLUE.
Away across the line of trees,
Away beyond the belt of blue,
There is a city high with spires,
Wide fields there are that streams run through.
Sometimes I think I see the spires
And hear a mighty city’s stir ;
And some I smell the sweet of fields
Drowned under drifts of wattle-burr.
But when I smell the sweet of fields,
Or when I hear the city’s voice,
A dimness trembles on my sight,
My soul seems driven to a choice
Between those two aerial lands —
One wrought in marble’s noble hue,
Carven and chased past earthly art,
And reared against a heav’nlier blue;
And one low-lying, simple, slow,
Stretched out past reach of shore or sea,
A limitless, low-lying world,
Where silence reigns eternally.
With every wind the wattle lies
In golden velvet on the fields.
Our earth weaves golden wattle-webs,
But none the gold that this world yields.
And always in the morning sweet,
And sweeter still at noon, at night.
With no too closely-woven shade,
And no too brightly-beating light.
The silence is the sweetest thing,
The silent dawn, the silent day;
A sleep might last a century;
The birds that sing, sing far away.
And O, the clover in the fields !
O, white magnolia on the air !
The sweetest flowers from every land
Grow all together sweetly there.
Or is the space the sweetest thing ?
The rolling, green, unending plains,
The paddocks starred with dandelions,
The dreamy, verdant, English lanes.
My soul seems driven to a choice
Between these two aerial lands—
The simple, silent world of fields,
The City built by artist hands.
The City’s soul knows rarer life,
And greater dreams and deeds than here,
And I was ever one for dreams,
Longed ever in great deeds to share.
And underneath those cupolas
It may be that the poets dwell . . .
But O, the silence of those fields !
And O, the meadow’s endless swell!
So every eve the struggle wakes.
The years go on : not yet I know
If, when I pass I choose my place,
To which of these my soul would go.
LOUISE MACK.
IF DEATH SHOULD COME.
If Death should come before me on a day
To pilot me across the river gray,
(Should I not weep : “ Behold, he beckoneth !
Alas ! O Death ! ”
And shudder and draw backward at his breath,
And with a sudden pallor and despair
Cry to my gods to spare
This trembling life ?
And all the sorrow of the wasted years
And torture of unconsummated hope,
And purpose unfulfilled,
Mixed with the menace of some awful change,
Encompass me ?
Yet why?
Has not the splendor of sweet summer days
Eloated thro’ all my blood ?
The tinted dream of mountain, sky and tree,
Low velvet valleys where slow rivers wind,
And granite forelands barren to the storm,
And cold, still, snowy peaks ;
Thunder of waves, and slumber of the sea
When like a yellow lamp the moon hangs low ;
Fierce whirl and riot of cities,
And the corroding calm of pastoral peace ?
Have I not known all that a man may know—
Triumph, despair, joy, grief, hate, happiness,
The smile of treachery, proved friendship’s hand?
Been humbled to the dust and risen again
And triumphed in my turn, and fallen oft ?
Have I not worn the crown and felt the thorn
Of true love's tenderness and lust’s remorse;
And by a subtle alchemy dissolved
The crust of things and laid the kernel bare ?
What then so darkly daunts?
Ah God ! it is the loneliness, the breath
Of mightier ethers,
The unspeakable vast horror of the void,
That chills the soul,
Trembling within the solemn ante-room
Of Power Supreme !
Q.

A Bairnsdale, Yic., farmer of the unhide-
bound variety planted three acres with the small
black sunflower, w hich needs no attention worth
speaking of. Result: 30 bags of seed, each con-

£25 per acre.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 10, 1896.
Some More Jubilee.
A few days ago Queen Victoria put up a
record as the British sovereign with the
longest reign recorded within the period of
moderately unreliable history. Away back
in the misty early-Saxon times there is al-
leged to have been a monarch of even greater
duration. But in the general uncertainty as
to whether the Saxons really were not Swedes,
and whether there were any Saxons at all, it
seems to be generally agreed to drop him out
and say no more about him. Therefore, Vic-
toria is held to have broken all past British
records in her own particular line of industry,
and the circumstance has been allowed to
pass with such very scant notice that The
Bulletin begins to wonder if the great art
of Grovel is really dying out. The occasion
just gone by was a much greater one than
the royal jubilee of some ten years ago—
jubilees at the best are common and ordinary ;
but the difference in Australian public senti-
ment over the two events was remarkable.
On the jubilee occasion Sydney threatened
to burst into riot. The great congratulatory
meeting at the Exhibition Building was
packed with Freemasons, footballers, prize-
fighters, Primrose Leaguers, sailors,'police,
alleged troops, and swarms of miscellaneous
loyalists. More troops waited outside, ready
to rush in and drown the insurrection in
blood if necessary. The famous address to
the alleged “ Pre-eminent in Grief ” came to
light on this occasion. The seven provinces
were full of jubilee literature written in the
spirit of one who goes down on all-fours and
bumps his head against the dust in honor of
his little tin deity. Three Primrose Leaguers
called round at The Bulletin office with
sticks to assault somebody for not taking a
proper view of the matter, but thought
better of it and went away without doing any
damage. It was a great occasion of glare
and gas and whoop and victuals and illumina-
tion and bad poetry, and everybody read
loyal addresses to everybody else, and Sydney
flowed with beer and excitement and exhor-
tation. /
♦ * * *
Looking back on that wild episode of less
than ten years ago it looks practically impos-
sible. On the present occasion, it is true,
Victoria requested that the public and
official riot should be postponed till the end
of the sixtieth year of her reign. But so
far as Australia was concerned there was
practically nothing to postpone. Unofficial
grovel was allowed to an unlimited extent,
but the public was apathetic. Except that
President Zeal, of the Victorian House of
48 Rich Landlords, gave some kind of enter-
tainment to his fellow fossils, and the party
went to the theatre afterwards, nothing
worth speaking of happened, or seemed as if
it had the slightest desire to happen. There
is no Primrose League now, and Tanker-
ville Bennet, of that wondrous organisation,
rages in the press about the beastly dis-
loyalty of Australia no longer. No aggres-
sive British person calls up at this office now
and wants to fight the staff for want of
respect to J. Bull and the “Crown.” On
the day when Victoria put up her great re-
cord of placid longevity and mild continuous-
ness hardly anybody even wrote to the
papers about it in an enthusiastic strain. A
few leading articles appeared here and there,
but they were mere weak platitudes and
figures. Also, one or two municipal councils
talked of sending addresses, and the dirt-
colored ferry-boat in Sydney Harbor had a flag
hying, and that was about all. There was so
little general interest in the event that when
a Bulletin paragraph badly misplaced the
date of it, no one noticed the mistake with
sufficient energy to write about it. For
these and other reasons it would seem that
the great art of grovel is by no means what
it was in Australia.
/ * * * *
Probably the steady increase in the num-
bers of the native-born population is wearing
out the active spirit of loyalty. No com-
munity can take an active interest for ever
in a very ordinary-looking person whom it
never saw, and is never likely to see, and
who, if she was seen, would look very much
like any other ordinary-looking person. The
percentage of the population which has ever
been within less than 10,000 miles range of
the Throne is steadily diminishing, and
loyalty at 10,000 miles range is a very much
strained emotion. In former days God and
Victoria and the Bunyip were the three
great abstractions of this country, and the
same fading away of interest is perceptible as
regards all three. The Bunyip is dropping
out of local mythology because he refuses to
turn up and keep himself in evidence.
Victoria also doesn’t turn up, and long
absence has created a popular feeling that
she really doesn’t matter. And as for the
common Deity of nineteenth-century com-
merce, absence and the collection - plate
and the doings of His alleged priests
and a growing suspicion that He isn’t
anything like the Deity of the New Testa-
ment teachings are gradually dropping him
also into oblivion. The latter-day hustle
for sordid bread and meat, too, has done a
lot to wreck Australia’s loyalty. Ten years
ago, in the jubilee days, this.country was
still young and garish and could spare
the time and energy to go out and
howl over mere abstractions. The banks
hadn’t gone broke then, and the borrowed
money was still rolling in gaily, and both
N.S. Wales and Victoria had their booms in
full blast, and there was work (paid for, more
or less directly, with borrowed money) for
nearly everybody. There was no Labor
Bureau then, no sand-shifting jobs, no
pauper relief in the shape of scrub-cutting on
the desolate Bogan, no land-tax or income-
tax or deficit. Wool-prices were high, and
the average payment for making a pair of
slop pants was nearly three times what it
now is, and the Public Service hadn’t been
retrenched, and the populations of Sydney
and Melbourne hadn’t started to diminish,
and the Public Works policy was a steady
source of employment, and generally things
were different. Now Australia has suddenly
grown very old, and tired, and poor, and
doesn’t go out in its old volatile fashion to
yell from pure lightness of heart. The gene-
ral feeling is that loyalty isn’t victuals, and
Victoria isn’t boots, or clothes, or ready
money, and this pretty accurately expresses
one great reason why there is no more jubilee
in the land, and probably never will be.
* * * *
It is an open question whether Australian
republicanism also is quite as strong a senti-
ment as it was ten years ago. A great part
of it—the aggressive, brick-thro wing part—
was simply an expression of disgust at the
crawlsome spirit of loyalty and the “ Pre-emi-
nent in Grief ” variety of literature, and a
well-founded desire to kick that objectionable
sentiment hard. Nowadays there is little
very conspicuous loyalty to kick, and the
“Pre-eminent in Grief” species of litera-
ture has almost wholly ceased to vex the
land, and the Primrose League, as already
mentioned, is defunct. The lowest stratum
of republicanism is like the lowest stratum
of anything else. One glaring variety of
Protestantism, for instance, would die a
natural death if there were no Catholics left
to infuriate with its yellow banner on the
12th of July, and in like manner one section
[of Australian republicanism dropped out of
;sight to a great extent when the frantic
loyalism of ten years ago became ingloriously
defunct for want of sufficient interest in
itself to keep itself alive. The Throne and
Person are not objected to in Australia with
quite the old vigor of language, because there
is an increasing tendency to forget that such
things exist. A continued study of the
British papers seems even to suggest
that John Bull doesn’t grovel with
quite his old abundant worminess, and
in this theory, doubtful as it is, there
seems to be some slight, hope for better
thing in the future. If a constitutional
monarchy like that of Britain could exist
absolutely without the element of grovel,
then The Bulletin might possibly give up
preaching republicanism. The British sove-
reign, being almost entirely devoid of political
authority, can do no harm, in a purely poli-
tical sense, that is worth mentioning. She
permanently and peacefully stops a hole
through which the cold blast of party politics
would otherwise blow. She saves the worry
of periodically electing a State figurehead,
and though the hereditary principle is
supremely ridiculous, yet if the nation
chooses to let a person be born into a certain
position merely to save the dust which would
arise through other people rushing for it,
the idea w r ould do well enough until a per-
manent steam monarch could be invented.
So far, however, the curse of monarchy has
been that it led a large section of the com-
munity to go on all-fours, and rub its head
in the dust by way of abject abasement, and
write bad prose and awful poetry, and it
has given rise to the National Anathema and
other horrors. And though there are hopes
that the dog-like part of the British nation
isn’t so abjectly horizontal as it was, still
there is little reliable data to go upon so far.
* * * *
Whether the increasing lack of interest in
the British monarch and monarchy will
hasten or retard the arrival of Australian
independence it is difficult to say. There
are apparently far fewer people in this
country who would exert themselves violently
to maintain the British connection than
there were ten years ago, and it is possible
there are also fewer who take enough inter-
est in it to object to it very much. It
requires a certain amount of interest to be
actively hostile, and there are comparatively
few signs of that interest at present. The
Bulletin sometimes imagines that a time
will come when the mass of Australians will
not really be aware that there is any British
monarch at all. They used to be kept
aware of the fact by the daily papers and
by much National Anthem and by jubilees
and birthday celebrations and the like ; but
the Anthem is a great deal less virulent
than it was, and the jubilee business is appa-
rently played out, and the press, to suit the
taste of its readers, enthuses in a minor key
and often drops the subject altogether.
Even The Bulletin notices just here that
it often forgets Victoria's existence for six
issues at a stretch. Possibly a time may
come when the monarchy will gently and un-
consciously cease to exist because one sove-
reign will die and the nation will quite forget
to crown another one. The fact that the
British monarchy will cease to exist some
day may be taken for granted, as everything
ceases to exist some day, and this ending
appears, in the present trend of things, to
be as probable as any other. f
The Law and the Regulations.
At Albury Circuit Court Mr. Justice Simpson
had the courage to state his views on the sub-
ject of solitary confinement. Fortunately his
Honor has not been giving expression to mere
sentimental objections ; he asserts that he had
an opportunity of observing the working of the
solitary confinement system, and that no one
unacquainted with the treatment can realise
the mental suffering prisoners must undergo.
The Bulletin wishes that all Judges adminis-
tering the criminal law would follow Judge
Simpson’s example, and make themselves
acquainted with the effect of the draconian
sentences imposed by them with such airy
liberality.
♦ * * £
One of the worst features of the N.S. W.
penal system is the secrecy which surrounds
the administration of the prisons. Strange
stories of the iniquitous way in which prisoners
are treated now and then reach the public ear,
only, however, to be contradicted by some frog-
blooded official from his place in Parliament;
and a credulous House and an apathetic public
unreservedly accept the denial. The N.S. W.
criminal code is a century behind civilisation.
The traditions of Botany Bay still cling to us ;
all N.S.W.’s penal laws are overshadowed by
the triangles and the gallows.
* * * *
A prisoner, sentenced to three years im-
prisonment, is doomed to pass nine months in
solitary confinement—an addition to his sen-
tence brutal in the extreme. Wheuce comes
the authority for inflicting this torture? In
vain is the N.S. W. statute-book appealed to for
an answer. The only hint given is in the Gaols
Act, which provides for the Governor-in-
Coundl making regulations for the conduct of
prisons, and, given that authority, a merciless
Executive has fixed this solitary confinement as
an incident of all long periods of imprisonment ! !
One Act alone deals directly with the subject—
the Criminal Law Amendment Act—which, in
this instance, falls far below its monotonous
level of brutal cruelty, and becomes almost
humane. Sec. 398 provides that in awarding
imprisonment, the court may direct the offender
to be kept in solitary confinement for any por-
tion, or portions, of the term “ not exceeding
one month at a time, and not exceeding three
months within any year.” That is, no Judge,
even of the Supreme Court, in sentencing a
prisoner, can inflict anything like the period of
solitary confinement to which every long-sen-
tence prisoner is subjected bv the gaol regula-
tions !
* * * *
Judge Simpson, like everyone else who has
really considered the question, doubts very
much if solitary confinement ever does any
good. He further says that in future, except
in the worst form of cases, he will not impose
sentences that will carry with them solitary
confinement. When a native-born Supreme
Court Judge speaks like this, it affords hope
that N.S. Wales may, in a few years, shake off
her convict-forged fetters. The Bulletin
would suggest that next time Judge Simpson
feels compelled to impose more than three years
imprisonment, he should fix, in the sentence,
the terms of “ solitary ” to be served ; then, in
the event of the gaol authorities attempting to
enforce their savage regulations, the prisoner
and his friends will be able to test their validity
in the Supreme Court.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Johnson on Language.
Whittingdale Johnson, a well-known Sydney
magistrate, took it upon him the other day to
summarily repeal a great part of the law against
obscene language, and, considering the possible
consequences of his high-handed action, The
Bulletin invites the Government to seriously
consider Mr. Johnson. The trouble began with
a policeman arresting a man for using particu-
larly gamey language in the street. Consider-
ing that there is probably more loathsome
language in the streets of Sydney than in any
other city of its size, and that there are few
places in it where you can hear less than three
curses and one unpublishable expression at
once, and that the average policeman simply
wanders by and lets them curse, and that the
law against obscene language is next door to a
dead letter (partly because policemen won’t
arrest offenders, and chiefly because a ridicu-
lously incompetent magistracy does practically
nothing to them when they are arrested) —con-
sidering all these things, it would seem that
this policeman was a little more deserving than
the average. Magistrate Johnson, however,
actually sat on him heavily for his attempt to
enforce the law—as follows (Sydney D.T. re-
port) :
At this point the magistrate asked the constable whether
there were any persons within hearing who were likely to
have been shocked by the language complained of. The
constable could give no proof to this effect, whereupon
the magistrate remarked that he could not deal with the
case under the Vagrant Act. The language, he said, was
the normal language of some people, and he seldom
passed a group of laboring men without hearing similar
expressions. So long as they were not used in the pres-
ence of females or other persons likely to be shocked by
them the police had no right to interfere. He was con
tinually endeavoring to instruct the police in their duties
in this respect, but it seemed useless.
The Bulletin regretfully records its opinion
just here that Magistrate Johnson seems even
more useless than his attempts at instructing
the police. The law distinctly prohibits ob-
scene language in public places, without any
reference to whether the people within hearing
were likely to be shocked or not. The theory
that it is perfectly allowable unless the people
who have to listen to it are prepared to go into
court and swear that they were shocked i?
practically a license to everybody to be as dis-
gusting as he pleases, seeing that very few
people can afford the time to follow up the
offender in the police-courts, or care for the
publicity it involves. In the present case the
policeman had to listen to the language, and so
had the inhabitants of any adjacent houses,
and according to the Johnson theory these
people must go on hearing it indefinitely. No
doubt the great Australian adjective and other
things are the “normal language of some
people,” just as burglary is the normal occupa-
tion of some other people ; it is so largely
because the police and the Johnson variety of
magistrate fail so disgracefully j n their duty.
But the fact that there is much cursing in the
streets doesn’t make cursing legal or pleasant
or beautiful, any more than the fact that there
is much dirt makes dirt any cleaner, or the fact
that there is much embezzlement makes em-
bezzlement any the more honest,and the factthat
there is much cursing is no reason why there
should be more cursing. The moral aspect of the
case may not affect Mr. Johnson, but the legal
aspect certainly does. And the legal aspect is
that the law makes obscenity and profanity in
public places distinctly punishable ; that the
police and Mr. Johnson are both paid by the
country to enforce the law as it stands; and
that Mr. Johnson absolutely refused in this
case to enforce the law, and practically ordered
the police not to enforce it either. In other
words, he instructed the police to let obscene
language, under certain circumstances, which
are of frequent occurrence, fill the air with im-
punity, and, because it is already bad, he holds
that it is a good thing to let it remain bad or
grow worse. And the country doesn’t pay Mr.
Johnson his salary that he may, directly or
indirectly, encourage obscene language under
any circumstances whatever.
Freetrade and Japs. : the Doom of the
White Cabinet Maker.
Dear Bulletin, —Knowing that the Australian
must earn a decent wage to live, and being
desirous of convincing him of the Freetrade
error, I give you an idea of how Freetrade
suits the clever Jap. In the cabinet-trade
Sydney and Melbourne are overrun with
Chinese, and the white man (even with steam
appliances) finds that the Chow is too clever
for him. Now, the Jap. has not yet had to be
reckoned with ; here is a sample of his skill
A 4ft. 6in. oval 100 table, best Italian walnut
veneer, inlaid marquetrie centre—with outer
strings of willow, and carved edge moulding-
deep and bold ; four pillar and claw pedestal
beautifully turned and carved out of Italian
walnut. The claws carved clean and bold on
cap, toe and sides, centre drop ornaments,
castored and well polished (not varnished, but
properly rubbed), landed in any port free-on-
wharf to Australia, 50s.—a total eclipse of even
the white slave who hawks his goods in London
on a Friday night and, because he can’t procure
a purchaser, pawns them for half their value.
Again, a Chippendale whatnot, beautifully
enamelled, five plate-glass bevels in back, clean,
well-cut, handsome pattern fret work—a centre
cabinet, glass door, side shelves, square turned,
work both novel and rare, 6ft. Jin. high by
4ft. 3in. wide, f.0.w., Australia, 375., honestly
worth under the hammer in Sydney, £7.
This would be worth £4 unpolished in
London. A music-stool with patent screw,
five-inch pillar—fluted and carved—three claws
beautifully carved, twelve-inch top, carved
rim, upholstered in hair, well-sewn edge, stiff
and strong, covered in morocco—not skivers—
and buttoned down and well polished, f.0.w.,
Australia, 11s. —worth in London 165., un-


“He has an exquisite style to refrain
from.”— Mks. Meynell.
Onlys : “ You ask what is Art? Take
two parts of Nature, one of Man; add something
that is neither Man nor Nature ; serve up in a gilt
frame or otherwise to suit the taste. Or, Art is
Nature’s cry when the soul of a Genius has been
breathed into her lungs. Or, Art is the medium
of exchange between Man and Nature —with
Genius stamped on the one side; on the other,
sympathy,”
polished and not upholstered. A high-low
suite of frames complete, comprising six small
chairs, balloon backs, cabriole legs, beautifully
made with iron cleats over every joint, prettily
carved —one lady’s chair, one gent’s chair, and
one six-foot-six high-low couch one mass of
bold, deep, beautifully-relieved carvings—
stuffing bars out of red wood quite as strong as
the beech used in English work ; the whole
suite of nine pieces, unpolished, £B. They
much resemble the Italian frames Rittenberg
used to import and never sold for less than £lB
per suite. These goods are to be dumped on
the Australian market in ship-loads, and the
controlling power is now journeying all over
the principal cities of Australia. Will Premier
Reid please digest this and ask himself—How
are any cabinet-makers, turners, upholsterers,
carvers and polishers to compete against
these clever Japs. ? They simply can-
not compete and will starve, and yet in the
face of such truths daily being told the solid
grafter has to submit and be driven out of his
own market by preventible aliens —too strong
for the slaves of Curtain-road, London. —
Cephalos.
The "Ass” on Manly Independence.
Liberty, the Sydney organ of the National Ass,
denounces the Old Age Pension idea and insidi-
ously attacks the noble quality of Thrift —the
thing Fat Man gets an extra vote for —in the
rear, as follows :
The latest move is that to provide a system of old-age
pensions. It is impossible to speak in sufficiently strong
terms of deprecation of this proposal. It is one of the
most dangerous, because most insidious, attacks upon the
quality of manly independence which has raised the
British people to their proud ascendancy amongst the
nations, and this colony in particular to its present strong
position. Nothing could more clearly demonstrate the
weakness of moral fibre which is gradually but surely
pervading the public spirit of the colony than the fact
that a body of presumedly intelligent men are prepared
to recommend the adoption of a system of State pensions.
Evidently the beautiful virtue of Thrift has
suddenly fallen to a heavy discount. Thrift
consists largely in the individual putting away
money to support him in his old age. Under
the Old Age Pension scheme the individual pro-
poses to do this ; only, instead of putting money
in the bank run by the National Ass person
and letting him fool it away (as he did in the
cases of the Federal Bank, City of Melb. ditto,
the Mercantile, Real Estate, Commercial of
S.A., Bank of S.A., Van Diemen’s Land, Bank
of Queensland, Bank of Australia, Town and
Country, Bank of Port Phillip, Oriental, Bank
of Tasmania, and several dozen more) it is pro-
posed to put the savings in charge of the State,
where there seems reason to believe they will
be moderately safe. The individual will tax
himself to provide the money, and will receive
it back again under certain fixed conditions im-
posed on himself by himself, and thus he pro-
poses to teach himself on a large scale the vir-
tues of thrift, foresight, self-denial, and other
beautiful things which the National Ass has
strongly commended until now. The only
aspect in which the matter is not one of mere
■thrift and self-denial is that the first batch of
annuitants —those who are already qualified
—will not have put up the funds them-
selves, but will be provided for by the rest
of the population. These people don’t
demand the charity which it is proposed to
extend to them; they are far too few to
make any demand that can be heard at the
polls, or to bring any pressure to bear on Par-
liament, or otherwise to influence the matter in
any way. A very large proportion of them are
itinerant derelicts who haven’t even votes, and
if they all had votes they wouldn’t amount to a
circumstance. If the Old Age Pension scheme
is adopted it must be because the people who
have to pay for the pensions are in favor of it,
and these people propose to pay, not only to
establish a fund for their own old age when
they get there, but also for the derelicts who
are past paying—the social wrecks and paupers
and outcasts left over from the good old days
of “ manly independence ” that the Ass organ
laments about. This is a beautiful example of
charity as well as thrift. Also it has a manly
independence of its own. The present genera-
tion is beginning to decide that, instead of
going on in the ancient way which the National
Ass approves of, and, if disaster comes upon
them, living on alms in their old age, they will
begin now to make provision for the future by
their own act, at their own expense. And if
that doesn’t come under the head of manly in-
dependence—well, what is the meaning of the
term ?
The Vested Interest in Crime.
Speaking recently at a political meeting, in the
Sydney Protestant Hall, W. P. Crick, lawyer
and legislator, frankly declared that he could
advocate Protection without being accused of
self-interest, as all he wanted was a good crop
of criminals every year. This naive statement
makes one pause and reflect on the enormous
vested interest there is in crime and general
human frailty. The average man talks as if
employment per se were the supreme object to
be attained, and, under existing conditions, he
dreads anything likely to diminish the number
of billets, however much good it might do the
community at large. We all wonder why it is
that conditions seemingly calculated to make
people criminals are allowed to continue from
year to year ; but just think what would happen
if the supply of law-breakers ceased ! There
would be no more openings in the “ foorce ” for
the raw-boned “Moikes” and “ Tugalds,”
there would be retrenchment amongst gaolers
and turnkeys, the West Macquarie pet
and his fellows in the Devil’s brigade could
no longer gain laurels (and fees) by restoring
the burglar or pickpocket to his friends and his
relations, and the proprietors and distributors
of the evening papers would find a lamentable
falling-off in their circulation. The contractors
who supply the prisons would have a reduced
custom, and the flourishing industry of building
new gaols and court-houses in every one-horse
township would receive a severe check. If the
Prohibitionists had their way, what would be-
come of the dividends of the brewing companies
or the monopoly values of the hotel properties ?
If social purity prevailed, how could the saintly
landlord get liis fancy rents for houses now oc-
cupied by the daughters of joy ? Let the
betting-house and the tote be suppressed and
Rachel would weep for her starving hook-
nosed offspring. Vice and crime are a great
local industry and must be maintained at all
hazards, otherwise many worthy and unworthy
people will have to join the unemployed.
To avert such a catastrophe let us fill
our legislatures with lawyers to prevent
the making of better laws or to so mangle such
as are passed that there will ensue an endless
supply of court cases and appeals whereby the
legal sharks may wax fat on the litigants’ sub-
stance. Let employers pay inadequate salaries
to those who hold responsible positions and
they will thus multiply embezzlers. Allow the
publican to vend the vilest fire-water unchecked
to madden the thirsty citizen into crime or
lunacy. Let us continue to associate first
offenders with hardened gaol-birds. Let the
police shepherd the discharged convict and run
him in as a suspect ere he can possibly get work
to do. Let every seedily-attired man about
the streets be arrested for the alleged crime of
being poor. Let promotion in the police
depend on the number of convictions obtained.
It is to be feared that a long time will elapse
ere the extensive and influential vested interest
in a “ good crop of criminals ” is much inter-
fered with through the efforts of the whole
body of social reformers.
The Parson Who Blabbed.
Archdeacon Piddington, a Church of England
cleric, is chaplain of Tam worth Gaol, N.S.W.
In the course of his ministrations to prisoners
he recently interviewed two brothers named
Dederer, charged with murdering an Indian
hawker. They were rough, unlettered men—
wild growths of the wild bush, never brought
into contact with humanising or civilising in-
fluences. Of religion they were as ignorant as
of most other things, but they seem to have had
that instinctive faith in the supernatural which
is the attribute of undeveloped intellect in indi-
viduals and races. They were no more Church
of Englanders, apparently, than they were
Parsees or Muggletonians, but were handed over
to the Church of England in accordance with
the local official tradition which credits to that
organisation waifs unclaimed by other creeds.
To them came Archdeacon Piddington, invested
with all the sanctity of the priestly office, repre-
sented as the intercessor with heaven, the embodi-
ment of virtue on earth. This priest questioned
these prisoners about the crime imputed to
them, and exhorted them to tell him the whole
truth. Then, solemnly: “Now, which of you
killed the hawker?” “ I did,” said one. The
trial came on. Archdeacon Piddington got
into the witness-box. “ That man, one of the
prisoners, told me he killed the hawker.” And,
partly on this evidence (at any rate after it), the
brothers were found guilty and sentenced to
death. Did Mr. Piddington use any per-
suasion to get the confession out ? He says he
only offered them “a moral and religious in-
ducement ? ” But did he explicitly tell them
what might be the consequence of yielding to
this moral and religious inducement? Did he
caution them that “ whatever you may say will
be used in evidence against you at the trial ? ”
Did he say point blank : “ What you tell me as
a priest I’m going to swear against you as a
witness. If you tell the truth, you may
possibly go to Heaven, but you will almost
certainly hang ? ” Or did he induce them to
confess on pious grounds altogether, omitting
to give them notice that he intended to blab
the confession on the grounds of exalted
citizenship ? On these points, so far, Arch-
deacon Piddington, as reported in the daily
papers, is not clear. But even admitting that,
once the Archdeacon had acquired knowledge of
the Dederers’ guilt, he should divulge it in
the interests of the community—-admitting this,
which no priest of the R.C. Church would
admit for a moment in the circumstances—why
should he acquire it to the Dederers’ injury ?
Archdeacon Piddington has bruited it to the
world that a doctor, a lawyer, can keep a pro-
fessional secret, a priest of the R.C. faith can
die rather than reveal the confidences of the
confessional, but a Church of England parson—
well, The Bulletin believes that Archdeacon
Piddington will yet be ready to admit that he
has done his Church an inexpressibly grievous
wrong.

The Convict Butler.
“H.T.C.” to The Bulletin :
Re the convict Butler. “ P’leeceman X” has left a gap
or two in his interesting letter. I lived in Dunedin
when Butler first burgled and got four years. In
addition to stealing a gold chalice from the R.C.
bishop, he burgled a leading jeweller. The plunder never
came to light. Shortly after the completion of his sentence,
the residence of the detective who arrested him was found
in flames, and the same night the Crown-Prosecutor’s
house was burgled; while the police were hot on the scent,
the Dewar tragedy takes place. Butler is traced to a
suburban hotel, where, while waiting for breakfast,
he plays selections of mass music on the piano. The
waitress, a Catholic, pauses in, her work and enters the
room. The music was l2th Mass and on each
page was stamped “P. P. Moran, St. Joseph’s.” The
Crown received the evidence of the waitress in the trial
for murder as highly important. The murder took place
at about 5 a.m. or a little after. Butler was at the hotel
between six and seven. Though she was subjected to a
searching examination the girl’s evidence only amounted
to identification of the Bishop’s music. When arrested,
Butler was at Waikouaiti, about eight miles from Dun-
edin. A pair of field-glasses found on him had the name
“ Stamper ” inscribed on a sliver plate, the name of
the prosecuting barrister at his first trial, whose house,
only a day or two previous, he had burglariously
entered. s.s to the motive for the Dewar murder, the
theory was generally accepted that Nellie Dewar, who
was engaged to Butler when he started his four years’
sentence, was entrusted with the jewellery he had
acquired by burglary. Before he was released she mar-
ried, and this and the fact that his old flame would not
hand over the proceeds of his former robberies incited
him to vengeance. It was proved at the trial that
drawers and boxes were emptied of their contents, which
were strewn all over the place. This was the popular
theory. The populace was much disgusted at Butler’s
acquittal on the murder charge, and it was only by a ruse
that he was conveyed safely that night from court to
gaol.
Queen Victoria last week put up the record
reign in British history (over 59 years), and she
is now lighting out in pursuit of Louis XlV.’s
famous French record of 72 years. London
Telegraph called for plans and specifications
for a great national rejoicing to celebrate the
event, and the following list (by the Evening
News’s London correspondent) contains the
principal suggestions sent in :
A volunteer review. All London, even the tiniest
streets, courts, groves, and alleys, to be illuminated. Two
celebrations, one “finite,” one “infinite.” The “finite”
general rejoicing, the “infinite” a national holiday.
Everybody to get their wages. A big bonus to be given
to encourage weak-kneed British youth to join the “first
line of defence.” The gift to the Queen by the nation of
a mansion, to be absolutely her own. The collection of a
charitable fund, to be called “ Queen Victoria’s Boon.” A
revising of the words of the National Anthem, which are
denounced as “ doggerel.” An annual bank-holiday, to
be called “ Victoria’s Day,” and to be observed for ever.
An issue of gold and silver coinage, to be offered to the
public at double its face-value, the profit to be given
to the poor. The passing of an Act of Parliament
making it a criminal offence to pay a woman less
than 2s. for a day’s work of twelve hours. That
the pay of soldiers should be raised to that of police and
firemen. All the board school children of England to
have a picnic and a medal. The city to present a golden
casket to the Queen. A special commemoration Bible to
be published. A History of the reign of Queen Victoria
—bound in morocco, and profusely illustrated, to be pub-
lished at 6d. That Her Majesty be humbly entreated to
sit for her portrait to a British painter. That, all deserv-
ing indigent persons of 77 years be “ set up” for the rest
of their days. A huge illuminated cyclists’ parade. That
thAevent be celebrated by building cottage homes for
and giving pensions to those who can work no more. That
“ God Save the Queen ” be played and sung simul-
taneously all over Great Britain, her colonies and depen-
dencies at noon precisely (Greenwich time), September
23. That on that day all theatres and music-halls be
thrown open free. That “homes” be built and endowed
for “ poor children by the sea.” That London should be
encircled with an open space a mile wide, to be called
“ Victoria’s belt.” The establishment of a central “sus-
tentation fund” for the “ half-starved clergy.” That all
the London theatres should present a Shakesperian play
for this night only, admission free ; programmes (illumin-
ated) gratis. That a new arrangement of the laws
take place, to be called “ The Code Victoria.”
That widows in workhouses of the age of 77
be withdrawn from there and placed in alms-
houses specially constructed for them. That each
board school child in the land receive a mug, a Bible,
a bag of bulls’-eyes, a portrait of her Majesty, an
orange, and a history of her reign. That a “History of
the Reign of Victoria ” illustrated, with a portrait of her
Majesty, be presented to every person in the United
Kingdom free. (Bradford regrets it cannot make a sug-
gestion at present, as the town clerk is just dead.) The
publication daily in the morning papers of a portion of
the Holy Scriptures, “ in plain, eveiyday language.” The
culminating suggestion was that “ her Majesty should lay
the first stone of a memorial chapel to her own memory,
said chapel to be erected as near as possible in West-
minster Abbey.”
Truly John Bull and his wife don’t know how
to rejoice. What England really wants on
such an occasion is a universal drunk and a
general dog-fight, and every man to have the
privilege, for one night only, to wallop his
“missus” within reasonable limits, also the
passing of an Act of Parliament by which
Britain annexes every place, and the proceed-
ings to finish with an international cricket-
match in which England makes 18,000 runs.
Magistrate Johnson, of Sydney, has instructed
the police from the bench that obscene language
is to be permitted unless it is used in the
Eresence of people who are likely to be shocked
y it. How is a policeman to recognise a
person who is likely to be shocked ? Pre-
sumably by his black coat and his tall bell-
topper. Mr. Johnson discharged the obscene
person brought before him, because it couldn’t
be proved that people likely to be shocked
were present. As the language was used after
11 p.m., presumably the people in the adjacent
houses couldn’t come out and state that they
disliked the language because they hadn’t their
clothes on. The Johnson variety of logic
admits of unlimited cursing in the hearing of a
person who is in bed, or is in a bath, or is
afraid to come out and state his or her objec-
tions for fear of worse language or road-
metal, or in the hearing of children who are too
young to have any distinct views on the matter,
or of anybody who is afraid of being “ stoushed ”
afterwards. Also, it gives the offender abund-
ant time to get away while the policeman is
trying to find out if he has been heard by any-
body who is likely to object. Most people who
don’t object to obscenity are in that state
simply because they are used to it, and they
are used to it simply because they have to hear
it, and they have to hear it because the police
and Johnson are disgracefully lax in their duty.
And, as Johnson facilitates cursing because
they are used to it already, they will necessarily
keep on being used to it, and therefore they
will keep on not objecting. The logic of John-
son works in a circle.
Queensland, which was announced a week or
two ago as having passed the Federation Bill,
all save the formality of the Governor’s
sanction, has managed to get that measure tied
in a hard knot at the last moment, after all.
The Assembly refuses to let the people elect
delegates, and insists on appointing them it-
self. The Council demands a voice in the
appointment —in other words, it wants part of
the steal—and the result is a deadlock. The
Westralian Government also has quite unex-
pectedly resolved to move in the matter, and
the Federation Bill has been introduced in the
Perth Legislature. Therefore, with Queensland
not yet arrived, and with a chance of Westralia
arriving later on, there is something to be said
for Premier Reid’s policy of waiting a little
longer before fixing a date for the election of
delegates. On the other hand, it might be just
as well for Australia in the end if Queensland
and Westralia were not there at the beginning
of the Federation. The other four provinces
would probably fix up a much more democratic
Federal Constitution without them, and then if
the two outsiders wanted to come in afterwards
they would have to accept the ready-made
Constitution or none at all. And the chances
are that the pressure of circumstances would
compel them to come in. Still, for the sake
of preserving the Federal spirit—what there is
of it —it is impossible to rush on the elections
for the express purpose of preventing Queens-
land and Westralia having a say in drafting the
Constitution, therefore the logic of events goes
to prove that Premier Reid is right.
The recent Yic. Leg. Council vacancy for the
North-West Province —said vacancy being only
brought about by the fact that the fossil who
previously represented the district refused to
go back —was a fine example of the incurable-
ness of Victoria’s unpaid Council. The N.W.
Province covers about one-fifth of the country,
so the reasons why there were only two candi-
dates were pretty numerous. AnM.L.C., as is
well-known, must have an income of £lOO a
year from land, therefore he must be a very
considerable landowner. Out of its handful of
Fat Men, therefore, this constituency, if it
was to have a contest at all, had to find two
with the requisite income from land, with
enough leisure to spend a great part of their
time in Melbourne, enough money to keep them
there without payment for their legislative
services (this meant that the qualifica-
tion was really an independent income of
<£soo or £6OO a year at the very least), enough
ready cash to canvass the huge constituency
where railways are few and travelling very
costly, and enough energy or a big enough axe
to grind to make it worth while going in for the
business. Under these disadvantages the dis-
trict raised just two dull, orthodox Tories, with
the requisite qualifications —men who said that
the Council should continue to exist because its
advantages were “obvious”—and Pharez
Phillips was elected. Phillips said that he
believed in “political rest;” and the other
candidate was of the same view. Nobody
could find a candidate with the requisite quali-
fications who believed in motion, for the suffi-
cient reason that when a man gets rich enough
to qualify for the House of 48 Rich Landlords
he is generally so tired that motion makes him
feel bad in his stummick.

Melb. Age cheerfully relates that a young man
named Callender, convicted on a charge of
tampering with a nine-year-old girl, was
brought into the flogging-room at Pentridge
last week to receive 15 lashes. “The culprit,
however, had not got the physical courage to
face the music. He no sooner beheld the
triangles and the flagellator with his coat off
and a bandana encircling his head and the cords
in his hands for tricing him up than he trem-
bled in every fibre of his body, and went off in
a dead faint. The doctor in attendance care-
fully diagnosed the physical condition of the
prisoner, and owing to his representation it was
deemed desirable to postpone the punishment.
In these cases, of course, the date of the post-
ponement cannot be fixed definitely.” And so,
for the present the Law is employed in tuning
Callender up to concert-pitch, that he may
be able to face the music of the “ cat” at the
earliest possible date. The fact that he can’t
contemplate the prospect of fifteen lashes
with equanimity makes Callender a difficult
subject to build up, but the Law hopes that,
with the aid of tonics, it will succeed in bring-
ing off the flogging at next attempt. If Cal-
lender faints again at the sight of the triangles
and the flagellator’s shirt-sleeves and the ban-
dana handkerchief for absorbing brow-sweat,
he will have to endure further strychnine and
beef-tea, and another long dose of mental
agony whilst undergoing a third preparation
for his gory engagement. And when the Law
has completed its contract—as it certainly will,
if Callender doesn’t faint to death in the
meantime—the sexual lunatic will go back into
the world and tamper with little girls as before.
His brain-disease having been aggravated by
the cat-cure, he will, naturally, become a more
rabid tamperer than ever. That’s all.
Several N.S.W. legislators express them-
selves in favor of M.’sP. being allowed to
frank all their official correspondence, and
it is understood that a scheme to effect
this is now under consideration. No doubt
it is often a tax on a member to have to
pay postage on certain official letters, and
as there are gabby letter-writers as well as
gabby talkers the outlay in postage-stamps
must be sometimes very considerable. Yet this
franking onslaught should be repulsed. Are
members to be believed when they say they will
frank only official correspondence ? The case
pivots here. “ Address the book to the Earl of
Ross,” wrote the amiable Maria Edgeworth
to Crofton Croker in the old English frank-
ing days. “ Put an inside cover on addressed
to me. The Earl will tear off the outside cover
and then frank the book on to me.” The Earl
and Maria were as honest as the average
N.S.W. member.
Superintendent Sadlier, of the Victorian
police, reports that, in his opinion, the proviso
contained in the Infant Life Protection Act,
compelling nurses to keep a record of the names
and addresses of the mothers of children com-
mitted to their care, conduces to the abandon-
ment of illegitimate children, owing to the
record being open to inspection by visitors, and
the mothers’ consequent dread that their frailty
will be published. One can mentally see a
roach-backed Social Gimlet female with an
ill-fitting dress and a libidinous smirk gloating
over the record, and then departing to harry
the unfortunate mothers for further particulars.
And the way of a girl who has loved too well,
and her lover, who have fallen into the hands
of the aforesaid female, is indeed rough. Mrs.
Grundy, when she opposes a Foundling Hos-
pital where a child could be left without the
slightest chance of anyone discovering the
identity of its mother, instigates the wholesale
murder of little children.
It is notorious that the legal persecution con-
demned by Judge Coffey at Darlinghurst
sessions last week is regularly employed by
corporations and private employers against
embezzling employes, and will only be sup-
pressed by Judges keeping their eyes open and
boldly refusing to act as accomplices in the
scheme. “It savours of persecution,” said
Judge Coffey, “ when a man who has em-
bezzled a large sum of money is brought before
a magistrate on a charge of embezzling a por-
tion of the general deficiency, and after he has
been punished on that charge to proceed against
him on a charge of embezzling another portion
of the general deficiency.” In several cases
the conduct of prosecutors has amounted
to the compounding of felonies. When a clerk
embezzles £lOO, for instance, his employer
threatens to have him prosecuted for £2O.
When the embezzler’s friends rake up £2O and
hand it over, the employer goes for another
£2O, and so on. And over and over the Courts
have lent a blind sanction to such proceedings.

eexandra “Robbery Under Arms.”
somewhat attenuated comedy skeleton
is “ Mrs. Ponderburv’s Past,” which owes a good
deal more to the Sydney Criterion co. than to its
adapter, Burnand the man who omits the good
things from London Punch at a large annual
salary the noticeably weak part about “Mrs.
1 onderbury is that the lady hasn’t any Past
worth mentioning. She is a fierce boss-female
with a miserably crumpled husband, and the first
act finds this crushed he-worm locked up in a
room under orders to write out 200 charity cir-
culars, as a punishment for being found possessed
of a comic song with an undress female on the
cover. He is harried in various ways through
three acts, just as he has been harried for the pre-
vious 20 years, but the real horror of his existence
comes in when he has to tell the story—which he
has been telling more or less all his married life—
°f how Mrs. Ponderbury, in her youthful days,
routed with a fruit-knife and great slaughter a
wicked male person who climbed up the balcony.
The fruit-knife with which she did the deed hangs
in the drawing-room, and Ponderbury is periodi-
cally stood up in a pained attitude to explain the
matter and illustrate it with appropriate convul-
sions. The knife is the secret of the lady’s
power; her slave feels it is no good resisting a
woman like that. But at the very lowest ebb of
his fortunes, when he has been found in a room
along with an actress dressed mainly in long hair
and a wrapper, and has been discovered doing a
song and dance with the same person, and has
vainly tried to suicide by rushing through a two-
storey window, and has been sentenced to two
years’ confinement and continuous jaw in an un-
lettable house on a sand-hill, relief comes unex-
pectedly. A nautical person irrupts' rather awk-
wardly into the story and explains that he was
the man who climbed the balcony 20 years ago.
Also, he wasn’t routed with the fruit-knife, but
hugged Mrs. Ponderbury considerably, and found
her an ordinary weak woman who was quite wil-
ling* to hug’ back. Then it dawns on Ponderbury
that the woman he has grovelled to so long is only
a bogey after all, and he revolts, and dashes the
fruit-knife on the floor, and confiscates the key of
the front door, and sentences his broken-down
tyrant to write out charitable circulars herself till
further orders, but lets her out under the First
Offenders Act on the understanding that she
thereafter goes reverently on all fours and is
always ready on the carpet to be stepped upon at
the shortest notice. There is so very little Past
about the affair, however, that the lady’s utter
downfall doesn’t look reasonable. Her natural
policy would be to suppress the insurrection and
make Ponderbury write out 2000 circulars, but in-
stead of adopting this course she goes to pieces like
a house built of eggs.
* * * *
The play hangs almost wholly on Mr. and
Mrs. Brough as the two Ponderburys. Brough’s
male Ponderbury is one of the best things that
accomplished artist has done. Mrs. Brough has
comparatively few opportunities, her part being
mostly glare and monotonous ferocity, but she
makes the she-Ponderbury about as good as she
reasonably could be. The only other person of
note is a giddy music-hall lady who shows a great
amount of the upper part of herself at various
cnse.s. Miss Temple does this character in a
cheerful, off-hand fashion, and the upper part of
her is plump and white and altogether pic-
turesque. The rest are mostly outlines. The
pretty Miss Hardy has little to do, and Miss
litheradge still less. Five other people do hardly
anything but drift across the stage, and a mildly
unsatisfactory painter, who stays longer than the
rest, wouldn’t be missed if he drifted likewise.
An individual like a terrified curate looks at one
time as if he was going to do something, but
thinks better of it. If arrangements could be
made for him to really do the thing which he once
or twice looks as if he was going to attempt it
might possibly strengthen the play somewhat.
** * *
That old English comedy, “The Rivals,”
was staged at Sydney Lyceum last Saturday
night, in the presence of an unusually attenuated
audience. Possibly Sydney rushes after the
lady rather than the gentleman variety of
star, and Miss Elliot’s large, florid, copious
style of beauty and rather commonplace acting
don t appeal to it. Possibly it doesn’t care
for the old English style of comedy. Possibly it
thinks that old English comedy and a bran-new
Amurnkin accent don’t go well together. Still
except as regards Goodwin himself, the accent has
been so suppressed as to be hardly perceptible, and
though that gentleman’s voice is the modern voice
of Broadway, N’York, attired in the tight pants
and steeple-hat of England in the last century or
some other part of the indefinite past, still his Bob
Acres is an eminently successful character all
through. The only drawback worth mentioning
is that Nat Goodwin has the misfortune to look
not old by effluxion of time so much as born
°ld but, despite this and his accent, the gorgeous
coat and the tight pants, and the conical hat and
the part generally sit well upon him. Clarence
Handyside makes a very fine and rampant and
inflamed Anthony Absolute ; and Miss Gertrude
Elliots Lucy is nearly perfect. Sister Gertrude
has large, eloquent eyes and an expressionful
countenance, and a cheerful way of dancing
through her part which is very charming ; also
she seems to have a lot of artistic ability—at all
events everything she has yet done in Sydney has
been done remarkably well. There is room, also,
to say many kind things about the Malaprop of
Miss Estelle Mortimer, and the usually rather
gradual and heavy-going beauty-actress shows a
little more than her ordinary animation.
• * *
u Altogether the coldness with which the
Rivals’ was received isn’t wholly justifiable,
the fun of that ancient comedy was never very
vigorous according to modern ideas, and in the
new version a play that was formerly too long has
been cut down till it is quite shore enough, if not
a little too short. It belongs to an age when the
English people took their mirth even more
seriously than they do now, and if it was a per-
fectly new production it would probably fall some-
what flat. But it is an interesting relic of the
past, and the last century play in general gives an
opportunity for woman— whose legs are so much
criticised in tights— to observe that not one man
m a hundred dozen has extremities which look
even moderately well in the close-fitting togs of a
century ago, and to reflect that he is a shambling
annual who wears loose pants to disguise the
shapeless knobbiness of his knees and the way his
ankles go askew. This reflection doesn’t apply
specially to the Goodwin co., which stands in-
spection much better than usual, but is intended
as an anathema on the legs of male humanity all
round. Also, in an irrelevant sort of way it may
be remarked just here that “ A Gilded Fool” will
be staged on Saturday.

The new management at Her Majesty’s put
up as its second effort, “A Trip to Chicago,” and
the best thing that could happen to that sad pro-
dnotion is that it should be buried in oblivion and
nothing more said about it. The “Trip” is a
second edition of the Widow O’Brien—the Widow
grown older and more vulgar, and dressed in
glaring green bloomers, and arriving at noplace
m particular in a steam motor, and making old,
old jokes in the same old brogue, and riding a
horse-race in her green bloomers with her red hair
streaming behind her. The first act consists
mainly in the widow’s son-in-law coming home
drunk and throwing books and derision at her in
her night-dress, and even the people who laughed
when that lady appeared in her night-dress on
