Mr. Walter Barnett (of Falk and Co ) and
Mr. Westmacott allege that they are running
i-™ e authorised cinematographe in Austra
lici at 237 Pitt-street, Sydney.
* * • *
The principle of the Cinematographe now
having so marvellous and well - deserved a run
is exposing lin. by fin. photographic films at
the rate of 45 per second to a 2000-candle-
power electric light, behind a lens, thus pro-
joolnig each picture life-size upon a 10ft. bv
12ft. screen. It is a great step ahead of the
Hmetoscope, and reproduces with the colorlessness
of photography the movements and details of any
phase of life. Two Frenchmen, the brothers
August and Louis Lumiere, manufacturers of
photographic materials, invented this machine
after considerable expenditure of time and money
Like every other novelty, it has been harassed bv
more or less successful imitators. In London it
was speedily opposed by some 20 imitations but
these seem to have gradually lost public support.
London, Paris, Manchester, Birmingham, like
Australia, are understood to have each but one
Lumiere Cmematographe. The London one, at
the Empire Theatre, is credited with paying the
inventors £4O royalty every time it shows, and
Lumieres’ world’s total of 23 machines are esti-
mated to return £l5OOO royalty monthly. The
machine will be managed in Australia on business
lines, and will shortly add local subjects to its
stock. The Cmematographe seems likely to prove
the very best show-speculation ever brought to
Australia. By the way, Mr. Barnett asserts that
Anshutz, of Berlin, and not Edison, is the in-
ventor of the Kinetoscope, and he will give Sydney
Hospital £lOO on this statement being proved in-
correct.
** * *
Alfred Dampier returned to Melbourne
Alexandra on Saturday, and the crowd in front
roared at him with all the enthusiasm of former
times. Also, the tumult was repeated after every
act, therefore it may be taken for granted that
Dampier and Co. have come to stay for a reason-
able period. “ Robbery Under Arms,” regarded
as a play, seems none the better for reconstruction,
but, in the matter of appealing to the lawless
instincts of the Alexandra gods, it is stronger than
before. Boldrewood’s novel teaches the beautifnl
lesson that it is safer to be a policeman than to go
cattle-stealing, and that the wages of sticking-up
the mail coach is tribulation, whereas the riotous
drama under notice incites to felony and heaps
contumely on the foorce. The fact that gallant
Captain Dampier doesn’t rob women is palmed off
upon the gallery as a virtue, but, considering that
the principal spinster in the plot is always on the
verge of shooting a police-inspector in the execution
of his duty, the Captain’s peculiarity seems open
to argument, anyway. Miss Lily Dampier is in
every way a more formidable heroine than she
used to be, and her composed manner of pointing
a new revolver at the Law contrasts favorably
with the scared deportment of sister Rose. The
show otherwise has not gained in weight. Alfred
Dampier s fine voice rings out as cheerfully as
ever, and the solemn tones of Edmund Holloway
have not grown less, likewise Lachlan M‘Gowan
makes his meaning clear, but several new people
in old familiar parts will need to speak up louder
as the season advances. “ Robbery Under
Arms,” which is well supplied with Australian
bush landscape, promises to run until consider
ably further notice.

“ Tommy Atkins ” thrives fairly at Mel-
bourne Royal. British valor is rather a drug on
the market just now, neither does the hoisting of
a Union Jack, which is apt to get stuck half-way
up the pole, stir the multitude to their vitals,
nevertheless, “ Tommy Atkins ” will continue for
wee k or two. The plot has several points
of difference from other yarns of its class, and the
depraved organist who revenges himself upon his
false sweetheart by making her best soldier drunk
every evening of his honeymoon is a new kind of
reflection on the blessed church.

Dear Bbllrtjv,— Your correspondent “Brigham
Brown’s ” criticism on the Revd. Chas. Clark as a lecturer
appears to have raised the ire of at least one occa-
sional contributor. As one who also went to hear
“ the Prince of Lecturers,” expecting much, and who
was wofully disappointed, I should like to say
wry gently and very confidentially (for fear of
being called “a cracked mirror of epileptic thought”)
that. I consider “Brigham Brown” deserves thanks for
having “ bell’d the cat.” The fact of the matter is that
given a clever high priest the universal worship of a
fetish is a natural result. Chas, Clark is the fetish. The
astute and insistent business-manager is the Hierophant.
Keep on telling people that a thing is very good indeed
and they will believe it implicitly. Clark has “ one of tht
marvellously accurate memories of the world.” That and
not much more. It enables him to declaim for two solid
hours without losing a word. But it is declamation which
moves the hearts of his listeners not one throb quicker,
dims no eye, brings no lump into the throat-at least his
even, passionless recital of Dickens’s “Carol” brought
none into mine. Clark at his best is always a preacher,
and the platform is his pulpit. Memory"! Yes, but if
mnemonics are synonymous with a “fine interpretation,"
then Bidder, the boy who could remember and reel o’f
five pages of figures, must have made even arithmetic
seem poetical.—B.E.

An interesting letter from “The Trio” .--Dear Bulle-
tin,—About street busking. As it is pretty well known
by now that we re among- the latest additions to Sydney
stieet players, it may satisfy curiosity to learn some of
our experiences. That there is some curiosity we have no
cloubt—‘Ns the kid’s carryin’ ’em through,” “What’s
that blessed thing she’s playing ? ’’—such remarks, and
many more, we hear on every corner. So anxious are the
youngsters to get a peep at the takings that our hat
often conies grievously near a spill. Even the bank-teller,
as we take in our earnings, follows our movements with
open curiosity. Well, the result has been good-beyond
all expectations. We entered the business, not with
great hopes, but from dire necessity, the result of a long
sickness and a crippling of funds. I have read in your
columns of street musicians turning over £6OO in the 12
months. I can now reckon it quite possible, when during
our three weeks' trial we have averaged 30s. a day of
about hours-and we are new to it! Except when
working at night, we have started at 12 and knocked off
at 5.30, when the rush of homeward people spoils a crowd.
Our biggest day has been a Saturday—l 2 to 6 p.m., 435. 6d
On a single stand in the marine end of George-st., anight
or two back, we gathered 17s. odd in less than 15 minutes.
Night playing is decidedly best, but the most risky.
The ponce are more numerous, and have less to occupy
then time. As a rule, both in town and country, under
various conditions, I have found the police a fine, broad-
minded set of men, but a devil in the force is a devil, and
street-players need not go far to find one a man who
needlessly harasses itinerant musicians and the miser-
ables who “do the street” for a living. Among our
most generous patrons have always been the Jews’
and let me tell you that the Chinese don t slink
away when the hat comes round— oft-times they
have put the only silver coin in. This means of liv-
ing is wearying, risky, and very monotonous. Our per-
sonal friends compliment us bn our nerve. Anyhow, our
object is now gained. We have put away a solid £2O out
weeks m the streets,” and 1 trust they will see
unfihvw J- ,Ut doesn’t the result point, to a peculiar
unhtimss of things when, without expense, we can make
moie dollars than half a dozen members of the many com-
t oo ha ! ldful . s of people in the halls and
theaties of Sydney? There is a vast crowd which no pre-
text whatei er will entice under a roof, yet who give freely
as tne spirit moves.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The little Temperance and General Life Assur-
ance Society has just finished its five-yearly investigation.
The profit shown-i.e., the surplus which the book-value
of the assets shows over the liabilities-—is A 27,850, but of
this it has been decided to write off £23,485 to cover de-
preciation of assets, so the balance for bonus purposes is
onlv £4365. Consequently the bonus will be a very
humble affair. Still the Temperance and General has un-
doubtedly done the only right thing under the circum-
stances, and if the fact can only be driven into the public
comprehension that security is the first consideration and
the bonus only a secondary affair, its action should boom
the business considerably." The writing-off represents 10
per cent, of the entire assets (£238,000), but the losses are
presumably confined to the mortgages and freehold pro-
perties, and in that case it means that nearly 12 per cent,
has been written off the book-values. As far as The Bul-
letin knows, this is the biggest writing-down on record
among Australian insurance societies, except that which
a Victorian office did when it set itself, some months
a°-o, to bring all its assets down to bed-rock values. And
the figures raise the old uneasiness about the state of a
lot of insurance business in this country. The bulk
of the funds of Australian offices are invested in free-
hold propertv and mortgages on ditto. The drop in
freehold values in N.S.W., Victoria and Maoriland, and in
a less degree in other provinces, has been stupendous,
and a return to the old values is not a thing to be
relied on too confidently. And when the very large
percentages written off by three offices are compared
with the comparatively small provision for depreciation
made by some other offices, it raises a suspicion that some
insurance assets—like a lot of bank assets—can’t bear too
much inspection. Nothing short of a rigid and regular
State audit can set the doubt at rest.
One thing is certain : The reserve-funds of the
biggest insurance societies and those of the biggest banks
are very different affairs. The Commercial Banking Co.
of Sydney has a reserve of £1,000,000 to cover possible
loss on £12,000,000 of property ; the A.M.P. Society has a
reserve of £424,000 to Cover possible loss on £13,000,000.
The policy of distributing practically all the profit in
bonuses as they went along has.been the weak point
among Australian insurance offices. And the reason why
The Bulletin calls aloud for State supervision is that the
insurance office can become more and more rotten for a
century without detection, provided it keeps setting down
its depreciated assets at book-value, and it gets enough
premiums each year to meet its outgoings so that it
hasn’t actually to stop payment, and none of its confi-
dential officers talk in their sleep and thus let the matter
out.
The Temperance and General now fixes its as-
sumed future rate of interest at 34 per cent. Several
other offices, notably the A.M.P. and Mutual Life Associa-
tion, are now down to this rate or just getting to it, and
they will probably all get there shortly. This reduction
from the old 4-or-more per cent, will make bonuses smaller
in the future ; but it is inevitable, as things now are, if
there is to be any decent security for policy-holders, and
the sooner it is generally arrived at the better.
* * • *
For the 12 months ended Sept. 30, the Westra-
lian revenue was £2,220,000 against £1,235,000 for the
previous year, an increase of nearly a million. Westralia,
on the strength of its giddy boom, is now passing a bill to
authorise the borrowing of another £3,500,000, and a
further loan of £3,500,000 is expected shortly afterwards,
as the Government has about £7,000,000 of works in
prospect. Even without this, however, the loan now
actually in view will make Westralia, in proportion to
population, very nearly the most indebted country on
earth, only Queensland, S. A. and Maoriland being ahead
of it, and it will have got in five years to a point which
the other provinces arrived at in about twenty. If the
second £3,500,000 loan proves a reality, then Westralia
should have the biggest debt per head in this world.
What will ultimately become of the riotous province is the
most interesting question of the day. If the gold-boom
should burst, the biggest smash Australia has yet seen is
nothing to what will happen.
* * * *
The Maoriland Midland Railway Co., that
beautiful embodiment of “private enterprise” is again
howling for State assistance. The Midland Co. agreed, in
the first place, to build a railway provided the State gave
it an immense subsidy in land. It collected a lot of the
subsidy, but it only built a small part of the line, and
then it collapsed, the whole trouble being that the share-
holders refused flatly to put up the required capital, and
their credit was so bad that they couldn’t borrow enough
for their purpose. So they stopped work, and their con-
tract expired, and the line being unfinished, with no pro-
spect of anything further being done, the Government
took possession as provided in the agreement. Now the
Midland Co. suggests (1) that the line should be handed
back; (2) that the Government should give it another
long contract so that it may have time to think seriously
over the job again ; (3) that the State should lend it
£600,000 worth of 3 per cent, debentures, so that it can
sell them in order to raise the money to build its own
railway as it won’t risk its own money on the job. The
State could about build the line itself for this sum, and
the practically busted co. which asks the nation to put up
£600,000, never had more than £250,000 of its own money
at risk, and has got a, lot of that back in dividends which
were paid out of capital. Yet it calls itself private enter-
prise. These provinces are about full up of .1. Bull’s
private enterprise, and Maoriland in particular isn’t tak-
ing any more Midland Railway at present.
* * * *
It is stated that the Maoriland Midland Railway
Co. for 10 years, paid its manager £2600 a year salary, and
from 5 to 10 per cent, on contracts, which made up his
screw to an average of £9600. Think of it; £9600 a year
for being nominal boss of one small railway, not one-third
built. Yet they talk of the economy of private, as com-
pared to State, enterprise—and the State in these pro-
vinces gets a good manager for 10 times as much railway
at one-third the money.
*** * *
Still another “private enterprise” has started
to howl piteously on the door-mat of this country for
State assistance. The Mildura Irrigation Co.’s British
debenture-holders are the trouble this time, and what
they want is that the Vic. Government shall not resume
the unearned portion of the Chaffe.vs’ grant because it is
“part of their (the debenture-holders’) security.” The
Chaffeys received their grant conditionally on their exe-
cuting certain irrigation works; if the works were
executed the grant became absolute —not other-
wise. The English debenture-holders chose to lend
them money on the security of the land Chaf-
feys’ co. was to get if it fulfilled the necessary
conditions, and charged high interest because the security
was shaky. The conditions were not fulfilled, and the
Vic. Government refuses to see that it should part with
the remaining land without receiving the agreed-upon
consideration merely to pay the Mildura co.’s bad debts.
And now a shriek is going up that the Government’s re-
fusal to pay these debts by handing over a vast area of
land is “unfair” and “ will be commented on most un-
favorably in Great Britain.” Probably it will. Great
Britain always comments unfavorably unless it gets
everything it wants, and it wants such a lot.
* * ♦ *
The Freehold Assets Realisation Co. (Melb.),
which took over the difficulties of the-old Freehold Invest-
ment and Banking Co., is making good progress on a
novel plan. Whenever a decent sum in cash is accumu-
lated, it calls for tenders among holders of the old com-
pany’s deposit-receipts, and buys up those that are offered
cheapest. The last event of this kind resulted in the ex-
tinction of over £27,000 worth of liabilities for about
£5OOO. It is an interesting gamble ; the man who holds
on to the last may get 20s. in the £, or he may get j.d. or
less, just as things turn out.
* * • •
Five Dock, the most bankrupt municipality-
round Sydney, has had the furniture of the Council
Chambers sold up, the overdraft stopped, the street gas-
supply shut off (leaving the district in darkness), and a
swarm of writs issued against it by miscellaneous credi-
tors. Now it is waiting to see what will happen next.
Under the Municipalities Act no Council can borrow to
the extent of more than five years’ revenue, but according
to Cbghlan’s statistics Five Dock has a revenue of £959
and a debt of about £6OOO. The chief use of Five Dock is
as an awful example of the results of too much local
government.
A howl goes up in Tasmania for a private rail-
way from Hobart toZeelian. “It must be done by pri-
vate enterprise,” says a local paper. “ The Government
haven’t got, nor never will have, the money to accom-
plish the task.” Possibly, but if the railway is built by a
private co. it will be a foreign co. that will do it, and as
Tasmania must find the dividends it will owe the money
just as much as if the Government borrowed it and built
the line in the usual way. The difference would be that
the Government could get the money at 3J4 percent,
while the co. will probably raise it by issuing debentures
at 4 or 4>4 or 5 per cent., so the country will have to find
more money for the private enterprise than it would for
the public one.
* * * *
Sundry British capitalists have offered to go in
largely for the wine industry in Victoria and invest
£250,000 therein, provided Vic. Government guarantees 3
per cent, interest on their money for 14 years—£7soo a
year £105,000 in all. This State-subsidised concern
would, of course, be described as private enterprise. The
State would stand the loss up to 3 per cent., but it would
get none of the profit, if there was one, and there would
apparently be no guarantee that the co. wouldn’t flicker
down to nothing in its second year, and the Government
be left to subsidise a practically dead institution for 12
years thereafter. Victoria is now considering whether, if
it is going to risk £105,000 to prop up anybody’s private
w'ine business, it might not as well subsidise local in-
vestors with the money. A guarantee of 3 per cent, for
14 years would probably cause all the old boomers of
Melb. to rush together and float a big company inside a
week. And the local men know something about wine ;
they would spend the State subsidy locally ; they would
be more within reach of control; the Treasurer w'buld get
part of the subsidy back in probate duty when they died,
and they wouldn’t fill the British papers with curses about
how they were shamefully' ill-treated by a beastly colonial
Government—after the fashion of the Midland Railway
gang and the Mildura debenture-holders.
* * * *
Company-floaters and other financial wind-
raisers in N.S.W. will want to keep a steady eye on
Income-tax Spiller. Properties have, it is understood,
recently changed hands on representations as to last
year’s income largely in excess of the income reported to
the Taxing Department. Whatever the value of caveat
emptor in the purchasers, that piece of persiflage serves
merely as a whet to the appetite of the Income-tax Com-
missioners.
* » * *
N.S.W. Income-tax Commissioners supply a
fine example of official impudence. After having received
returns of trust-moneys paid, and three or four days
before tax-payment was due, they served apparently
everybody who received money under a trust with a
demand to pay tax on the amount. Now, unless the
payee was in receipt of over £2OO a year in trust-
moneys, or in trust-moneys and other moneys com-
bined, no income-tax was legally due. But, with splen-
did recklessness, the Commissioners “hanged” the Par-
liamentary enactment, and forwarded their please-pay-ups
to possibly thousands of widows, wards, and orphans, whose
sole income was derived from a trust, and ranged from
£lO a year to anything under the exemption-limit of
£2OO. Seemingly, not a word was said about exemption ;
only three or four days’ notice was given ; and the demand
to pay was peremptory. The consequence was that a
large number of trustees and w'ards did pay up—quite
illegally.
One man read up the law, and went to seek an
explanation. _ “ Here is my sister drawing £4O a year
from a trust ; it is her sole income ; why do you tax her?”
“ Oh,” said the Commissioner, cheerfully, “ we thought
she might have some other income.” “ But you have
already asked people with incomes over the exemption-
limit to furnish returns ; she has not done so ; therefore,
you know her income is not over the exemption-limit.”
The Commissioner cheerfully waived this aspect of the
case. “Then you have actually collected hundreds of
small sums illegally, at three or four days’ notice, from
people too ignorant or too hurried to protest.” Tile
Commissioners were understood to cheerfully admit that
wajS so. “But,” the expostulator was told, “if it turns
out that there was no tax due you can get it back at the
Treasury.” “When?” “Oh, in two or three months
time.”
Isn’t it amazing ? The Bulletin exhorts
trustees who have responded to this illegal demand to
clamor at the Treasury for a refund —it is apparently the
only thing they can do—and to make the Treasury stamp
the receipt which it will oblige them to give. And it calls
Parliamentary attention to the fact that, by making
absurd and illegal demands, the Commissioners are really
doing their best to upset the scheme of direct-taxation
altogether.
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin, —My life-insurance history
apropos of recent “ wild-cat” comments. In 1877, at the
age of 37, I insured my life for £5OO ; premium £l7 per
annum, payable half-yearly. For 18)4 years I never failed
to pay the premium ; paid in all £314 10s. In Dec., ’B2, I
borrowed £4O on policy ;in May, ’87,1 borrowed a further
£l6. I paid no interest on these loans, thinking it would
be met by bonus additions. The interest on that £56 has
caused my policy to lapse, and I received no notice for
months afterwards. Thus, by borrowing £56 I have lost
£258 (to say nothing of interest). In July, ’93, when
thought to be dying, I got a letter from the society show-
ing the policy to be then worth £432. Could a State life-
department treat its members so?—J.H.N. [Will the
writer send along verificatory papers under registered
cover, please ? —Ed. B.]

* * * *
After the passing of the Bill to amend the Insol-
vency Court now before Parliament, Vic. Govt, intends, it
is reported, to bring in a Bill to place the county court
Judges, who sit in the Insolvency Court, on the same
footing as the Supreme Court Judges, as regards being
irremovable except by a vote of both Houses. This is
“ locking the stable-door,” &c. Still it is just as well to
lock it.
• * * *
The latest little unpleasantness between the
Vic. Solicitor-General and Judge Molesworth has recalled
the attention of Parliament to the absurd position of
affairs. As matters stand, the Judge, in the event of his
missing a day’s work through illness, is liable to get fined
a whole or half-day’s salary at the discretion of a man who
may be practising before him as a barrister next morning.
* * * *
Melbourne telephone service, sticking to the
original expensive rate, has only 1750 subscribers on the
roll, 250 less than Sydney numbered previous to the re-
duction of the rate to a “ fiver,” an experiment that
promptly doubled its telephone circulation. P.M.G.
Duffy, therefore, is being vehemently implored to try a
reduced rate in Melbourne, but that superior personage
merely blinks behind his pince-nez. Candidly speaking,
the local telephone department is not in a fit state to deal
with an increase of business. It has only lately begun to
recover from a long razzle-dazzle, and the hat which it
jams with difficulty upon its fevered brow still carries the
toe-mark of the yellow-haired barmaid who used to kick
so high in the high old time.
* * * *
Again the N.S.W. Assembly has been barking
up the wrong tree in the debate on the Patents Amend-
ment Bill. It is all on account of the cyanide patentees.
As a fact, the real object of the bill is to enable the
inventor of the (Ashcroft) sulphide process to so amend
his specifications as to hold the rights he lias so fairly
earned. Flaws in the specifications were discovered in
America long ago, and more than one journal pointed out
that the law of N.S.W. did not provide for the required
alterations. The cyanide patent, as well as others, may
benefit by the bill incidentally.
* * * *
Silas Fischer, who suicided at a friend’s office
the other day, was erstwhile the swell financial “under-
taker” of Melbourne. He was of Hebraic appearance,
with a lustrous black beard and bald head. During the
boom he was the “ ghost ” of a well-advertised coterie of
big operators, and managed the commercial future of
many busted boomers. But when all the corpses of the
boom had been buried, Fischer found his occupation gone,
and so took a prussic-acid ticket to the Never-Never.
* * * *
An important day’s business in the City of
Melb. Bank trial gets no more than nine inches of report
in the Age. Three different conclusions may be drawn
from this circumstance. The most harmless conclusion is
that the Age supposes its readers to be indifferent to
financial scandals nowadays.
* * * *
The Grand Hotel, Melb., which was more or less
launched by James Munro on a wave of teetotal cant, is
now sailing fairly towards the Black Sea of intoxicating
liquor. A strong body of shareholders, having David
Syme for a backbone, declared last week in favor of con-
verting the coffee-palace into a plain straightforward grog-
shop, where residents may whisky-up to the glory of the
management instead of buying their insobriety by
stealth from an adjacent pub. The reformers propose to
strengthen their property with a native wine-license,
and “ a grocer’s license to sell wine and spirits of any sort
in nob less than pint bottles,” and it is said that a poll
taken on this question would have resulted in the over-
throw of the old snufflebusting policy but for an unfor-
tunate accident to a bundle of “booze” proxies which
came reeling in a few minutes after time for closing the
poll. Anyway, the Grand Hotel in its present form seems
to be doomed. The typical lodger of the near future will
be a red-faced man with nothing less than a pint bottle
to his lips when he feels so disposed, and then the strictly
temperance shareholders will stand afar off and bemoan
the growth of a new and bloated prosperity that insults
their principles and gives them a sinful return for their
investment. Amen.
* * * *
The writer, having for years vainly advo-
cated in this page the establishment in N.S. Wales
of a national smelting works, now wishes every
success to the (private) works going up in the Illa-
warra district under the direction of the capable
John Howell. The luck of the venture very largely
depends on the quantity of various ores purchasable by
assay in N.S.W. Therein lies the difficulty. Throughout
Australia, and especially in N.S.W., there exists a deep-
rooted suspicion that the system means robbery. The
writer never saw' a stamp-mill that gave satisfaction when
it crushed for the public. The gold was always “ left in
the tailings.” Letters received every week at Cl.yde
works show that all ores are thought to contain much
more value than the actual test shows can be extracted.
In America custom-works will have sometimes on their
dumps half-a-million worth of bought ore awaiting treat-
ment. The question is : how long will it take to overcome
this prejudice in Australia ? State works would be
trusted, hence it is that so many lots are sent to be
treated by a rattle-trap battery at the Mint. The Govt,
toy works started at Clyde does not fill a want. What,
for instance, could be done with ten tons of ore containing
gold, with a large percentage of zinc and antimony ? And
this little crushing and concentrating plant is called the
N.S.W. Govt. Metallurgical Works! Mr. Howell may be
relied on to give every reasonable guarantee to his clients.
* * * *
After months of careful experimenting and
manipulating in the face of great difficulties, among them
the departure of most of their best men to the West,
Block 10 will have at length been got to do fair work —
fair, that is, for a concentrating plant, which is a poor
thing at the best, and considering the refractory stuff at
disposal. A pretty fair idea of what is meant by “ the
solution of the sulphide problem,” so far as concentrating
is relied upon as the solution, can be got from an analysis
of Block 10’s figures. The mill, we are told, treated
1347 J tons, assaying in the bulk 20'50z. silver, 20'5 per
cent, lead, and 29 per cent. zinc. That is, approximately,
26,9550 z. silver, 2694 tons lead, and 390$ tons zinc.
The concentrates weighed 316 tons, assaying 32T00z.
silver, 66T per cent, lead, and B‘3 per cent,
zinc. That is, the concentrates contained 10,1440 z.
silver and 208$ tons lead, while the losses were 16,8110 z.
silver and tons lead. There still remained in the
concentrates 25$ tons zinc. To eliminate 365 tons of zinc
contained in 1347J4 tons ore it cost, besides the expense of
the concentrating process, metal (apart from the zinc)
worth £2750. Yet, as said just now, this is very fair
work. So much for the concentrating method : the field
is still open. For even tl.e Ashcroft and Howell pro-
cesses depend, to some extent at least, upon preliminary
concentration. The average of the ore broken through-
out Block 10 is, by the way, very close to the value of the
ore put through the mill during the week referred to in
this par. ; the silver average is a little low'er in the present
faces perhaps.
The British mill is treating about the same
quantity of ore as Block 10 —rather less —but for a soine-
what higher concentrate return. The British is, however,
treating carbonates to a large extent. The lead contents
are about the same as Block 10’s, but the silver contents
are 4 per cent, lower. With regard to these concentrate
returns, however, it oug'ht to be remembered that unless
they give the percentage of zinc contained they are
worthless, and often misleading. It is easy' enough to get
good silver and lead percentages if no special care is taken
to eliminate the zinc. But the smelters make a serious
deduction per unit of zinc above about 6 per cent.
Block 14 continues to open out on medium-grade
milling sulphide ore remarkably well.
* * * *
Broken Hill people didn’t believe it could be
that Col. Morgan who formerly managed the British
8.H., to whom the nasty disclosures in connection with
the allegedly-spurious Buchan silver and lead mine,
Gippsland, referred. But when they read the manager's
description of how the gallant colonel toyed with a
revolver, their doubts ended absolutely. Morgan was
sent out by the English co. to manage the British just
before the big strike; and very early in the trouble he
was attacked by boss Sleath. The colonel promptly sent
down a challenge to fight, and begged Sleath to choose
the weapons without delay'. Sleath’s reply was a retrac-
tation more complete than he had ever made before. But
the colonel was voted a good sort, and habitually regaled
with whisky the picket sergeants-as they did their rounds
in the cold nights.
» * * *
Father Curran returns from touring White
Cliffs (N.S.W.) field filled with the belief that the opal-
bearing country is much more extensive than the area
now being prospected. Since the English co. got to work
on the field the demand for the gem has fallen away, as
fewer buyers are now on the spot, the co. sending all its
stuff (comprising more than half of the total product of
the field) direct to the European markets. The small
claimholders are consequently combining to sen 4 to
Europe an agent in their interests, and to that end are
seeking the co-operation of the Queensland opal-getters—
so far, however, without success.
* * * *
Jack Roberts, the capable mining representa-
tive of Sydney' D.T., has let some daylight into the Yul-
gilbar goldfield, N.S.W. He proves the rush unwarranted.
Disappointed diggers are returning ; the biggest nugget
found on the field, which, according to the shipping
reporter of the Stab, would outrival Ballarat and make
Coolgardie a byword and a reproach, was 71dwts. The
alleged mountain of gold is a massive ridge of granite,
tough as adamant. The veins are not only small, but are
embedded in granite, without a “dig” of any kind.
Numbers of people have been grossly misled by this
“ marine-mining reporter,” and implored Roberts not to
delay in revealing the true state of things, hence his first
article was wired down.

“ ’Tis never too late,” &c. The Sabbath tipster :
“ We are disgusted with the sulphide affair, and you had
better get out of the others.” Other people are dis-
gusted with something else, and it is easy to say “ get
out.” Again : “We wish to goodness you had taken our
advice and left mining alone. Your best plan now is to
get out of everything of the kind. It is very seldom that
ladies do any good when they commence to deal.” A
little more repentance and The*Bulletin will regard the
Sabbath tipster as well and truly converted.

The poor old London market is threatened with
another mine from Stuart Town, the Perseverance, a good
name for any of Bull’s mines. Up to date The Bulletin
has never heard much of the mine, but it is satisfactory
to learn that each of the directors is to get £l5O a year
and the chairman £2OO for looking after it in London.
Capital £112,500. Proposed to offer shortly, but if, mean-
while, Bull is made aware of the sick state of another of
his mines on the same field —the Golden Gully—the Per-
severance promoters, however good or bad their show,
will have a time.

* * * *
Lady Shenton, Menzies, W.A., 262 tons,
10950 z. Splendid if this yield can be maintained. That
suspicion exists on every field in the world with the first
few crushings. In W.A. the suspicion is ten times greater,
because from one end of the country to the other it has
been proved that, where one mine lives, a hundred have
a very sudden way of dying. “ Too much white quartz.”
Kalgoorlie may be the great exception, and it has much
hope in the fact that it is not working white quartz.
* * * *
The horse and camel markets have both moved
on from Coolgardie to Kalgoorlie, which marks another
stage in the downfall of the original goldfield metropolis.
Coolgardie and Kalgoorlie promise to be Silverton and
Broken Hill over again, barring the fact that Kalgoorlie
isn’t quite a Broken Hill yet. Silverton is little better
than a memory now, and not a very bright one at that.

W.A. Mines Minister has notified that the
coming Xmas exemptions will begin, as usual, about the
middle of Dec. As usual, too, no doubt they will be ex-
tended from five to seven or eight weeks.
* * * *
East Mexican mine, C. Towers, adjoins the
Brilliant on the western side, and has been worked for
the last 10 years by an Adelaide co. without a crushing,
much less a div. Sank a main shaft deep enough to strike
the Brilliant reef, but didn’t—came on a fault. The
Brilliant has worked two levels up to its boundary, and
the Mexican is now working from No. 6 Brilliant level
where the stone is about 2ft. and worth from 1 to 2oz.
No. 7 level in the boss mine has fully 4ft. of good stone on
the boundary. Hence it will be seen that the E.M. mine
is worth watching.

Ex-constable H. A. Slater, of Sydney, is
reaching, as it were, for the scalp of Capt. Slocum,
the adventurous Yankee mariner, who is single-
handedly sailing round the world and has just
arrived in Newcastle. Slater claims to have for-
merly sailed with Slocum and to be in a position
to show that the captain is hardly entitled to the
public welcome which it is proposed he should be
accorded at Sydney. Slater has submitted a sum-
mary of his argument to The Bulletin, which,
however, proposes to let plaintiff and defendant
fight the matter out themselves.

COOLALTA
CAMEO
The State funeral to P. P. Gillen, the late
S.A. Lands Minister, was far more decorous than
most such functions are, for everyone attending
was sincerely sorry for his death. At Clare, how-
ever, by some mistake the local residents appro-
priated the drags set apart for Ministers, legis-
lators, and heads of departments, and so those
functionaries had to travel on foot behind the
hearse for a distance of five miles to the grave at
Seven Hills college cemetery. When Education
Minister Bews was buried six years ago, the
travellers by special train were supplied with in-
toxicants on the journey at the State expense, and
the result was that nearly a dozen of the pas-
sengers became obtrusively drunk. In the case of
Mr. Gillen, light refreshments were provided at
Clare, but only tea and coffee were paid for by
Govt. Mr. Gillen, like every one of his colleagues,
except the Chief Secretary, was a teetotaller.

The primitive township of Kalgoorlie tried
recently to imprison a lady for a month, and failed

SACRED GERMS.
RESCUE SISTER : “ What! keeping the Court Bible from the flies? ”
CONSTABLE: “No, miss; I'm a-keepin ’ the flies from the Court Bible. It
ain’t good for ’em.”
The lady, who was in trouble over the sly-grog
business, was too ill to be run down through the
glare and the dust-storms to Perth. The local
authorities hadn’t nerve enough to chain her to a
tree for the term of her sentence, so they sent her
home, and a policeman lived in the house and kept
her in nominal custody till her term expired. And
now people want to know if it is the right thing
in a civilised country to sentence any woman to
have a strange man in the house for a month ?

It is healthy just now to remind the Aus-

* * * *
The other night, some unknown Goth
smashed the figure of the Venus di Medici, in Syd-
ney Botanic Gardens. Many years ago, a similar
thing happened in Melb., at the hands of some
“ bloods ” out for the night. One was the son of a
well-known legal official and associate to a Judge,
himself a patron of art; another was a Govt,
official and brother to an eminent barrister. They
had been “ indulging ” over-night, and early in
the morning visited Fitzroy Gardens, and with
their walking-sticks broke several statues. The
offenders were recognised, and the Judge desired
his associate never to see him again. The elder
culprit had influence enough to square matters, and
managed to keep his billet.
* * * *
The latest cruelty item arose out of a mid
night spree at Wollongong (N.S.W.), where two
youths have pleaded guilty to maliciously injuring
horses. A fore-leg of one of the animals was
almost cut through just above the hoof ; a large
splinter of wood was stuck in the poor brute’s
forehead, and a tin-dish was tied to its tail.
Another horse had a piece of barbed-wire twisted
round its body with a post-rail attached to the
end of it. Every time the animal moved the
points of the wire cut into its body. The inter-
esting pair were committed for trial.
Next week several more young women leave
N.S.W. as missionaries to the heathen in China.

There was a rather singular scene at Suf-
field’s trial during the cross-examination of the
prosecutrix by barrister Edmunds, representing
Suffield. It was alleged that to a fellow-servant
prosecutrix had described her assailant as wearing
“a rather hairy coat, like Master ’s.”
(“ Master ” was a young gentleman who had
a frieze overcoat. Suffield had no such coat.) She
was asked hadn’t she said “rough hairy coat.”
This she admitted. “And Tike Master ’s’?’
This she denied.
Then counsel asked that she should be allowed
to feel Suffield’s coat, which he was wearing in
the dock, and say whether it was like the one
worn by her assailant. Suffield said he would
save witness the trouble of coming down to
the dock, and took off his coat and handed it up.
It was of fairly rough twill. She felt it and said,
“Yes, it was like that,” and when the barrister
asked “ Do you call that a rough or a hairy coat?”
Judge Windeyer interposed “You had better be
careful.” “Surely, your honor,” said counsel,
VEGETABLES,
“ Does your wi give you enough vegetables?”
“ She gives me beans when I come home at two in
the morning. ”
“I have not said or done anything that calls for
reproof.” “No, certainly not,” said the Judge,
“ but please remember that a woman’s sense of
texture is very different from a man’s.” Then Mr.
Edmunds said he would ask the jury to say if it
could be called a rough coat. And the jury appar-
ently thought it could.

Judge Casey (Vic.) says that wives who are
unsympathetic deserve little consideration from
the Court. He hints, in effect, that he has but a
slight opinion of women who come before him to
complain of their husbands’ cruelty, and have not
to be carried to court in a litter.
In a certain Melb. coffee-palace
a notice appears in each bedroom to
the effect that the management is not
responsible for the safety of boots left
outside bedroom doors at nights. In
a common drinky hotel you can leave
your boots outside with perfect safety,
but in that holy coffee-palace the. only
safe way to get them cleaned is to
leave them in the office the night be-
fore, and get a receipt for them.
Apropos: though you can safely leave
your boots outside in a hotel it doesn’t
necessarily follow that they will be
cleaned. The present writer once went
on a walking tour in the Illawarra dis-
trict, in the course of which he left
his boots out at 13 different
hotels. They were cleaned ex-
actly four times. Twice he
cleaned them himself.
The other seven times
there were no
brushes, so on
three occasions he
wiped them on the
counterpane, and
four times he
washed them in
the basin in his
bedroom, and dried
them vindictively
on the towel.
Hingston tells
how, at the Java hotels,
every bed for lady or gen-
tleman is furnished with a
piece of wood about 6ft. long
called “the wife,” without a
shadow of jocularity. The heat is
insupportable that this is a relief
placed between the wrists and ankles. The first
Javan pub. Hingston struck, the servant girl
asked if he wanted a wife. “Yes.” She then
carefully fetched him the bit of wood.
* * * *
At a recent W.C.T.U. pow-wow :
Mrs. Shearing 1 presented “ the unfermented wine ” re-
port, and stated that the use of unfermented wine for
church purposes is becoming more and more general. The
Bishop of Adelaide had been communicated with on the
subject, and had courteously replied through his chaplain
(the Rev. L. P. Crawfurd), although not promising to in-
troduce unfermented wine into the Anglican communion.
The letter stated :
“ The bishop only received your letter just before he
left for a time in the country. He asked me to acknow-
ledge your letter with many thanks to you for it, but at
the same time he does not see any possibility of introduc-
ing unfermented wine into the service for Holy Com-
munion. He begs at the same time to assure you that he
tries to make the wine as light as possible by mixing
water with it. Again thanking you on behalf of the
bishop.”
O for the humorlessness of His Gaiters of Adelaide!
Where does he draw the line, and why, when once
he does water the wine, should he draw any line
at all ? For the watering-the-wine question is like
the divorce-question : once admit the possibility
of qualifying matrimony with divorce and where
are you going to stop ? You can’t logically stop
anywhere.
* * * *
It’s just this way. The sexes already
mingle at choir-practice; why shouldn’t they be
allowed to mix in sea-bathing?
* * * *
There is no moral about the Yic. bogus-
will-case, which collapsed the other day, save the
old moral that it is only the awkward malefactor
who has accomplices. The man who forges a will
himself may get through all right, provided he
buys the stationery in a town 500 miles away, and
does the forgery in a room with the door shut and
the window secured, and copies the signatures
accurately, and makes sure that both witnesses
were there or thereabouts at the time of the alleged
signing, and that they have both died since, and
he doesn’t leave the draft of the document lying
about. But the man who calls on casual lawyers
asking them to do the job for him is lost. Bor
that matter, the other man is often lost too, for,
as likely as not, the lawyer whom he didn’t ask to
forge the document will roll up and swear, for a
small consideration, that he did. The legal pro-
fession has to swear to nearly anything for a liveli-
hood in these hard times.

At a Vic. police-court, the other day, a
woman was fined 40s. for cruelty to a cow. At the
same court a man was fined 10s. for cruelty to his
wife —grabbing her by the hair and wiping the
floor with her.

+ * * *
The utter fallacy of sentencing men guilty
of abnormal sexual offences to any fixed term, let
alone flogging them, was again shown in Melbourne
last week when a silly-looking youth was sent to
gaol for two years with floggings for indecently
assaulting a little girl. The police-records showed
that this lunatic had already been twice convicted
of exposure, having on each occasion served a year
and once received a 20-lash flogging.
V * * *
In Victoria, the other day, a young man
(A.) was charged with seduction of a girl under
16. Judge Holroyd gave him eight months hard
labor. Last June another young man (B.) was
charged with seduction of another young girl
under 16. Judge Hodges set him free under good-
behaviour recognizances of ,£4O. Where the cir-
cumstances were not exactly similar they were
more favorable to A. To which culprit, then, was
justice done ?
* * * *
The Adelaide medical trouble continues.
Dr. Leith Napier, the imported hospital superin-
tendent, the other day threatened to throw one of
his opponents out of the operating-theatre if a
course of disrespect was persisted in.

Under the Infants’ Life Protection Act in
Victoria householders are liable to a stiff penalty
if the birth of an illegitimate child be unregis-
tered within three days of that occurrence —
in wedlock the time allowed for registration
is 60 days. This compels every householder
who may take in a woman-lodger to make
certain, as a matter of prudence, whether
the latter is married or single, but how it is
to be done the Act doesn’t say. Anyhow,
a Melb. suburban lodging-housekeeper
was fined the other day for failing to
comply with the Act, and, although
he registered within the time demanded
in regard to married women, the law
considered that he should have known
all about the statute. The child
wasn’t his, the woman passed herself
off to him as a grass-widow, and sakl
her husband would be down from
the country in a month or so.
But this didn’t save him. At this
rate down South no lodging-
housekeeper, or coffee-palace,
or hotel boss will, in future,
accept as lodger any single

SPECIAL BATES IFOIR HOTELS.
KINDLY SEND FOR A CIRCULAR.
Neat difficulty raised by Vic. Board of
Examiners for lawyers. Can a person receiving
pav as officer of militia be admitted to the shark
fraternity? On English law, which seems to be
in force in Vic., he cannot. Without deciding the
point, Vic. Full Court recently admitted two
officers—Edwards, of Ballarat, and MTnerney, a
brother of the well-known LL.D.
* * * w
Tasmanian Supreme Court objects to being
made a half-way house for Vic. lawyers seeking
entrance into Westralia. Future applicants must
prove a bona Hdc intention to practise in as-
mania.
A Tasmanian barrister, Picken, was admitted
to the Victorian bar recently. Which slightly
widens the Vic. dog-in-the-manger Act. One
Maoriland barrister has been admitted and one
refused ; South Australia has yet to try ; Queens-
land and N.S.W. have no chance ; and Westralia
would possibly be admitted now, but isn’t likely
to give the Vic. Court a chance to climb down,
and thus enable her own preserves to be swamped
by needy Victorians.
A Vic. barrister, when being admitted the
other day, took affirmations instead of the usual
oaths. This is the first Vic. case on record of a
lawyer doing so, although the profession does not
lack those who, in private, contemn the absurd
practise of oscillatory imprecation.
A Melbourne tailor objects to the coming
half-holiday for employes on the ground that God
commanded man to work six days a week. The
Turks also say that it is the will of Allah they
should kill the Armenians. The Almighty is
acquiring a nice character in one way or another.
* * * *
According to the evidence taken on the
trial of a S. Melb. butcher, charged with exposing
for sale tuberculous beef, the disgusting stuff would
have gone into consumption—“consumption” is
here a good word—but that it was exposed at the
Victoria market, at which an inspector is employed
to condemn diseased meat. Very little food can
stand being traced back to its natural state (re-
marks a Bulletin correspondent). The writer
remembers, when at a township in Canterbury,
Maoriland, some years ago, looking into a shed
where a man was engaged curing bacon; what
spoilt his appetite for pig-meat was not so much
that the salt was being trodden into the flesh with
the curer’s boots as that that gentleman at night-
time trotted round with a lantern and spade per-
forming the duties of local sanitary contractor.
* * * *
Queensland has almost the highest mar-
riage-rate among the Australian provinces, and the
highest birth-rate of all. The sugar-town of
Bundaberg, Glassey’s electorate, is the most pro-
lific town in Queensland. It may interest people
who regard the North as the very gateway of
Sheol to learn that the death-rate in the United
Kingdom is twice that of Bananaland.
* * * *
According to last year’s statistics, two-
thirds of Queensland husbands, in ’95, chose
fellow-countrywomen for wives—but only 609 out
of 1367 Q. girls chose native-born Australians for
husbands ! Amazing numbers of girls gave their
age as 21 —which is attributed to false declarations
on the part of girls wanting to marry. The little
dears will commit perjury, face imprisonment, or
anything else, only to secure a man. By the way,
Rockhampton marriages increased 51 on previous
year’s record, while Brisbane increased only to the
extent of 34. Another incontestable proof of
Centralian greatness.
SARCASM.
LITTLE PONDO (of the Zoo, as one of the “very nicest”
passes by): “Mother, what’s that?”
“ My dear, I don’t know —but they call it Evolution."
The Big Synod (Anglican) of all Austra-
lasia earned the approval of the Sydney Press
Jeameses for its display of man-millinery. Jeames
was profoundly moved at what he was proud to
call a “spectacular display.’ Yes, “they’d got
’em on,” right enough, but your Anglican bishop
in all his bravery can’t come near the Kyardinal
when he dons his scarlet, or even the wigged
buffoonery of a Full Court on dress parade. What
would Religion or Justice be to Man without
livery? The epaulettes make the soldier; the
ermine makes the judge ; the lawn makes the
bishop. But the Papalists are easily first.

Cairns Chows recently held a joyous picnic
on the Barron. Roast pig and chicken, together
with fearsome banging on an inverted copper
cylinder mingled with weird and wondrous cym-
bal melody, constituted two-thirds of the enter-
tainment. Cost of the outing, £so—seventeen
pounds of which was subscribed by European busi-
ness people.
c * * * *
Pastor Bevan (Melb.) now
allows “ free discussion ” after his
Sunday morning’s service. The
privilege of giving back-talk to the
reverend should brighten up the
tabernacle a lot. Hitherto the rev.
has slanged the congregation and
told them they were miserable
sinners with impunity, but when a
hysterical sister is allowed to get
up and state that she isn’t any more
miserable than he is, and anyhow
she is a decent married woman,
and anybody who says she isn’t
should be ashamed of himself, and
the preacher is a cantankerous old
man, and she wouldn’t be seen in
the street with a hat like his, there
will be a lot of thrill about the
proceedings.
* * *
An up-country medico was
recently called in a midnight hurry
to attend a poor woman, who had
only half-a-candle in the house.
After vainly asking for more, he
used it to the last atom, left his
patient well, and was passing out
when an elder child, who had been
told by her father that the doctor
might find a baby under a goose-
berry-bush and bring it along, ran
out in her nightgown to enquire:
“Oh, doctor, did you find one?”
“Yes, my dear,” said the doctor
“I found two.” “Two! Oh,
father, if we had only had another
bit of candle ! ”

* * « *
The prize Sobraon (N.S.W. reformatory-
training ship) boy has made his appearance in
Franklyn, aged 16, who last week pleaded guilty,
at Quarter Sessions, to breaking, entering and
stealing. He was bound over and let go, only to
be immediately arrested for being illegally at large
from the Sobraon. Sent there originally, the lad
was apprenticed to a farmer, but bolted ; was cap-
tured, and sent to Brush Farm for two years. He
broke out, and was brought back. Escaping again,
he was recaptured and returned to the Sobraon,
and ultimately apprenticed to another country
employer, from whom he took French-leave, to re-
appear shortly afterwards at the Sessions.
* * * *
The waitresses in Melb. coffee palaces
have all received notice that their wages will be
reduced from Ist inst., when the Factories Act
begins to operate.
Captain of the s.s. Chindera (lost on the
Tweed bar, Sept. 81ast) cross-examined at N.S.W.
Marine Board enquiry (daily paper report) :
To Mr. Arnott: The vessel was the worst steering boat
he had ever been in, and no matter what course she was
being steered could not be relied on to go within three
points of it. No man who had ever been in her could
steer her straight. She had been lengthened, too, and
built up on top, after which alterations—which in his
opinion weakened her —the name had been changed from
Karuah to Chindera, and she was registered as a new
ship.
Adolph Dahlberg, acting mate, corroborated.
This dodge of re-naming old boats is an ancient
one and much practised in certain circles. By the
way, if the captain and mate of the Chindera are
telling the truth, who licenses the Chindera to go
to sea ?

divorces since the passing of the Shiels Act is a proof that
the law was badly needed. If people rush to avail them-
selves of a new law, it proves that the new law meets a
widely-felt want. (2) The C. of E. (or any other Church)
has every right to refuse to marry divorced (or any) per-

“adulterous” is outside any Church’s jurisdiction, and
comes clearly within the penalties of the defamation law.

Esq., and when he tells people everywhere to take
Warner’s Safe Cure for liver trouble, all know
that he means just what he says.
(t Melbourne, 18th Sept., 1896.
. About seven or eight years ago I was suffering
with severe pains in my right side and under the
shoulder blades: also flatulency and indiges-
tion. _ My urine was very much discolored and
contained a thick sediment. This naturally un-
fitted me for my business, and with loss of sleep
and no appetite I thought my time had come. 1
had some of the best medical men attending me,
who prescribed all sorts of medicines without
doing me the least good, so I decided to give them
up and try Warner’s Safe Cure, which had not
only been recommended me by several friends who
had derived great benefits from it, but I had read
one of their pamphlets of cases similar to mine
being cured by it, and testimonials from well-
known citizens whom I knew. I quickly decided
to'try it, and consequently sent for three bottles
and some of the Pills. When I took the first
bottle I did not feel a bit better: in fact, I
thought I was worse, but as one bottle does not
always have the effect, I persevered with it, and
when I had got through with the fourth bottle the
improvement was so marked that I still kept at it,
and when I had taken about fourteen bottles, I
was so well and strong that I had no necessity to
take any more, as I really felt a new man and fit
for business, with more energy than I had before.
I have much confidence in recommending Warner’s
Safe Cure to anyone who suffers with liver com-
plaint. ’’—Robert Langford, (Fish Market, Melb.)
This medicine is better than all the stimulants
or other stuff which people take to “ brace up ” on,
because it is a scientific remedy that has been
tried in every civilised country, and because its
effect is permanent. It does not make a person
better for a short time and then lose its influence,
but when it is taken properly it gives them new
life. It builds up the weakened system and re-
stores the liver to its normal activity. Send post-
card to Warner’s Safe Cure Company for a new
pamphlet treating of liver and kidney diseases.

Tenor Newbury seems to have been scared off his pro-
posed VVestralian tour by gruesome tales of typhoid. At
latest he had gone to Tasmania on a concert-tour.
“ Alick,” the aboriginal who died in Maitland hospital
a few days ago, was once well-known in N.Q. by his native
name, “ Reka.” _ Some time back he left his tribe and
joined a travelling circus. The aboriginals, by the way,
are exhibiting an unhallowed desire to go into the show
business wherever they can. In an attempt to throw a
somersault over some horses, Reka missed his distance,
fell on the last horse and broke his back. The sure-
footed and keen-eyed black, who can climb the sheer
trunks of trees that a white man would shudder to try at,
generally comes to grief when he attempts the tricks that
a white fellow thinks nothing of.
W. J. Turner contrives to get hold of promising novices
for his “Promenade” concerts at Melb. Exhibition. On
Saturday he introduced a successful debutante in Miss
Minnie Waugh, a tall and comely damsel with a very nice
quality of mezzo-soprano. Miss Waugh, who was facing
a big audience in a big building for the first time, fetched
an encore for her “When the Heart is Young.”
Brough tries Melb. Bijou again.
In the enlargement of Talma’s photo., Melb. Princess’
auditorium, fully a thousand portraits come out.
Had it not been for a Castlemaine solicitor (F. K. Best)
I 1 rank Cates, who died unknown in Castlemaine hospital,
would have been buried in a pauper’s grave. Best sent
word of Cates’ death to Vic. Dramatic Ass., and that
body gave Cates decent burial. Cates was on his way to
Echuca Sanatorium, but was taken so ill when passing
through Castlemaine that he had to be removed to the
hospital to die there.
The cinematographe is the most wonderful illusion of
the century. It is to the camera obscura (to which
it has been unthinkingly likened) what the modern
photograph is to the old scissored silhouette.
True, it is as yet imperfect, a cin-etc. picture being
like something viewed through continuous flashes
°£ lightning or a storm of rain, an unpleasant
effect due to electric vibration. When this is reme-
died, and further inventive genius solves the photo-
graphic-reproduction of color problem and allies it to the
above marvel, optical illusion will have reached its ulti-
mate. Given a by-that-time perfected phonograph and
cinematographe, and the 2ist century will still see and
hear all that was famous in the 20th.
Sims Reeves is just now concert singing in South Africa,
where he celebrated his 79th birthday on 26th Sept.
Brisbane gods have had their claim to howl during the
intervals admitted by the Firm’s Opera Co., after a struggle
with manager Bracy.
Fanny Simonsen used to claim, as in her own experi-
ence, the story of the old woman who knelt to a statue
of the Madonna : “ Holy mother ! you know me—me and
you is alike. I have had a son guillotined, and you have
had a son crucified. We have both of us had' a deal of
trouble in our family.”
The effect of George Rignold’s coaching is very evident
in the “Tommy Atkins” performance at Melb. Royal.
For once in a way George stands out of the cast and
directs operations from behind the scenes.
The caf6 chantant “turn” in Paris just now, writes a
travelling Australian to The Bulletin, is a particularly
dirty piece of dumbshow, largely patronised by respectable
citizens with their ivives and daughters. Half a dozen
well-set-up, deep-chested, handsomely-dressed young
women enter as if just arrived by steamer. A Customs
officer of the zealous stamp suspects them of smug-
ghng, and insists on them removing their various gar-
ments for inspection. Just imagine a clever music-hall
Frenchman in such a part and you will have a slight idea
of the disgusting pantomime that keeps the house in roars
of excited laughter. A coarse can-can closes the indecent
sketch.
Sydney Philharmonic Society is'hard at work on “St.
Paul ” (Mendelssohn), which is to be given on 14th instant!
Melbourne Amateur Orchestral Society, which has been
practising stealthily for months, will reveal itself in a
complimentary concert to its conductor, G. E. Howard
at Prahran (Melb.) Town Hall on Friday, Oct. 9th. The
society intends to attack Schubert’s No. 8 Symphony
also Mendelssohn’s overture to “Midsummer Night’s
Dream,” subsequent to a short lecture on Shakespeare’s
fairies by the Rev. Bevan. The spectacle of plump,
cherubic Bevan discussing the airy creatures of classic
fiction should be a huge success: Songs by Buzzi, Mrs.
I aimer, and Miss Maggie Stirling, and a ’cello solo
by George Howard will variegate the programme.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Oct. 6,, 1:896i
Mr Dear Myee—
A very thin house was a depressing’ circum-
stance of Monday’s performance of “ The Rivals,’”
though the gorgeous Malaprop and Languish;
gowns helped to raise the spirits of the scattered;
few present. This much-paragraphed vesture,
quite came up to advance descriptions. LydSa’s-
(Miss Elliot) first allurement was of blue taffeta,
bunched over a white silk petticoat; her seconds
triumph showed salmon-pink blushing through a
white chiffon, paniers and trappings caught with’;
roses and long grasses; her celebrated jetty hair
was hanging down her back. Altogether she was
aradiantvisionof loveliness. Aunt (Miss Mortimer)
trailed some yards of expensive pink brocade
behind her as a Watteau train; afterwards, ditto
in yellow over white lace skirt, and showed too.
brief glimpses of a red-heeled shoe in the minuet..
Minus his curl-papers the bashful Bob Acres was
a wee, modest, crimson-tipped flower in canary
smalls. Gertrude Elliott, as Lucy, gratified her
fancy for scarlet by coming out dyed red all over.
Mrs. Ponderbury’s past was so long ago and such
a very little one—for it consisted of a single kiss
merely—that a fashionable first - night audience
might, but for the aggressiveness of her protesta-
tions of virtue, have condoned it entirely. Be-
sides, Mrs. Brough looked much too pretty as the
rampageous Lucretia to quite convince us that
even Rumford’s version of the affair wasn’t under-
drawn and faintly-colored. Moreover, no husband
in his senses would shy at having to personally
conduct the wearer of that first-act gown—made
of silk of a confused-looking floral pattern of many
colors, green coming uppermost in the struggle.
The bodice had some cunningly-arranged black:
and white chiffon and a new idea in sleeves which
gave a hint at once to every woman in the d.c.;;
the bonnet was like a bunch of watercress with up-
standing roses nodding k la mode, also the
velvet cloak was good to look at. Last year’s trip.
’Ome gave Mrs. B. many valuable suggestions,
about stage-dressing, and, being a clever woman,,
she has carried them out to the pleasure of her ad-
mirers but to the utter destruction of our faith ini
the probability of Ponderbury.
Emma Temple isn’t half garish enough in her’
garments for her part, but her description of the.
recent Inglis-Hamilton wedding is likely to be one.
of the hits of the piece. At first, the audience
didn't quite catch on, but when they got over their-
puzzlement, they roared appreciatively. Kathe-
rine Hardy has a festive gown of apricot silk and;
puffy white lace. The whole play has been given,
a liberal suit of whitewash by the English adaptor,
but it is extremely thin in spots, with large bare,
patches here and there, where the suit has given
way at the seams. There are signs that even a,
coat of whitewash would have been useful to veil
the situation in the original second act. The first,
night’s top-dressing on the Nicest ran mainly to gay
and glittering theatre coats, which, however be-
spangled, give a woman the appearance of having’
walked out in a glorified dressing-jacket. There
was one cape, with gigantic hood, reminiscent of
Camille, but not, alas, the Potter profile to be set.
off therewith. A new musical idea was the.
“Bicycle Fantasia,” played between the acts,,
descriptive of the joys and troubles of the rolling-
wheel. It was very bright, and quite loud enough,
to be sometimes audible above the cackle.
The vice-regal presence gave tone to the first;
dajr’s sports of N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union, an aggre-
gation of clubs bound together in the silken bonds,
of trying to find the best lily-white amateur to.
represent Australasia in England. His Ex. was
chiefly occupied in struggling with a jibbing-
starting-pistol which refused to pop when asked,
and went off at odd moments to the surprise of
everybody, even the firer. There was a pensive
droop about the proceedings—except the band,
which was going strong to the last, and some of
the grand-stand raiment, which quarrelled cruelly
among its component parts. The new “ tulip ’”
parasol abounded on the lawn; worn with taste,
it is a graceful fancy, but a purple-and-pink
“ tulip ” shading a magenta-flowered hat and
vesture of yellow muslin really yells at passers-by.
Among the riders, I principally noticed one with
large white shoes which made his feet look like
washing-bundles, but which flashed around first to
the winning-post; also, the well-known amateur
with the smile which was once said to have bent
the corner of a country race-track.
An enterprising tea-room proprietor has devised
an original livery for his waitresses. They are to
wear pink cambric bloomer costumes with black
waist-belts and neckties.
Some of the women in the West could tell heroic
stories if they chose. They don’t generally, and
therein lies the heroism. I heard lately of a girl
in Kalgoorlie who supports, by type-writing, her
father, mother and seven brothers and sisters. She
saved in Perth .£25 to enable her to go to the field,
and, on leaving, placed most of the amount in her
sisters’ hands; she arrived in Hannan’s with 3d.
For three months she slept on the bare ground,
but now is in full work and coins money. There
is no moral to this story except that hard luck
usually doesn’t last with smart people.
Spirits are very attentive to wealthy widows. It
is probably solely the devotion of the disembodied
ones to rich men’s relics that booms the business
at all. In a Vic. seaside town there is a diamond-
drill at work boring for coal through a sandstone
bluff, in obedience to directions received per ghost
at a guinea stance with the lights turned down
and the moneyed novice in the room. The pro-
moters of the search haven’t come to coal yet (nor
to the bottom of the widow’s purse), but they are
still boring enthusiastically. Another much-
gilt lady has been standing a siege of begging-
letters from the sheeted and oofless dead for some
time past. They arrived by post through a noted
medium from the perturbed spirit of her husband,
and were all on the same lines—making an im-
passioned appeal that conscience money be at once
paid to the “ meejum ” on the perturbed spirit’s
behalf, whereby much coin would ultimately flow
into the pockets of the would-be payee, a tried
and trusted and named friend of deceased. As the
widow had some prudence she showed the letters
to her brother and others having authority and—
■well, now there is a row.
At her own fancy-dress ball Mrs. J. R. Wood
appeared as Juliet
. The man who hired a cab by the month and used
it as a dwelling house, only getting out for his
meals, has been imitated in Sydney recently. A
lavish person with plenteous coin paid a cabman
to drive him about indefinitely. After drifting
round a block or two the capitalist would declare a
dividend and hand up a sov. through the trap-hole.
This was repeated every mile or so until cabby
felt that he had struck a portable gold-mine.
While the lode lasted it yielded £2(l a day.
Another Croesus spilling his wealth around just
now. is staying at a Southern hotel and getting rid
of his annual .£20,000 or so in a manner recalling
the brave days of old. He never demeans himself
by accepting change, and allows the waiters five
shillings on every bottle of champagne; also, it is
recorded that he met the same waiter about the
hotel five times before breakfast and gave him a
'sovereign every time.
But people of this sort are rare now-a-days.
During the recent Sydney race-week there were in
town at least ten young bachelors not one of whom
had an income of less than £5OOO a year. And,
:so far as the public reputation of those ten young
men goes, there was not one of them who would
■decline the change out of a shilling after having
jhis hair cut.
Experience of the long-drawn R.C. Melbourne
'Cathedral f£te has stimulated St. Mary’s Cathe-
dral authorities to arrange for next Easter a fair
run on similar lines, including race-games, tom-
bolas, and all the most popular coin-extracting
'schemes of the Melb. show. Up to date this
planet has seen no better money-raising instru-
ment than Mother Church. Money never smells
■once the faithful have a grip on it; while Angli-
oans and other Protestants are coquetting with
bazaar-raffles or observing dubiously on an
astonished eucalyptus a placard announcing a
dance for church funds, the robust Roman con-
science approves of raking in the shekels from all
■quarters. At St. Patrick’s fair women who have
revolted at the idea of backing their fancy at
Elemington came out many pounds to the bad
from the race-game. Nuns are now allowing
skirt-dancing in a mild form to be taught in con-
vent schools. They ought to smile on the worldly
pastime of dancing, seeing that the light fantastic
toe has been tripped for Sydney R.C. charities
this year to the tune of £l3OO at four large balls.

results have led to a “Trilby” cult among the
orthodox. A vendor of church requisites in the

of copies of the book of the beautiful bunionless

. Art-unions, if put down in one form, will arise
in another. They are too useful to needy human
nature to be “ hurled into obscurity,” as the arch-
deacon of Hobart phrased it at the Synod. A year
or two back a gilded maiden I wot of was induced
by her art-instructor to buy a whole bookful of
chances to secure all or any of eight or eleven
masterpieces which he had just dashed off with
impressionist effects of pink rocks washed by violet
sea-spray, and purple gulls flying in a red-ochre
sky. After many unsuccessful attempts to form
other ’eart-unions with Australian heiresses, who
weren t taking anything in imported painter at
that time, he ultimately left for Europe, and now
holds shows advertised by royalty. But the date
of his art-union drawing has not yet been fixed.
The thousands of people who knew that plucky
and clever old lady, Mrs. Carl Eischer, will be
grieved to hear of her death, which took place
early on Tuesday morning. Mrs. Eischer was
connected with one or other of the Fairfaxes’
papers for many years, and did a vast amount of
good journalistic work in Sydney.
In a gushing notice of the increase of a pro-
minent citizen’s family, an up-country paper
makes a funny printer’s error. “ Mrs. Blank pre-
sented her husband with duplicate olive branches
on Thursday—twin sins ! ”
Rosehill on Monday was a surging mass Of
crowd and sandwich-paper, diversified with th*
mangled remains of a mountain of Sydney’s
national fruit. Whenever the people turn-out in
their thousands like that, it wouldn’t be a bad idea
to stand a few dozen waste-baskets around to
receive fragments, and make the place look less
like a wrecked marmalade-factory next door to a
ruined paper-mill. Assorted games went on all
day, under the guidance of the Eight-Hour
Demonstration Committee, in white hats with
blue ribbon badges—who gracefully posed before
the camera-man during the afternoon and had
their photos, taken. In this gathering I missed
two sights familiar in the corresponding Mel-
bourne demonstration—there were no ‘‘down-
with-sweating ” placards in the procession, and no
pert little misses with short frocks, tight waists
and carefully-touzled hair in the crowd. The
latter product of civilisation seems indigenous
only to the Southern city. Sydney has some
things to be thankful for.
A certain very, very pretty actress and her com-
pany were lately travelling by train. The train
was uncomfortably crowded, and the ladies were
cooped up with a squalling baby in the rear of the
sleeper. Everyone in the men’s end of the car was
up and dressed except the occupant of an upper
berth, when the fascinating mummer and her com-
panion came in and sat talking with friends. The
ladies’ end of the sleeper was being made up and
there was no place to sit. The actress explained this
politely to the upper-berth man when he put his
head out and requested the ladies to withdraw,
but he happened to be a very modest man, and
consequently, became very angry indeed. Being
unable to divine his feelings, the lady became angry
too, and said his toilette had no charms for her,
but she would sit where she was and look stead-
fastly out of the window—which she did amidst
the laughter of the other passengers. But if she
had known who her now raging and furious fellow-
passenger was she would probably have spared his
blushes.
I am told that the wife of baronet Clarke, who
had organised a swell entertainment in aid of a
consumptive hospital, was almost worried out of
her wits by the demands of the Board of Health,
who ordered that half-a-dozen doors in the ball-
room of the East Melbourne mansion be taken
down, another half-dozen swung outwards instead
of inwards, a platform erected near the windows
so that people might jump out easily in ease of
fire, and an entire re-arrangement of lighting and
seating accommodation, in case the place blew up
through the blaze of society gems. The lady
enquired the.penalty if all this were not done, and
the Board said “gaol.” She signified her readi-
ness to go there as the quickest way of meeting
the difficulty. Then the Board refused to allow
the entertainment to take place unless the altera-
tions were made. And made they had to be at
great cost.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
“Do you apply for bail ?” sweetly asked
Sydney P.M. Addison of an accused. “Yes, your
Worship.” “Why, the prisoner’s serving a
sentence now,” said a policeman. “How dare
you apply for bail, sir, when you are serving a
sentence in gaol already?’’thundered Mr. Addison.
“ Ah, really—l had quite forgotten all about it,”
blandly answered the prisoner. He might have
added that he didn’t ask the beak; the beak
asked him.
That part of the N.S.Wi Civil Service
whose screw was reduced by the Commission, got
its reduction promptly on Ist July. That part of
it which got its salary increased—though the in-
creases were also supposed to date from Ist July—
hasn't seen a penny of the extra salary yet. It
was understood that everything would be paid up
when the estimates were passed by the Assembly,
and when they got through last month the Service
rushed out and ordered its new suit on the strength
of it. Then Ist Oct. came and nothing happened,
just as before, and if The Bulletin printed the
Service’s present candid opinion of G. H. Reid, it
would get the editor three months at the very least.
In Sydney the policeman makes a point of
bailing-up and cross-questioning any poorly-
dressed man who may be carrying a bag, while
the “ swell ” passes unchallenged. Yet, as a
matter of fact, thieves are often better-dressed
than honest men. Example : A man with a
dozen aliases has been sent up by Judge Coffey
for 54 years, i.e., for 6 years on each of nine
charges, the sentences to run concurrently. The
man dressed fashionably, drove a smart sulky,
called at houses ostensibly on business, and
“ lifted ” whatever jewellery he could find in the
absence of the owners. He passed daily under
the noses of the police, and was only bowled out
through the information of a pal.
A poor, miserable, drink-sodden indivi-
dual loaded himself with blue-metal last Thursday
night and went to N.S.W. Parliament House to
“deal it out” to members of the Assembly.
Dacey, M.L.A. for Botany, was upbraiding sundry
legislators for decrying the labors and abilities of
aldermen: “It is all very well for honorable
gentlemen to cast stones in this light and airy
manner at aldermen ” when the alcoholised
stranger, with a vague idea of the appropriateness
of his action, forthwith hurled two rocks at him.
Drink had so shattered his nerves that though the
house was full he failed in two shots to hit any-
thing harder than the floor. He was promptly
dragged forth and handed over to the police.
Thank Heaven, Australia is a law-abiding place.
In an older civilisation those stones might have
been bombs.
Time deals gently with the woman who
uses “Babeskin” soap. It softly removes the
tell-tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. S. Harris, 78 King-street, Sydney.*
The Melb.-newspaper story that Julian
Thomas (“The Vagabond”) was “in 1870 and
thereabouts a cicerone to rich Americans, explain-
ing to them everything connected with Old Paris,
and that he was also an occasional worshipper in
the Notre Dame de Nazareth Synagogue, and was
known by the name of Sarfati,” looks somewhat
improbable, for “ The Vag.” was not a sufficiently
good linguist to successfully bear either an Italian
or a French name.
Essie, lately Juliet,
Maidenly-named Capulet,
How does married life suit you,
Wedded to It. Montague ?
Do you miss the vaulted gloom
Of your ancient family tomb ?
Or regret that tragedy
Luckily has passed you by ?
How does Romeo fill the bill,
Has he ina-in-law’s ill-will ?
Is he always home to dine—
Ever mention Rosaline ?
“ Bounty boundless as the sea,”
“ Love as deep ” —your maiden plea—
All that speech ’bout Phoebus’ horse,
Can you still the above endorse ?
After years of wedded bliss
Other parts now come amiss:
You above them all still set
Name of loving Juliet.

William Luxton, of Corowa (N.S.W.), was,
it is stated, one of the Balaclava Six Hundred.
He received a pension, but transferred it years
ago to his mother, and now makes ends meet by
driving a wood-cart. A theme for Kipling.
