publicity given to his authorship of the “Bleeds
that won the Empire,” but he has spent much
trouble of late months in endeavoring to have
them in a collected form (for which, presumably,
he is to be paid), appointed to be read in the publio
schools of Australasia.
Singular how many old military officers,
after a life of slaughter, lapse into prayerful reli-
giosity, while clergymen hanker after gore. Thus,
while Major-General Tulloch, late v ic. Military
Commandant, devotes his leisure to solving the
problem of how the Children of Israel crossed the
Red Sea, Pastor Fitchett wallows in blood and
slime and revels in smoke and battle-dust in his
yawps about British fights.
Mahlstick : “Fred. Barnard, whose death
through a smoking-in-bed accident is cabled, was
not altogether a successful limner. The reason
was the common mistake of trying to force a dead,
played-out style on a changed public, instead of
taking his cue. In short, he would not draw. So
he was greasy, with all his invention, and had not
the clientele of effete Gilbert, Cruikshank, Leech,
Phiz, Kenny Meadows, &c.”
John Howard Angas, reputedly the
wealthiest man in S.A., was 73 on Oct. 5. His
father lived to be 90, and the son looks as active
and full of life now as most men do at 50. He still
personally superintends all his big business, and
at every show is to be seen admiring his stud cattle
or sheep. Angas pere, among other things,
founded the wealthy South Australian Co., the
now defunct Bank of S.A., and the Union Bank,
which swallowed it up.
Yic. Ministry did a graceful thing by
Premier Turner, the other evening, in the form of
a cafe dinner, to mark the second anniversary of
his accession to office. Isaacs, on behalf of his
brethren, presented their modest ruler with a gold
bracelet and brooch for his wife, who, in her way,
assists the Government by taking Turner for a
blow down St. Kilda pier whenever she can catch
the indefatigable attorney off duty.
Spagnoletti : “The soldier is the most
consummate ass on this planet, the mere jumping-
jack firework cracker of a Bonaparte, Bismarck,
or even of a Gladstone, who would never declare
war on Asia, if he had to fight himself. Just as
Melb. boils over with enthusiasm on a newspaper
discussion over the Balaclava Charge, it is found
that one of the Six Hundred, after 30 years’ slow
starvation, is actually dying on the spot.”

The late Chief Justice, by the way, once con-

verses embodying what must have been about all
the sentiment his chilly nature had to spare.

MARTIN , THE CYCLIST.
One of the late Julian Thomas’s many
anecdotes was located at Ballyhegney, a back-
block township in the wilds of Tipperary, which
he struck when suffering from acute rheumatism
in the knee. Casting his luggage into the only
pub., “the Vag.” tumbled into bed, and asked
that a doctor be called. “ Divil a docthor is
there, at all, at all, fur this twinty mode,” said
the boniface, “but,” he observed confidentially,
“there’s a cousin av moine, a vet., he’s a power-
ful clever man, an’ he threats us all. “Fetch
him,” groaned the suffering visitor, and some
minutes later the learned leech was disturbed
from his work of forking manure, and ushered
into the bedroom. “ The Vag.,” however, des-
cribed his symptoms, the other seized, his wrist,
and, assuming an air of profound wisdom, sat
silent for about lOsec. Then he pulled down the
coverlet and began prodding the invalid’s chest
with his knuckles. A dozen knocks, and the
pressman let out: “What the devil are you
doing ? I’ve got rheumatism in the leg. ’ The vet.
did not look up, but, waving a hand, imperiously
exclaimed—“ Whisht! It’s all the same to the
faculty.” Which it probably was.

“Yesterday (Aug. 16),”
writes a travelling Australian
to The Bulletin from Lon-
don, “I lunched with Hori
Grey—ah ! so old, feeble, and
almost voiceless now. For an
hour I sat beside him listen-
ing to his low, whispered
tones. He talked to me of
our side of the world, and as
he spoke his dimmed eyes
lighted up and a faint smile
came to his pallid lips. Our
hostess —a charming, graceful
woman (a daughter of Richard
Gobden) —treated the old war-
rior with such reverent re-
spect that my heart went out
to her with a jump. By-and-
bye, as we sat by a window
overlooking a park, a waving
mass of green (’twas in Here-
ford Square), he pressed my
hand —‘You and I have seen
places more beautiful,’ _ he
said ; ‘ but yet, how dear it is
to be so near Nature and God,’
and his tremulous hand point-
ed to the trees beneath. Old,
so old. Yet brain so clear,
and in his eyes that strange,
patient look that says ‘My
day is done; lam waiting for
the End.’ He lives but a
short distance away, and, with
myhostess’shusband, I walked
home with him. We stood a
little while on the doorstep,
and as he turned to me with
his weak old hand held out
and said in a whisper ‘You
will come and see me some-
times, won’t you ? ’ I could
only bend my head and turn
away.”
Like Mr. Gillen, who has just died,
O’Loughlin, the new S.A. Lands Commissioner,
was born in the province, of Irish parents. He is
a Catholic, and a teetotaller. The deceased
Minister and his successor lived in the same hotel
rooms when in the city attending Parliament, and
they were bosom friends. O’Loughlin is 42 years
old, and has always voted on the Democratic
side, but he will add little strength to the Ministry.
John Douglas, now visiting the South,
when Q. Premier declared the whole Chinese
Empire an infected port. It was in the days
of the Palmer rush, and cute Brisbane mer-
chants were importing eager Chinese in bat-
talions sometimes at the rate of 1000 a
week. Very frequently these proved leprous or
insane, and became a charge on the province.
Douglas passed an Act imposing a <£lo poll-tax,
and even this mild guarantee was to be returned
if the immigrant proved sound in mind and limb.
But Downing-street, much more bumptious then,
refused to concur. Douglas seized on a case of
small-pox and a bright idea, quarantined every
vessel arriving from China, and at one time had
4000 Chinese in durance on Fitzroy Island, near
Cairns. That stopped the invasion. Quarantine
charges knocked all the profit out of the business,
and the Brisbane traders dropped it suddenly.
But Douglas couldn’t manage to send his 4000
back, and by degrees they drifted into circulation.

Yic. Chief Justice Madden invariably
writes out his more important judgments at home,
and apparently seizes upon the handiest piece e;
paper, irrespective of its contents, for the purpose
The obverse side of his decisions is often amusing
and occasionally startling to the outsider. Invi-
tations for “Sir John’s” presence at Soanso's
garden-party are, of course, commonplace enough,
but when bills for bear’s-grease and moustache
wax from fashionable hairdressers are handed frote
the bench an audible smile occasionally arises,
Justice Williams once handed back a judgment ol
the C.J.’s to his associate, remarking: “ Really, t
don’t want to know the size of ’s waist.” 1*
was a corsetifere’s account.
Of children this knight had no less than eighteen,
Twice nine little heads with a marriage between ;
He had nine when a barrister, nine when a judge,
And of “ sex”—since to Nature he owed not a grudge—
Nine exactly were girls, the other half hoys,
An equal division ‘twixt quiet and noise ;
While if by marriage the number be reckoned
There were nine of the first and nine of the second.
Nine in Tasmania, nine New South Wales ;
Then (to show with what justice he still held the scales),
Since nine it was clear he could not divide
(A third sex yet having never been tried),
Five sons and four daughters in Hobart were born,
That four sons, five daughters might Sydney adorn !
Twin daughters, twin sons, complete the strange story
Of this patron of “ wigs," though constant old Tory.

t{ A Sydney Sunday paper calls the C. of E.
the State Church.” Not in Australia. Australia
is not taking any State Church, and never will.
A Maoriland paper states red, white, and
blue are the British colors. Wrong. The British
colors are a streak of white on a ground of black,
brown, and yellow.
Premier Reid’s Colored Races Restriction
Bill contains a clause specially exempting from
its operations the native races of “Australia,
Maoriland and Tasmania.” And Tasmania is
thoughtful. l The ghost of Truganir; will be
delighted.
How does “ Lands ” Minister Carruthers
square. it with his Preetrade conscience to place
disabilities on Victorian incomers into N.S.W.
who want to take up land in the latter province ?
The “God-fearing” Ministry’s foreign-trade gospei
is largely leavened with protectionism.
It seems certain now that the Vic. teeto-
tallers will be against the Turner Ministry at next
election. Turner can’t promise them all they want
for he is the man with the majority, and would
have to make good his promises. The man with
the hopeless minority can safely promise anything.

caused Premier Reid’s sudden change of front on
this question. G. Reid’s proprietor, boss, and
political director issued his ukase that there was
to be no State Bank of Issue, and G. Reid re-
spectfully obeys.

M’sP. are invariably “ men who have failed in

Apropos of recent proceedings and loyalty
to the Queen. During the discussion of a postal
measure in Yic. Leg. Council Johnny Fawkner,
the alleged founder of Melbourne, proposed that
it should be a penal offence to affix a postage-
stamp to a,n envelope head downwards. Even the
Conservative Upper House couldn’t stomach that.
From the fact that N.S.W. Government,
without taking Parliament into its confidence,
conceded on Ist Jan., 1895, to the then Governor
the right to frank all his letters through the post,
the inference is drawn that there was truth in the
rumor of the mutually - admiring relations of
Premier Reid and Gov. Duff about the time
mentioned.
Last week’s work at Sydney Benevolent
Asylum included grants to 1977 cases of destitu-
tion representing 8221 adults and children—this
despite the recent despatch of hundreds of unem-
ployed to do scrub-cutting on the Bogan ! At the
end of July there were on the society’s books 1880
cases representing 7330 persons, so the rapidity
with which things are getting no better is obvious.
Papa Brunker (N.S.W. Colonial Secre-
tary) is on the horns of a dilemma. West
Maitland has a spick - and - span new court-
house, which, the residents say, will be a white
elephant if Quarter Sessions and Assize Courts
are not held there. East Maitlanders say that if
“Papa” consents to shift the superior Courts’
business from the East his seat will be to let at
next election.
Wrixon mournfully remarked the other
day that he was afraid “ the young Australian
colonies were departing from the old Anglo-Saxon
method of reforming the Constitution, which (the
method) was to wait, in order to ascertain if any
evils required to be remedied. Wrixon’s idea
seems to be that it is better political form to wait
for the doctor, or even the undertaker, than to call
in the sanitary inspector.
The recent autonomy debate in Banana-
land Assembly turned out a terrible fiasco —for
Separation. Only 14 members (all Labor) sup-
ported Kidston’s motion recommending the re-
ferring of the matter to a referendum, Northern
and Centralian Fat-Man Ms.L. A. stoutly support-
ing the Govt. By the way, Northern Queensland
(which, unfortunately, is quite indifferent to
Separation) has now a population of 91,000, while
Cantralia numbers about 54,000. And Autonomy
cannot count on one poor 10,000 supporters.
A FAIR DIVISION.
MISS SMITH M.B. : “ The etiquette of the profession requires that I should call
upon you, as lam the new-comer. lam going to settle here to practise.”
HE : “ Well, the practice isn’t enough for two—hut I suppose we will divide it.”
MISS SMITH : "Yes, we'll divide it. I’ll take all the births, and you can take all
the deaths .”
Premier Reid is steadily settling down
into the old M‘Millan-Parkes variety of Revenue
Tariffism, and the new Democratic Freetrade
movement is growing weak. It is distinctly pro-
mised teat the Land-tax is never to go further
than Id. in the £. The Customs are still to be one
of the principal sources of revenue—and are to
yield far more than the vaunted direct taxation.
The beer, wine, whisky and tobacco industries are
to be “ incidentally protected ” as heretofore. The
reform of the Upper House, the State Bank, the
Local Government Bill, the State Land Bank—all
seem to be dead. The proposal to make the State
the sole issuer of paper currency has dropped.
The Old Age Pension scheme, which has been a
good deal talked about of late, doesn’t seem to be
an item of the Government programme—the only
men who appear to take much interest in it are
the Labor Party and a few Oppositionists. The
Ministry mildly compromises with the Council
when the two bodies knock against each other
now; in fact, the new Freetrade lion has practi-
cally left off roaring.
Fraser, M.L.C., said recently that were it
not for the export of frozen meat God knows where
Victoria would be now. If Victoria were to ex-
port a few frozen M’sL.C. her whereabouts might
be even better known to the Almighty.
TALK BETWEEN KISSES.
(As overheard at a Sorrento hotel.)
HE : “ What do you think I had for dinner, pet? ”
SHE: “ Was it something dreadful?” —(Another long caress.)
HE: “ Sucking pig!”
SHE : “ Dearest, I never would have guessed it.”
According to Attorney-General Want, the
present N.S.W. electoral law is so strangely and
wonderfully arranged that the existing electoral
rights expire at the end of ’96, and no provision is
made for issuing new ones till 1899, and unless
things are altered the whole province will be
totally disfranchised for two years. Consequently
when the present Parliament expired no one would
be able to elect a new one, and representative
Government would drop through a large hole into
somewhere and vanish entirely.
Tozer was accused the other day, in Banana-
land Assembly, of sending Detective Clarke to
Rockhampton in order to ascertain who were fit
and proper persons to be jurymen in the Ayrshire
Downs arson case. He answered the charge
thus: “Surely it was not an improper thing to
find out accurately whether any man on the jury-
list was prejudiced or interested, from relationship
or other causes !! ” Sounds like a chapter from
the history of Castle government in Ireland.
It is believed that if he can manage the
political double-shuffle in Westralian scrip, pluto-
crat Solomon will be ready to take Treas. Holder’s
place in S.A. Govt, when that gentleman goes to
London as Agent-General. Solomon was whip to
ther Downer Ministry which Kingston displaced,
but he has thrown over the Opposition because he
was not consulted about the appointment of Copley
as leader. Solomon began political life as a
Liberal in 1890, but deserted to become stipendiary
whip to the Playford Government.
Out of 15 councillors in South Melb.
Council, five are house-agents. These, or some of
them, work the rolls pretty much as they please,
because the average tenant doesn’t care a rap
whether he is entitled to vote at municipal elec-
tions or not. Quite recently a well-known South
Melb. citizen wrote to the Age re the candidature
of one house-agent:—“l heard a relative of Mr.
say that if he had known in time that Mr.
was going to stand, he would have had 60 or
70 more ratepayers on the roll.” Nevertheless,
the Age gravely assures its readers that anything
like American ward-politics or wire-pulling is un-
known in Victoria.
Re the suggestion that if the Jacob Garrard
pietists of N.S.W. Assembly want to begin Par-
liamentary business with prayer, it will be needful
for them to bait the members with a hunk of some
sort of advantage. Lately in the English Com-
mons, the Speaker’s attention was drawn to the
fact that Dilke entered the chamber, reserved a
seat, sneaked out just as prayers were about to
commence, and sneaked back to the reserved place
when they were over. Whereupon the Speaker
ruled: “No prayers, no reserved seat,” and the
baronet was forced to retire. How many cranks
would carry stones to the gallery of N.S.W.
Assembly, if it became noised abroad that certain
M’sL.A. would be found on the floor of the House
engaged in prayer !

They say Major Le Caron is dead. Don’t vou believe it

Irishman, no treachery, no perjury is too base to
be beneath the Irish informer !
The Chow displaces the European, and the
Jap in turn displaces the Chow. In the Sandwich
Islands, the natives were superseded by Chinkies,
and now the latter are outnumbered by Japs in the
proportion of two to one.

clubs all over the
province; the num-
ber of them is certainly over 200, and may be any-
thing up to 1000. What is the good of trying to
put down Sunday trading and trading after hours
in the licensed hotels while the law allows this
sort of thing ?
Martin, the Champion Cyclist.
A height of sft. 6in., a weight of 12st. stripped
in condition, an unexpanded chest-measurement
of 40iri., an invincible will, a cool, sensible brain,
and the most temperate and regular of habits,
make up the chief features of that successful and
universally-popular cyclist, Martin—writes a well-
known sporting authority to The Bulletin. He
is a man who lives his athlete’s life wholly ; he is
stronger because of other men’s weaknesses. He
is an athletic paragon, eating and drinking only
what is good; a perfect clock in punctuality and
thoroughness at work ; as steady as an ascetic
when work is done. At 9.5 every night the man
who holds the scalps of all Australian cyclists is
in bed dead asleep. What match for him the men
who dig their graves in the midnight, and then on
the morrow, with jaded spirits and wearied
bodies, challenge this strong and rested man to a
bitter call upon the vital forces? Martin is a
study in muscle. He owes his sturdy physique to
Irish parentage and a life-time of muscular training.
Martin’s muscle has a history of four years spent
in the toils of the sea between the ages of 13 and
17; a record of jumping and long-distance run-
ning with a top effort of half-a-mile in 2min. 2sec. ;
a chronicle of systematic gymnastic work. Behind
Martin’s shoulder there is a ball of muscle which
is his referee when a row starts ; it should
knock skin and hair off a locomotive.
Martin first cycled six years ago. One of his
great feats was winning a six days and nights
wheel-race in ’9l in New York city. He rode 1466
miles with 11 hours’ sleep, and came in first, lead-
ing the second man by 25 miles. All the world’s
long-distance cyclists broke up that time. Not
long ago Martin was holding out a challenge to
race any three N.S.W. cyclists over 25 miles, a
fresh opponent at every miles. Nobody could
see his way clear to oblige Martin, however. The
great 25-mile match with M'Donell was a case of
boss rooster v. chicken. Martin broke his leg by
falling oft a high-wheeler in a race; after the
crash he remounted before discovering that his
was a case for splints. This accident makes his
right leg nearly an inch shorter than the left. In
Europe he raced at a weight of from 1501 b. to
1551 b. ; this year he is racing at from 16olb. to
1751 b. What is Martin’s age ? It’s the one ques-
tion he won’t answer. “Anyhow,” he remarks,
“ whenever they beat me it will be no credit to
them, because they will beat an old man and a
cripple. ”

A Clarion (Eng.) writer burns a little in-
cense at the altar of Lovely Woman —“Both
physically and mentally she has improved, and ia
still improving. I can’t deny that she is still too
much the slave'of Fashion, but even the feeblest
followers of that goddess are more independent,
more self-reliant, less artificial than the most ad-
vanced women of twenty-five years ago.” Mean-
time an American paper—and America raises its
hat to the fair sex higher than any other country
on earth—declares that the New Woman is being
physically and morally ruined by the bike.

* * * *
(l lraralgon (Vic.) Cricket Club recently held a
i soda!.” at which the Mason medals (given by the
local M.P ) were presented to the top scorers of the
season. The donor, in the course of his speech, offered
some instructive remarks on the subject of medals-“Of
one thing they could rest assured, and that was that so
long as they continued the great kindness of returning
hmi to Parliament so long would he continue to offer the
* nedals fp r competition. Although they were in reality
but small trophies, he felt that they were preferable to a
cup, which was only on exhibition for a short time and
then became, so to speak, nobody’s property. Medals
seemed a fairer way of recognising the merit of the indi-
vidual. They were things that he could keep, or he could
in turn piesent them to his wife or his sweetheart, or his
cousin, or his aunt, if he liked.” He can also, on occasion
present them to his uncle.
* * * *
Has this yarn been told before? At the local
cricket-matches on the Forest, at Nottingham, they have
a policeman engaged to clear away any enthusiastic
spectators who may be overstepping the boundaries. At
the opening match this season a very young policeman
with a profound ignorance of cricket, was put upon the
job. He went snorting around until he had cleared the
ova! of all non-players bar one roughly-clad interloper
who had taken up a position near the wicket. The ano-ry
policeman finding it impossible to shift this person with
a command, finally seized him from the rear and ran him
off the ground. It was the umpire.
* * * #
M‘Mullen, last season of the Glebe C.C., has
stepped into the place of probably best all-round player
in the Broken Hill Association, formerly held by bowler
Jones and after his departure by Burnett, P.M. M’Mullen
is batting very freely.
* * * *
The recent bicycle relay ride, with despatches,
between Sydney and Melbourne has induced a promoter
to devise a still faster mode of conveyance. He proposes
to enclose the missive in a cricket ball, to be thrown
from hand to hand by cricketers stationed about 80vds
apart along the road between the two cities. Not more
than 22 men would be required per mile.
* * * * *
Last year’s fishy action of the “ great ” Melb.
cricket-clubs in seceding from the Association to form a
body of their own and so prevent the weaker organisa-
tions from obtaining any part of the public gate-money,
has been followed (remarks a Bulletin correspondent)
>y eight of the Vic. football clubs, who have formed a new
Association and left the remaining five clubs to shift for
themselves. The most ludicrous part of the latter busi-
ness is that after the five leading clubs on the premiership
hs-t, the ‘ select ” Association is composed of St. Hilda
who come ninth, and Geelong and Carlton, who are
second and third last respectively. North, Ports and
Wilhamstown, who are ahead of the “Saints,” are care-
fully omitted, as is also Footscray, which precedes Carlton
The real point is that beyond the first raters St. Hilda is
included owing to the fact that it contains a “ sassietv ”
element, while Geelong and Carlton, with their past per-
formances, could scarcely be omitted with any degree of
decency. North, Ports, Williatnstown and Footscray
draw, firstly, their players from the moleskin ranks, and,
secondly, have no big bank balances to back them up, and
are consequently not considered “class” enough to asso-
ciate with the footballing Fatman.

Ned Gregory has a youth named Macbeth work-
ing on Sydney Cricket Ground ; said youth is sft. llin.
mgh, well-built, and, in N.G.’s opinion, a paralysing left-
hanfl bowler. “He bowls either fast or medium, keeps a
beautiful length, will pitch a ball on the leg stump which
will whip off the off bail; in junior matches he gets 6
wickets for 3, and 3 wickets for 0, and the other side pulls
up the sticks and goes home ; he should win all the inter-
provincial matches for N.S. W. Tried in private against
Giffen, Gregory, Donnan, Eady, &c., he did splendidly.”

* * * *
W. Edwards, well-known in Sydney as a long-
distance pedestrian early in the eighties, died recently in
England of heart-disease. His most noticeable perform-
ance was the beating, both in Sydney and Melbourne of
the much-boomed long-distance walker, Han. O’Leary
Scott, the Maorilander, beat him in 24 and 48-hour
matches.
* * * *
Young Archer Berry, son of the Vie. Speaker,
is one of the rising cyclists of the South, and will shortly
dispute the amateur supremacy with George Turner, jun.
It is, indeed, a coincidence that the heirs of the Speaker
and the Premier should both be top-notchers of Vic.
amateur cycling.

League will hold its summer carnival. Chief prizes—£loo

£5O first prize in the Orient Plate, 25 miles scratch race.
* * * *
Presiding at the season’s first meeting of N.S.W.
Swimming Association, that experienced medico and good
all-round athlete, Dr. Rot,h, condemned sprint swimming
as injurious to endurance, and upheld trick swimming as
calculated to inspire non-swimmers, or inferior ones, with
confidence. He looked upon this branch of athletics as
absolutely the best, and the onlj' one calculated to de-
velope all the muscles of the body.
* * * *
Moorite, thank Heaven ! was shipped to India
last week. This journal from time to time has allowed
irresponsible tipsters to weigh in with predictions that
Moorite was going to accomplish something which he in-
variably failed to do. If the beast dies on the voyage it
will be no more than he deserves.
* * * *
The success of the tote in Maoriland has inspired
an official of the Wellington R.G. to work out some
figures suggesting the advisability of paying divs. on both
first and second horses. At the recent W.R.C. meeting
the proposed system of two payments would have yielded
average divs. of about £6 10s. on the winner, and £6 on
the runner-up, a calculation that makes bookmakers’
odds look more sinfully ridiculous than ever. On behalf
of the scheme it may be pointed out that in England the
system of backing horses “ to win and 1,2,” is popular
with small owners as well as small punters, and there’s no
surface reason why the novelty of backing them for “ first
place and second place only” shouldn’t be even more
popular. As a device for silencing the weak-minded
person who has “ done nothing but back seconds all day
long” this would certainly fill a long-felt want.
* * * •
Kd. Bulletin,- —The M.L. bookmakers are
quite satisfied to lay tote odds, and will not likely go
broke at it, but the jockey clubs object; they say it is
defrauding them of their legitimate revenue, and so they
keep every known metallician off their courses, and if a
“book’’goes on the course he is disqualified for turf
malpractices and sued for trespass, irrespective of
whether he offers to make a bet or not. As a compro-
mise, the bookmakers have offered to refrain from laying
tote odds, and confine themselves strictly to their own
prices, but so far without the effect of their obtaining
admission. Tiib Bulletin seems to have an antipathy to
bookmakers, but it is mild compared to that of a Maori-
land Jockey Club—or - , I suppose, any other racing club
once it is granted the use of the totalisator.— lkey Mo,
Christchurch, M.L.
* * * *
A Melbourne paper has published a poor little
fable re Mossy Isaacs, in which that amusing old layer of
“ the double ” is represented as saying, “Afore I goes to
the Weedin' course,” &c., &c. This is first-cousin to a
criminal libel on the quite well-spoken Mossy.
* * * *
Once a week, or oftener, there is a pony race-
meeting at Ascot (Melb.), an unregistered course, where
the sport seems to be rather more genuine, on the whole,
than at, say, Caulfield. Stakes at Ascot seldom exceed
£2O. Last week, there was a Newmarket Handicap, of 50
golden so vs., and a desperate struggle ended in a dead
heat for first place. The betting showed the sincerity of
the 14 jockey's who weighed out, every starter being
backed, and some of them finding friends at the abnor-
mally liberal odds (for Ikey Mo) of 25 to I. The V.R.C.
and V.A.T.C. magnates never mention Ascot—they'have
no official knowledge of its existence, but the “great”
dailies pay it the respect due to a financially prosperous
show, which spends more money in advts. per annum
than all the representative racing clubs put together.
* » * *
The N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League credit balance
is now only a trifle over £l6OO, less than the League’s
scoop out of the Zimmerman carnival alone.
* * * *
The Victorian public is about to lose its right of
free access to the Flat at Flemington racecourse. The
comment of the Age on the dispute with the V.R.C. used
to be: “The claim made by the V.R.C. to the right to
exclude,” &c. Now it is : “ The claim made by the local
municipalities,” &c. Byron Moore, in a letter to Fleming-
ton Council, “regrets” that the municipalities have not
agreed to a compromise. What would Byron say if, on
catching a pickpocket attempting to filch his purse, the
pickpocket were to offer to take only half the contents if
he were permitted to escape ?
* * * jje ’
The Melbourne Cup in 1887 was won by a horse
named Dunlop. If a horse of that name ran in the same
race next Nov., probably every cyclist in Australia would
have a bit on.”
* * * *
The running in the Caulfield Stakes and Toorak
Handicap next Saturday may throw light on the Cup
question, which up to now is about as mixed as it has
usually been previous to the opening of the V.A.T.C.
meeting. Most of the likely Cup horses are entered for
one or other of the shorter races, and possibly the Toorak
winner may carry its 141 b. penalty nearly home in the big
handicap, as was the case with Devon two y'ears ago.
Anyway, the form shown by Hopscotch, Bloodshot’
Marusa( if they all go) and W. R. Wilson’s candidate in
the Stakes will be worth watching—better worth watch-
ing than wagering on. Guineas Day at Caulfield is
notorious for surprises.
In the meantime, a dozen or fifteen horses have
good paper chances for the Cup, as far as the present
writer can sort things out. Something without any
apparent chance whatever may do the trick—like Water-
fall last year—but there is no sort of excuse for merely
guessing at a “ turn-up.” The Bulletin will take the
following lot as probabilities, and try to pick one or two
particular fancies from among them—next week. Say
Hopscotch, Bloodshot, Marusa, The Parisienne The
Merry Boy, Merman or another of Mr. Wilson’s, Thunder
Queen, Duhallow and Ayrshire, with a distinct liking for
The Parisienne, Bloodshot, Marusa and Duhallow. How-
ever, this journal will consider the results of the first
Saturday at Caulfield in a calm, unbiassed spirit, and
revolutionise its ideas if necessary.
* * * *
The stories told of Hopscotch when he came to
Melbourne last year are told again now. He was then a
marvel,” “the fastest horse in Australia,” &c., until
the cracks met him and found out his weak spot. Hop-
scotch last Nov. cut a moderate figure when tackled at the
business end of the V.R.C. Flying Stakes by Hova and
WaUace Previously he had been beaten in the Melb.
Cup, Melb. Stakes, and Caulfield Cup after vanquishing l
fields of second-class horses in Sydney. He may be a
better stayer this season than he was twelve months ago
but, in the opinion of the erring mortal who writes these
*"P BC pt<;h has only a moderate chance of winninG*
the Caulfield Cup, and a much smaller show in the Mel-
bourne. A Sydney jockey, whose judgement is superior
to most peoples , recommends Fort for the C.C. in nre-
ference to anything else. e

Should one of W. R. Wilson’s be warmly backed
to win the Guineas on Saturday the public will have a
good enough tip to go upon ; also in any other event the
colors should be worth following. There is encourage-
ment in the recent performances of Resolute and Mer-
man. The three-year-old had some difficulty in beatino-
Bloodshot the other day, but the real quality of Blood"
shot, who ranked as a non-stayer in Maoriland will
reveal itself presently, luck permitting.
* * sfe *■
Daimio will have the services of Arthur Night-
ingale, the best across jumps in England, for the Grand
National in March next. Four miles and a-half through
soft, heavy country takes some getting.
Mrs. Langtry bought the Victorian mare,
Maluma, probably for racing purposes in the first in-
stance. Maluma, however, doesn’t figure among the
entries for the English autumn handicaps.
* * * *
Gus Millson is about our best trotting-rider in
saddle. After riding Oreole to victory at Brighton, Syd-
ney, he went over to Melbourne and at Ascot won the
handicap trot on Maggie.
* * * *
The second edition of J. J. Miller’s Sporting
Pamphlet, containing the performances of Melbourne and
Caulfield Cup horses up to the end of the A.J.O. meeting,
is now available. With the aid of the same firm’s Racing
Chronicle for the past half-year, in addition to the
Pamphlet, a careful analyst can discover all that he is
ever likely to know concerning the relative form of Cup
horses up to date.
* * * *
A significant move was made in Sydney clubs
last week in favor of Sabretache, who, for Melb. Derby
and Cup, was backed from an inspired quarter to win
over £lO,OOO.
* * *, *
Lnglish and ’Stralian papers are concerning them-
selves much as to the true turf-status of the great little
Pans when in Australia. His Metrop., Caulfield and Melb.
Cup performances alone demonstrated that he was
decidedly in the first class, and to say that he was pounds
behind the best Australian form is absurd.
* * * *
Persimmon, carrying 121 b. over weight-for-age,
by winning the Jockey Club Cup, has put the seal on his
fame as a three-year-old, and nearly 10,000 sovs. into the
pocket of his royal owner. This victory has now brought
the winnings of St. Simon’s progeny this year up to over
£50,000. Two sons of this great sire represent him here,
and the running of their stock will shortly be watched
with interest.
* * * *
At the annual meeting of V.A.T.C. the chair-
man announced a credit-balance of £6500. Mr. Albert
Miller headed the poll at election of committee.
* * * *
English handicappers seem to be avenging
Paris wins by lumping weight on to the more recently
imported Stralian horses. The Vie. chaser, having been
given 12st. 71b. (top weight) in Sefton Steeplechase, three
miles over Grand National course, the pen was put through
his name.
Australian trotting times have made vast strides
in the past decade. Honesty’s mile, 2min. 30-Msec., has
been reduced by Fritz to 2.14 ; and Honesty’s two miles,
smin. 13sec., has come down by the aid of Prince Harold
to 4.57*. Both Fritz and Prince Harold are N.S.W. bred.
Ten years ago, the best Australian-bred mile was held by
Leithamstead—2.42.
* * * »
Mr. H. C. White has just bought in England
for his Havilah stud a stallion by Sterling (sire of Isonomy)
out of a daughter of Musket. This new purchase intro-
duces the successful Oxford-Sterling line from Birdcatcher.
Sterling - also sired Isinglass, who won for millionaire
M'Cahnont more money than has been won by any other
horse in the world.
* * * *
Description by Frank Madden, M.L.A., of the
champion jumper Spondulix, which he formerly owned,
and which performed the record jump at Flemington
show : —“ I bought him because he could jump, and I
found that was all he could do. He had not the breeding
to be a racer, he had not the manners to be a hunter, he
had not the pace to be a roadster, and he was, unfortu-
nately, the wrong color for a hearse —but he could jump !”
* * * *
Surcingle: Why must Australian jockeys be
so inferior to the English ? Testimony both of English
and Australians.”
* * * *
The A.J.C. made a move in the right direction
by prohibiting two-year-old racing till Oct 1, thus putting
themselves in line with V.R.C., but they should have gone
one better by protracting the limit to Jan 1.
* * * *
A coincidental tip for the V.R.C. Derby and
Cup. The manager of a Newhaven (Eng.) bank is charged
with burgling the premises. The sporting significance of
this event is not clear to an ordinary mind, but it might
be interpreted by a spiritualist’s medium.
* * * *
A meeting between Pickles and the flying Cin-
derellen would be interesting. To carry lOst. 131 b. and
run a mile in lmin. 45sec. is a really grand performance
for a liliputian under 15 hands high; and this is what
Cinderellen did the other day.
* * J » *
Pharamond’s A.J.C. high-weight mile in lmin.
41J4sec., carrying list., is alongside the best Australian
records.
* * * *
Mullaebaa, the Vic. western district crack,
backed heavily for Caulfield Cup, has run last in eight
races and won none since arriving in Melb. And yet he has
his followers.
» * * *
The paucity of the entries and the meagre attend-
ance at Randwick on the off-day should lead the A.J.C.
to think there is a screw loose somewhere. Owners and
trainers blame the handicapping. The Sydney sporting
public never knew Mr. Scarr’s worth until' they lost him.
* * * *
The dear little sassiety girls brought no end of
influence to bear to have the Colac ball the night before
the Steeplechase, instead of after; because, as the
darlings said, “ if any of the gentlemen get killed, what
shall we do for partners 1 ” Thoughtful.
* * * *
The story goes that during his bout with Maher
Fitzsimmons was so dazed by a punch that Joe Choynski,
who helped to second him, had to jab him in the tender
region with the point of a penknife-blade to get him to
rise from his chair and answer the gong for the next
round.
* * * *
The news of O’Donnell’s victory over Slavin in
five rounds prepares the mind for any unexpected inci-
dent. Black Jackson, for instance, might come out as
Othello at Irving’s theatre, or a cash-amateur cyclist
might ride to win every time.
* * * *
Jem Mace is showing in England with Austra-
lia’s own Abe Hicken. Jem is pretty well old enough to
be “ old ” Abe’s grandfather. Hicken ought to be able to
get square with old Jem now. It was Mace who brought
Foley out to beat Hicken, and it was during the training
for that fight that Foley developed his great cleverness
with hands, head, and feet. Foley beat Hicken with a
t errific punch fair on the “ mark.”
* * * *
Mr. Rodd, an amateur pupil of George Seale’s,
and Jack Valentine had an interesting go with the gloves
at Larry Foley’s the other day, young Rodd showing to
best advantage at the finish. The amateur is a splendidly-
built young fellow and the makings of a boxer out of the
common.
* * * *
Followers of the ring in Sydney who remember
the “ slomaeby ” Sam Fitzpatrick showing at Foley’s in
the old days for half-a-sov. or so cannot imagine him
resplendent in frock-coat and bristling with brilliants as
described in the Yankee papers. Sam could never fi’o-ht
hard enough to keep himself warm, but it is as a handler of
men he shines. His training of Peter Jackson first put
him on his legs.
* * * *
Only 10,000 rolled up to witness the Collingwood
and South Melb. play-off for the Vic. football premiership.

A six-tooth cross-bred Lincoln reared as a
pet and killed at Purnim, Vic., the other day,
scaled 3021 b. gross: Body, 2201 b. ; pelt, 311 b. ;
offal, 331 b. ; fat, 181 b.
Scientist S. E. Peal predicted in Nature
on 6th Aug. that Herr Lilienthal, the flying-
machine man, though on the right track, would
have his machine turned over, and himself killed,
the first time he endeavored to turn round to meet
a strong wind. On Aug. 11, five days later, Lilien-
thal tried this feat and was at once corpsed.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION^
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ;6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery ; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
In Argentina a shovelful of sulphur and
another of quicklime placed in a herd’s drink-
ing-trough haVe been found a sure remedy for tick,
which, after the cattle have drunk nothing else for
several days, fall off poisoned.
Dear Bulletin, —“ Pirates ” is too manly
a designation for the Harms worths, who have been
noted as the literary and journalistic plagiarists of
London since their very first appearance as pub-
lishers. Their filching is more of the sneak-thief
variety, who stealthily “prigs a wipe ” and picks
out the initials. The Bulletin’s “ Kaiwarra
Mystery” appeared in the Evening News as
“our short story,” with Evison’s name omitted,
and one of Souter’s sketches, taken from The
Bulletin of June 27, is utilised to illustrate a
worked-up discussion m the Daily Mail. —
Tourist, London, Aug., ’96.

The meagre attendance was due not so much
interest in the game as to the tact that the pr ces were
doubled, around admission being raised to a i shilling, xne
advance was poor policy on the part of the co p g
clubs. A potent factor of the Collingwood ss
Saturday was the temperature, their light-built smaller
men skipping heat oUhe afternoon like cats.
A drag containing about 20 dreadful young
men, adorned with flags, ribbons, and a flaring
ment that Collingwood had won the football premiersh p
for 1896, was driven around Melbourne last Sunday, ine
20 unlovely occupants of the vehicle were understood to
be the heroes of the hour, swollen with glory and beer.
* * *
Saturday’s convincing match for the Vic. foot-
ball premiership was responsible for the season s large
crowd and very even play. Collingwood s i goals to 2 in
the first quarter mainly won them the premiership, :
South had the best of the game from then to the hnisn.
The final result, Collingwood 6er. 9b. to S. Melb. og. lob.,
is a fair criterion of the evenness of the match.
Linton, the great English crack, is understood
to have succumbed, in great measure, to high-gear. e
rode in the Bordeaux-to-Paris race with a gear of over
90in. Authorities say such high gears strain the heart and
make heavy calls upon both the nervous and muscular
system. Linton, who among other feats put up 31 muss
in an hour, was a wonderful athlete, and his demise will not
raise the hygienic reputation of the wheel. Martin, who
rides a 96-gear, has said to many friends : “ Some day 1
shut right up.” Another rider whom high-gear will
crack some day is Pither, the ex-Maorilander.
* * * *
The action for damages, Hunter and Wynne v.
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League v. Sydney City Council,
arising from H. and W.’s tandem skidding on a watered
street and tipping* its riders under a four-horse bus, will
prove of great interest to the league and cyclists gener-
ally. The case will, of course, turn on the question
whether the City people over-watered the street, and
whether such over-watering caused the trouble; for
cyclists can’t logically deny the Council’s right to lay the
dust in the interests of the majority, even at the risk of
breaking wheelmen’s necks.

after taking four bottles the sAvell-

been troubled with it since.”

Gov. Brassey expresses the opinion pretty
freely in private that the Victorians “expect too
much for <£7ooo a year.” Probably they do —but
then Gov. Brassey is “ too much.”
The champion suicide of the month is a
Maoriland man who chopped his hand off with an
axe, and then sat round till he bled to death.
The body of a mining expert (named Robins) was found
in the Yarra the other day. There was no evidence as to
how he got there, but The Bulletin can guess at it.
Perhaps the time is coming when an assem-
bly of hungry shepherds will pass a resolution in
favor of a thumping big land and absentee-
tax. At a recent meeting in Melbourne, Rev. ;
P. Robertson bemoaned the fact that more than !
half the soil of Bananaland had fallen into the
hands of absentee landlords and financial institu-
tions, neither of whom do anything to aid Presby-
terian churches in the neighborhood of their pro-
perties. We may live to see a graduated land-tax
among the articles of Kerristian faith in Aus-
tralia.

Dear Bulletin, —A casual specimen of
W.A. railway-management. I had occasion a few
days ago to wait at Coolgardie for the arrival of
the express—with my wife aboard. It was due at
3 p.m., but did not come until 10 next morning.
When about 15 hours behind time, the Coolgardie
officials woke up to the fact that the express was
late—somebody must have told them. The guard
of an up-train then signified his intention of wait-
ing no longer. The official in charge declined to
interfere, and left the responsibility entirely to the
guard, who thereupon started his train “on his
own.” As there is only a single line of rails which
the express must be on somewhere, I committed
my wife to the mercy of Providence and hoped for
the best. As it happened, the two trains met
when going slowly, and the up-train had to push
back to Coolgardie. No one was killed, and the
officials seemed somewhat soured over the result.
They are a happy-go-lucky crowd, and even the
fact that two trains were rushing at each other on
a single line of rails, and no one seemed really to
know where the express was or where the smash
might come off, didn’t disturb them much, appar-
ently.—C.L.

ROBINS, REQUIESGAT.
Beside the Yarra stream he strolled,
■Reflecting over “ fine reports ”
Which testified to grains of gold
In foolish little streaks of quartz ;
He thought, perchance, of assays made
From samples rich in mining guile,
Of cronk promoters who had preyed
Upon the public for a while;
Perhaps he felt a deep remorse
r For having aided with his pen
To smooth the dark, unholy course
i Of many, many wild-cat men.
Tiie river flowing gently by
Could wash away all sense of sin ;
He wiped the tear-drop from his eye,
And boldly took a header in.
There passed before his expert eyes
The prospect of a Golden Shore,
Where Truth is told instead of lies
Triumphantly for evermore;
He held his arms above his head,
Content to clutch at church belief,
And long before they found him dead
His soul had struck Salvation Reef.

He had a bad name, but he had earned it,
nor, after striving after it bo hard and so long, did
he seem anxious to lose it. It was now his sole
possession save a father and some debts, if such
can be styled possessions. His father had summed
him up to the dispenser of embrocation and snake-
essence as a“d d bad egg.” “Yes,” said
boniface, ruefully, “he owes me twenty-five bob
for some time. ” ‘ ‘ Sorry for you,” said the parental
possession, “ for he’ll never pay.” That evening
the Wreck was earnestly expostulating with Mr.
Bung ; he begged hard for a drink, cried, promised
eternal reformation, but no —boniface was firm,
and finally, putting his foot down hard, said :
“No, not if yer rots for it —not another drop.
Yer’ve ’ad me for twenty-five bobs’ worth already,
and yer own father says it’s as good as gone.” A
dazed and puzzled look came into the Wreck’s
face. Sorrowfully said he —“What! my father
said that?” “Yes.” “My father said that —
well, maybe I’m not much chop, but damme if I’ll
make the old man a liar.’’

AGENTS WANTED IN ALL COUNTRY DISTRICTS. j &T LIBERAL COMMISSION.
A municipal council election at Fitzroy
(Melb), the other day, was fought to a desperate
finish by a dentist and an undertaker. Both can-
didates were ardent supporters of an improved
drainage policy, but the undertaker’s interests
being clearly opposed to a decrease in the death-
rate, Fitzroy decided in favor of the dentist by
one vote. He, at any rate, was bound to want his
constituents to outlive their last tooth.

THE INFLUENZA SCOURGE.
NEWS AND HINTS.
HOW TO FACE THE EPIDEMIC.
The Epidemic of Influenza is not yet at an end ;
and the after effects of this terrible disease are
still felt by numerous sufferers. Indeed, severe as
Influenza is in its attack, the after-symptoms are
perhaps even more insiduously dangerous, and it
behoves all who have suffered from the dreaded
epidemic to safeguard themselves against its later
consequences.
The Scarborough Mercury describes .—. event
of local interest, which bears out this opinion. A
reporter of the great newspaper named interviewed
a Mr. Thomas Harland, of 10 Arncliffe Terrace,
Grlaisdale, Yorkshire, who had been rendered in-
capable of work by Influenza during a previous
epidemic. Mr. Harland said, in reply to questions,
“My illness began a year ago last Christmas with
a severe attack of influenza. Afterwards I began
rapidly to get weaker. I could not sleep at nights,
my appetite failed, and although I did not take
my bed yet I could scarcely support my own weight.
I tried all sorts of ‘ doctoring. ’ I was a month in
the Scarborough Sea Bathing Infirmary ; then I
went to Harrogate, and the doctor told me that I
was suffering from anaemia, or bloodlessness in
consequence of the influenza, which affects the
nerves, and causes great general weakness. I re-
turned home still very weak, and utterly unable
to be of any use. Just at this time, however, my
son saw an account in one of the papers of a
woman who had suffered from influenza like my-
self, and who told a reporter what marvellous
effect Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People
had upon her. I determined to try them. I could
not tell that I had received any relief from the
first box, although I was told I was looking better;
but before I had taken a second box I found my-
self rapidly improving. I was able to sleep better,
my nerves were stronger, and my appetite much
better. Now, after my fifth box, lam quite
strong again, and I strongly recommend them to
all who suffer.”
In all cases of Influenza and after-effects, Dr.
William’s Pink Pills have a miraculous efficacy.
They are recognised as one of the greatest modern
medicines, a perfect blood builder and nerve re-
storer, and an unfailing cure for rheumatism,
sciatica, neuralgia, partial paralysis, locomotor
ataxy, St. Vitus’ dance, nervous headache, pros-
tration, &c. They restore pale and sallow com-
plexions to the glow of health, and are a specific
for. all the troubles peculiar to the female sex,
while in the case of men they effect a radical cure
in all cases arising from mental worry, overwork,
or excess.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are obtainable from all
leading chemists, or from the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Coy., Queen’s Place, Sydney, who will for-
ward (post paid), on receipt of stamps or post order,
1 box for 2s. 9d. or \ dozen for 14s. 9d.*

“ The Billy ” writes :
Dear Hi'UjKtin, —Re your notice of Kingston’s proposed
amendments in S.A. Legal Practitioners Act. Doesn tit
strike you as inconsistent that a lawyer should he made
responsible for negligence in his business, and yet be
allowed to practise after serving only two-years articles ?
Such a law would make a man responsible for ignorance
of what he had not had time to learn, lie w uld not be
allowed to plead want of knowledge in answ , to such a
charge. In fact, the rule would probably increase ex-
pense, for he- wotild have to go for advice to an older
solicitor. Another point: supposing (as is and no doubt
will continue to be the case) that a man is not allowed to
begin practice till he is 21 years old, it would be useless
for him to enter articles till he is 19. What is he to do
between 16 and 19? Loaf? Certainly, experience in a
merchant’s office would be useful if he can get a billet.
But can he? And if he passes out before he is 21, what
will he do? Again, loaf? Apropos, in the Tas. new L.P.
Bill it is proposed not to allow a solicitor to take articled
clerks until he has passed three years. This comes rough
on those who can buy into big firms ; but they are too
few- to be considered, and the wisdom of the proposed
rule is obvious. Your objection, no doubt, would be that
it puts a premium ori the power to pay big article-money.
Why not settle that by law- ? Anyway, how- can a man
hope to succeed if he’s articled to a firm without practice ?
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputati >n for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Dear Bulletin, —One of the best bush-
fights I. ever heard of was at the once glorious min-
ing town of Brewinga. Billy Bloomer, in Richard-
son’s pub., called for a bucket of beer, and planked
down a sovereign on the counter. Bill had an
irresistible manner, so the landlord pocketed the
sovereign and handed a bucketful of beer over the
counter. Bill stood over it with his legs : apart,
pulled his dusty hat off and dashed it to the floor,
intimating that the first man who touched it
would, in the very near future, be called to account
for all the sins of his life. “No fool game ter
touch the ’at ? Then yer all curs ! so take that,
and that, and that! ” And Bill dipped the beer
out and slushed it into the faces of the crowd
with terrible earnestness and speed. Of course
they mobbed and downed him before it became
necessary to call for tenders for the building of a
second ark. Then with him on his back on the
floor and a man posted on each of his arms
and legs, they fairly deluged his face and
body with beer. “Look ’ere,” spluttered Bill,
it ain’t'fair t’ ’ave twenty onter one. But if yer
wants a mill, I’ll take any man in Brewinga.”
“ Oo’s the cove wants ter fight ? ” cried somebody
from the bench outside the pub. “ I’ll ’ave ado
at ’ini. I ain’t ’ad no decent exercise fer a week,
so trot ’im out! ” So they repaired to the nearest
paddock. It was very dark on this particular
night; and every time a fight was held in the
dark it was a recognised Brewinga rule that
the recipient of a blow should say “ Home !’,
and then state whether it was a heavy,
medium, or light blow. This was con-
sidered the best means of posting the crowd
up in the progress of affairs. This time the men
hadn’t started two minutes when Bill cried,
“ ’Ome ! Jaw ! Light ! ” Soon after the other
pugilist said, “ ’Ome ! Nob ! Ton ’o bricks ! ”
Then Bill called the next twice, “’Ome ! Blinker !
Goodun ! ” and “ ’Ome ! Ribs ! Knocked me onter
my back!” Then the other, “’Ome! Pinny!
Waster!” And Bill, “ ’Ome! Other blinker!
Beauty ! By gosh ! I reckon I’ve felt that paw
afore ternight. Give’s a match.” Somebody lit a
match, which Bill took and held near the face of
his opponent, and suddenly exclaimed, “ S’elp
ine. Why it’s Brummy Nolan ! I thought I’d
felt them pokes afore. Yer ’and, Brummy. Let’s
’ave a drink afore we finishes the business. Come
on, you fools, let’s all ’ave a drink. And we did.
—Yours, AiE.H.

Hour, 4 a.m. Scene, a northern metro-
polis. Actors : Jones, returning from a jollifica-
tion with good load of Hennessy aboard, and
cabby, who has demanded more than his fare.
Jones, to cabby : “ Eivsh shillingsh (hie) shtoo
mush farsh : one shillingsh (hie) plenty—reporsh
you traffick (hie) managsh ! Whatsh numbersh?”
Endeavors to write in pocket-book but collapses
with his head in a drain. Next morning, by the
sober light of day, reads as follows : ‘ ‘ Reportsh
3ads, traffiesh - managistsh. Nos. of cads
L 6333616311,60. Nos. of traffiesh - managish,
L 463161327. Nos. of drains, 1736543217.”

In the Queensland sugar-growing centres
one rarely or never meets a maimed Kanaka,
though they are employed in the sugar-fields, in
the mills, in fact, everywhere, the present writer,
m the course of about 15 years, has only met one
Kanaka with a wooden leg. Kanakas minus a
leg or an arm are of little if any commercial value
to the planters, so they obligingly die as the result
of any serious hurt.

Some of Fate’s Puppets .
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Looking toward his home, Moore caught a
glimpse of light through the trees. It came from
his wife’s bedroom, most likely ? He wondered
vaguely what would be her greeting. She had
expected him to return in five weeks; it was
exactly five hours since she had waved good-bye to
him from the wharf. He pondered without in-
terest the question what she would have thought,
and said, and done, had the steamer been moving
just a little faster and sent them all to eternity.
He had expected to find the house in darkness.
Yet where was the need to alter his pace? What
did he gain by getting home a minute sooner—say,
before his wife put out tier light ? He would have
to depict the scene of the collision for her; and
he was in the wrong mood for that. She would
be full of wifely interest, womanly sympathy, about
his share. Could a woman never escape from her
sex ? Exalted to a position of mock heroism by
his wife ! In contemplation of the part marked
for him he felt that he could weep—tears of petu-
The thought recurred —had it all ended other-
wise, and his wife been a widow ? A loss or gain
to her? Neither, in any great degree, he could
answer. She would marry again; he could look
calmly at that, with even a smile at his inability
to give distinct features to the figure his fancy
conjured up as his successor. There was the boy
—a different matter; but as he had his father’s
blood in him, he could almost be left uncon-
sidered ; he would shape his own course.
His wife was eight years younger—barely 30 ;
bright, clever, good-looking. One who met life
gaily, who, with a nature as deep as anyone’s, yet
immersed herself in superficialities. An anomaly ;
the centre of it hidden from him. Their natures
were widely divergent: they had daily inter-
course, yet trod separate paths.
Was that to be regretted ? No ; or at least in a
very limited number of aspects. Their married
life fell midway between happiness and discontent,
touching neither. Life to him was mostly on that
plane.
Perhaps the one thing he regretted—if anything
in life was serious enough to demand regret —was
the fact of his marrying at all. That had been a
mistake ; one among many ; all the natural issue
of the mistake of having been born. A world of
error ! How could it be otherwise, when the right
thing lay undecipherable amid a multitude of
wrong ?
Looking up at the house, he noted that it was
the drawing-room that was lighted—a thing that
called for surprise; but he was reluctant to confess
to any. The drawing-room was seldom used at
night, the dining-room downstairs being occupied
as a sitting-room. Only when society visitors
came in the evening—an unusual event —was it
necessary to go upstairs.
He went to the back of the house and found it
dark. The servants must have gone to bed, but
that was unlikely in the fact of visitors being pre-
sent. They had been given a night off, and were
not returned? But that seemed unlikely, since
the house was shut. He tried one door—to find
it locked. Was it that his wife was alone, and
feeling timid had locked up the house before the
servants came in ? Then there vvere no visitors,
and she was sitting by herself in the drawing-
room —a change from her ordinary habit, induced
by her loneliness. She had probably been at the
piano. On this theory the seeming contradictions
were congruous and intelligible.
He was about to ascend the back stairs when he
remembered that the key of one door had been
lost a few days before. He turned the handle and
entered; darkness enveloped him. He walked
carefully, so as not to startle his wife by any
noise. He was fortunate enough to find a clear
passage, and reaching the foot of the stairs safely
he began to ascend, keeping a hand on the
banister.
The silence of the house seemed an ominous
thing; Moore caught himself stopping to listen to
it. At the turning he halted; his heart was
beating strangely fast, his blood alert, as if expect-
ant of something. He was visited by an out-
rageous thought. “ Was he in the wrong house ? ”
He was about to consider the question, to sum up
remembrance, when he heard a voice—pitched,
sustained, with a musical clearness in the sound —
a man’s voice. He was struck aghast —the thing
was so contrary to anything the circumstances
made possible. He was awake, and in his own
home. His mind ran back, but he found no rest
for his thought ; no haziness in his remembrance.
A gleam of light shot across the events of the day,
revealing them to him in one immediate glance —
distinct and in order, carrying him to his presence
there.
He had ascended to the top of the stairs, and
his hand now encountered an overcoat. The
touch of it sent a shock through him that reduced
him to material facts of life. He took up the hat
that was on the stair-post and, handling it, wa g
vaguely glad that he could not name the owner.
Neither did the voice help him to a guess, though
he heard it more distinctly now—the voice of a
man reading aloud.
Why was his first conscious instinct a desire to
escape—to descend the stairs and go out into the
street till he could consider what to do ? Surely
small consideration was necessary ; his imperative
duty lay before him, not behind. Was it that he
felt some charity towards his wife? Was it for
her sake that he did not wish to come upon them ?
Or was it wholly a fear to take up his own part in
the discovery ; a fear that had masked itself in
subtle disguise?
He had leaned back against the wall, but now
stood up : he was in no need of support. But he
could not calm his heart; his blood tingled through
his veins, giving him an odd sense of a new value
in life—a value that deficient human senses could
not quite lay hold of. Existence seemed to pro-
mise something. A clear happiness when his pre-
sent task was accomplished ? He asked himself
the question.
Looking up the corridor, he saw the ray of light
that came through the drawing-room door. What
was the scene that the door hid from him ? And
what was the basis of the sudden, bitter prayer
that it should close fast now, and, opening to no
one’s effort, bury those two within walls ? It was
the awakening of the husband in him. He knew
it, and felt it in a weakening of his blood. He
was no longer his old self, but one among a hun-
dred ; acting a part that had so often been acted
before —the meanest part that could be given to a
high-minded man. In angry denial of such con-
ception of himself —in attempt to shut it out, he
went up the passage.
The length seemed interminable, the noise of his
walking was deafening, and the walls seemed to
close in upon him as he passed. Even at the
threshold of the door he had a prompting that it
was not too late to turn back ; one of the hated
promptings that were not any part of himself.
He compressed his lips and entered ; a flood of
warm light seemed to close around him. The
fancy gave him strength; he became calmer; it was
as if he had been spiritually welcomed.
In one glimpse he saw the pair ; the noise of his
footsteps had alarmed them ; they stood separate,
the first instinctive movement of each had been
towards an individual defence. The identity of
the man gave him no surprise ; he was not able to
be surprised, more intense feelings finding place
with him.
It was Leston, the accountant in the branch in-
surance-office which Moore managed. He remem-
bered, even at this turbulent moment, that he had
once remarked to his wife that Leston was dis-
tinctly a good-looking man ; a judgment that she
had not wholly acceded to. Was this idle word
the germinal cause of his present dishonor? The
thing was almost worth enquiry. He could not
recall that there had been much intimacy between
his wife and Leston. But then the husband is ever
the last to see what is happening at his side.
She was dressed in a pale-blue tea-gown, a rib-
bon at her throat. He could think that this even-
ing she must have looked remarkably well, but
now the deadly pallor and set cast of her features
showed only a mind in t. ~vail. She was standing
behind a chair, her hands clasping the back of it.
She was small in stature, but carried herself
well; her eyes gave a depth, her lips wero finely
cut, her face in every pose showed the strength of
fearlessness. Young, with good features and an
abundance of dark brown hair, she was a woman
who could always look stylish.
“ You ! ” she said quiveringly, almost as he en-
tered. It seemed as if the word broke from her,
before she had made an effort to speak.
He crossed over to the mantelpiece, and, resting
one arm there, stood facing her. He could see
that Leston was shifting his gaze from one to the
other, unknowing what to do, or what was to be
done. The feverish excitement of his wife stirred
an echo in him ; he endeavored to steady his voice.
“ I have disturbed you,” he said.
“It seems like it.” The laughter rang out;
recklessness, nervousness, echoing in the notes of
it.
She knew now that he had long suspected her ;
that his departure had been but a trick. He had
come back to discover the thing he looked for.
Let him make what he could of it ! She could
laugh again at the part that was his. In her
feminine mind, the pitiful meanness of his manner
of unmasking her—not to include the full success
of it—made him appear most contemptible. For
the moment, her sin was, in some retrospective
way, justified by it. As concerning herself, how
could she be less than glad that her life was re-
duced to fact; that she had no longer to deceive ?
And how much easier it was to acknowledge guilt
than it would have been to deny !
Moore kept his eyes off Leston, his intention
being to ignore him to the utmost. It would be a
sacrifice of dignity to take open notice of him;
and his wife would feel a keener shame in his
looking to her as the only guilty one.
He addressed himself to her with gay pretence.
“ A friend of yours ? ”
Could any man but he be so infamous as to
amuse himself with her shame ? Had he not mind
enough to know that the shame was partly his ?

She would force him to feel it. “ You can call
him what you like.”
“ A lover, then ? ’
She had courted the word, yet her woman’s
nature felt a stab. “ Lover! ” It seemed to cry
itself in the street, to be caught on the wind, and
carried echoing in the ears of the outer world.
But she had compelled him to explicitly attack
her; she had the victory she had attempted.
“ You have defined him—and yourself,” she said.
“ And also you.”
Strange that she had not foreseen to what she
would lay herself open. Was it that her mind
was not capable of calm thought? Must she
at ipowledge that she was not able to cope with
him ? She saw her woman’s heart as the founda-
tion of defeat. Was his strength more than of
manhood—an individual element in him ? She felt
suddenly that he could do as he willed with her ;
that lie could make her kneel to him; make her
pitifully repentant.
In protest of finding herself at that depth, she
looked at the man for whom she had sacrificed
herself. But there was no help for her there ; he
was younger, more hesitating than herself. In a
flash she realised that she was more to blame than
he ; that she had not less misshaped his life, than
her own. Shiveringly she felt her isolation ; a
guilty woman at the mercy of the world.
Moore watched her unflinchingly. He was sur-
prised at the ease with which he had taken com-
mand of the situation ; how calmly he held it. A
few moments before he had not known what to do,
but now he could think calmly, see clearly. Vic-
tory was his, even a kind of victory over Fate,
who had prepared a situation in which he was to
have been made to play a ridiculously pitiful part.
He felt no pang in knowing he was a dishonored
husband, and endeavored to persuade himself that
there was no reason why he should feel one. But
he was wise enough to know that his strength lay
in contempt of the pair before him, in making
them realise themselves contemptible.
“What is the book?” he said, gazing at a
volume that lay open on the floor where it had
been dropped.
“ Does that interest you ? ” she said angrily.
“ Naturally. It has interested you.”
“ Oh, enviable woman ! ” She laughed scorn-
fully—at herself and him.
“ You seem to regret something.”
“My marriage?” she suggested.
“ My God, woman, not half as much as I do —as
1 have done from the first.”
He moved his arm and accidentally upset a vase ;
it fell on the floor, breaking into fragments. The
noise went echoing through the house. To the
woman’s excited fancy it was a signal of something
that was to happen ; she looked to the door ex-
pecting to see a terrifying shape take entrance.
She listened, and afar off she thought she heard
the faint tolling of a bell. Her heart stopped,
then bea f to the tolling. She felt chilled to the
blood.
Leston coughed to clear his voice, but found
nothing to say. He was young, and felt the situ-
ation out of his reach. He could see in every
movement that they had advantages over him,
and these he could ascribe to the confidence en-
gendered by their years.
He had, to himself, imagined discovery often
enough, but not in this shape. He had not con-
ceived a husband calmly accepting the situation.
But, after all, since he could not take up a heroic
attitude, he could congratulate himself on being
almost wholly ignored. He would most probably
escape scathless—a sufficient achievement, had it
been by any effort of his own. Yet he was dully
aware that his position had yet to be defined, that
he must take the part that was put upon him.
Moore considered the import of the silence, then
permitted himself to add, at a measured pace—
“We both made a mistake. And the issue—we
have here. Well, even of that I can bear my
part. But your part—l honestly pity.”
She seemed heedless of all around ; her eyes on
the doorway expectantly. “I can do without
it,” she said pettishly.
“ I am aware of that, and I can easily under-
stand it. You get rid of me, and you take in my
place—this.” He turned to Leston, and the man
shrank back ashamed. “A pretty boy, young
enough to be educated to appreciate you. ”
“ Oh, stop ! stop ! ” she cried.
A child, barefooted and clad in a flannel night-
gown, stood hesitant at the door. He was about
seven years old, and in features a likeness of his
father. The mother saw him, and called him to
her with a look.
Moore followed her gaze, and knew then that it
was not the sting of his words that had moved her,
but only the fear of the child understanding.
“Frank !” he said sharply to the boy, “come
here ! ”
Disobedience of that voice was impossible; he
came like a dog.
“ Close the door—and take that chair.”
The boy did his bidding, and then sat looking
at his mother; his childish astonishment addressed
to her.
The woman stood up with locked hands—
supplicating, speechless.
“ You are not forbidden to look upon him,” said
Moore, coldly, in answer.
“Send him away,” she whispered, quiveringly.
“No. His proper place is here.”
She flung herself on the chair, in a passion of
weeping, her soul given up to despair.
“Father, —— ” began the boy.
“Sit there, sir ! Don’t move till I tell you ! ”he
was answered.
Leston crossed over and stood beside the woman;
an opening was offered him. “For the child’s
sake, if not for hers, send him away.”
Moore laughed in contemptuous derision.
“ Surely you are imposing on your position ! ”
“ I do not know about that, but you are cruelly
imposing upon yours.”
“ If you dare to question me, by the living God
I’ll throw you over that balcony.” His violent
utterance rang true, and the echo of it, adding
further excitement to his blood, made him danger-
ously fit for what he threatened to do.
“Don’t speak to him,” said Mrs. Moore,
alarmedly. “You do not know how passionate
And you can do no good. ”
Leston, warm in offence, hated to now acknow-
ledge to a physical fear. But for her sake he must
submit; and because he was so checked and ham-
Eered he began to look forward to escape. Yet
onor compelled him to stay with the woman ;
compelled him—did it not?—to making her his
wife if need were.
“ How long is this going to continue ? ” he said
at length. “ What are you going to do ? ”
“ There is nothing more for me to do ; the rest
falls on you to do.” The man’s whole thought ran
to dramatic utterance. Yet he had an educated
disdain of the stage.
‘ * What am I to do ? ”
“Leave this house at once—taking her with
you. ” Moore was only outwardly calm ; his heart
beat in frightened prospect of the departure of his
wife from out of his life. He was so used to her :
what would it mean ?
His strength had departed, and he knew that
he was physically ill. He feared that he could
not maintain his part to the end. He wished they
would hurry—a mad desire to escape from the room
had seized him.
“Come, Lucy,” said Leston, bending over her.
He wondered that he should so quietly accept the
burden of her, though he knew there was nothing
else that he could do.
Her sobs had ceased ; she sat with her face
hidden, giving no sign that she heard.
“ Lucy ! ” It was an appeal to her to look up,
so that he may read the reason of her unwilling-
ness.
She jumped up quickly, and, throwing herself
out of his embracing arms, staggered against a
small desk that stood by the wall, and halted with
her back to it.
“No, I will not go,” she cried. “Not though
he orders it. I stay here —my place is here. I
could not go with you. How could you think of
it ?It would mean ” She turned quickly to
her husband. “Can I stay,? Only till to-morrow.
See, I ask it; on my knees, if I must.”
“You can do as you will,” he said, simply. “ I
will offer no objection now.”
She turned to Leston, speaking from a centre
that was hidden from him. “ You can go.”
“ But you!”
“Go ! ” she commanded.
He made his pitiful departure. Moore felt an
infinite relief ; he sat down wearily in a chair, his
face turned from her.
She saw the depth of his despair, and her heart
moved madly—responsive to her thoughts. Her
fault rose up behind her, threatening to advance
and overwhelm her —a purpose beckoned her to
escape.
V Will you put Frank to bed ? ” She spoke
quietly ; a new strength had been given her.
He looked up in surprise. The fact of hav-
ing to be. told by her what he must do was a
shock to him. He saw how far beneath command
of himself he had sunk, but he made no effort to
recover.
“Yes,” he said.
The child followed him, nervous, crying piti-
fully, and was the more abandoned in his tears
that he knew not very definitely why he should
cry.
In quieting him, Moore promised that his mother
would not leave him ; that she would not be per-
mitted. He attempted to persuade himself that
though he had lost a wife, he had no right to
deprive the child of a mother. He felt happier in
such thought, though he knew he was but juggling
with his mind.
The soft promises he had to make to the boy
helped to soothe himself as well. But he saw no
course of action, nor what in honor he could offer
her.
He went back to the drawing-room, possessed
of a full indifference. He himself was under no
necessities. Fate could do with him as it would.
Or his wife could decide what was to be ; he would
stand aside, offering no opposition to either.
“You have come back,” she said. “ I hoped
you would.” He acquiesced dully and stood by
the doorway. He had a horror of taking up his
old position by the mantelpiece. The woman read
a hatred of coming near to her.
He had leisure to notice her; to see that her
face paled and flushed, that her eyes were lumin-
ously bright. She stood as when he had left her,
yet her attitude seemed constrained.
He had to break through a silence. “ What is it
you want with me? ”
“ Nothing. U nless
“ Well ? ”
“To hear your reproaches.’
“Lucy ! ” He reproached her levity.
She laughed defiantly, but her laughter rang
hollow. “Tell me how much you despise me,” she
said. “ How much you hate me ! Do ! ”
“ How much I pity you ; how much I pity us
both ! ”
“ Oh, Harry. Not that,” shesaid with a shiver.
“Do not conquer me ! Be harsh ! Treat me as I
deserve ! Why don’t you hate me ? ”
“ Say because Frank doesn’t. He will not let
his mother leave him ; I had to promise that she
would not. ”
She looked long in silence ; as if she could not
credit him. Her breath came in short gasps. Not
till he again glanced up at her did she find utter-
ance. “ The poor child—the darling! And J—
my pride in him should have saved me. I wonder
why it did not. I have been mad this last month
—not myself. Oh, the horror of it now ! ”
“ Will you stay ?”
“You had to promise him, you say. That was
cruelly hard on you.”
“ I have not said that. Will you stay ? ”
He was aware that he was offering her all
that was in his heart; and more than was
in his mind. He knew not where he stood.
His head swam; the walls of the room seemed
to recede, then halt and come slowly back.
He looked up—to see his wife fall forward on her
face.
He ran to her, and knelt beside her, his mind
in the travail of unshaped fears. He lifted her in
his arms and turned her face towards him.
“ Lucy, what is it? You are ill! You have
hurt yourself ! ” A convulsion seized her, and left
her with her features set in distortion.
“You would take me back? ” she whispered.
“For his sake.”
“Ah, yes,” she sighed. She held a pause, then
asked—“ Not for my own ? ”
His face hardened; she asked more than he
could give her.
“Let me go,” she cried. “Do not hold me!
How can you when you hate me so ? ” Her violence
carried her out of his arms, and she stopped at a
distance from him.
“I do not hate you, Lucy. You know I don’t.
But I cannot pretend that I have forgiven you.”
“I did not ask for that; I only meant—but it
doesn’t matter. It is best that it should end now.”
“ End, Lucy ? ”
“ Can’t you see ? ” she cried fiercely.
He caught an inspiration from her tone ; an in-
finite horror surged through him. “ Lucy ! have
you poisoned yourself ? ” He made a step towards
her.
“ Don’t come near me ! ” she screamed, raising
herself on her hands. “ I will not let you touch
me ! ”
“ Lucy ! ” Horror held him in its embrace; he
was capable only of a weak utterance of her name.
“Listen!’’ she cried excitedly. “Frank is
calling me. You hear him ? Let me go to him ? ”
She looked up for permission, as he stood sense-
lessly watching her.
“Do not follow me,” she said. “Let me go
alone. I must see him before ”
She staggered to her feet, and, reaching the
doorway, steadied herself there and went down
the passage.
The child was crying upon the bed ; her screams
had alarmed him. She hurried, and coming heavily
against the wall staggered back from it—fell, with
outstretched hands—quivered, and lay motionless.
Moore, looking vacantly down at her, wondered
dully why the child was sobbing.
Q.
JOHN REAY WATSON.

“ Theatre Royal, Sydney, April 20. 1896.
“ My Dear Mb. BONNINGTON.
“ Allow me, on behalf of Mrs. Holt, to thank you very sincerely for your
kind thought in sending her, on Saturday, a bottle of your famed Irish Moss. I may say that
it is an old and valued friend of ours, and I am sure our esteem of its virtues will be
strengthened by the acquaintance with it which we now renew, through your good agency.
“ With all sincere wishes for your success and well-being,
“I remain, cordially, BLAND HOLT.”
